Nothing, Hendricks says. That is the point of it, Joos. She is nothing to me, and the man who died for her was nothing to me, and the keeper who stood in front of her is not nothing to me, and I am asking anyway. When did the church last get a chance to do a thing this clean?

Clean, de Vries repeats. There is a body on a tavern table and half the Wall repeating a murderer's name, and the woman the coroner will want to look at is sitting on your timber. Clean.

The coroner will find the truth without her, Hendricks says. That room does not need the witness. It kept better evidence. He nods at the east, where the light is coming up dirty over the marshes. What she needs is to be gone before the tide turns, and what you need is a reason. So: the clerk came to your door and your caution sent him away, and he is dead. The reason is standing in front of you wearing the consequences.

It is a hard thing to say in front of the woman herself. Mara notes that he says it anyway — that he spends the deacon's guilt like coin, precisely, without apology, the way the keeper spent the room's shame at the trial. These northern people run their mercy through bookkeeping. She had thought it cold. She is beginning to see that it is the only mercy that can be audited, and that audited mercy is the only kind the powerful cannot later deny.

De Vries looks at the water for a while.

She cannot be a passenger, he says at last, in Portuguese, to both of them. A woman alone, entered as a passenger, is a question at every customs post from here to the Texel. Questions are lanterns. I will not light one over my ship.

Then do not enter me as a passenger, Mara says.

Both men look at her.

She has spent the silence learning the shape of the problem, because the problem is the oldest one she knows: what a book will hold. She points at the stacked timber in the barge, at the hold gaping amidships, at the manifest world entire.

Write me as cargo, she says. This time it is I who say so.

The deacon's careful face finally does something: a small contraction around the eyes, as if the sentence had a splinter in it, which it does, and he has felt it go in, which she intended.

Household goods, he says slowly. Consigned to Amsterdam. Unexamined, if the fees are attended to. They generally are.

And between here and Amsterdam she works, Hendricks says. She mends. There is always mending. He glances at Mara's hands, which are steady, and have been steady through everything, a fact the whole Pelican would swear to. And she is worth more than mending, Joos, but you will find that out at your own speed, the way the rest of us did.

***

## VI. Pieter's Coat

The transfer is a matter of minutes — the barge alongside, a ladder of battens, timber beginning to sling aboard above her head as the fluyt's crew wakes into its work. It is when she turns to climb that Hendricks stops her, with a hand held up, not touching. He has not once touched her. She has noticed.

From the locker under the sweep he brings out two things.

The first is a coat. Sea-wool, heavy as a rolled sail, the colour weather leaves when it has taken all the others, mended at the cuffs by a hand that mended for love and not for wages. It is far too big for her. It was too big for its owner too, she will think later, when she understands better what it was bought to do — to make a fevered man believe he could be warm.

My brother's, Hendricks says. Pieter. He went east with the first of the wolf-fleets, before your Company or mine had names. He came home with a fever and a stare, and died in a lodging house off the Ratcliff road raving about islands, and I have hauled this coat up and down this river for six years because throwing a dead man's coat in the tide is a thing I have not found the mathematics for. He holds it out flat across both arms, an offering, not a charity. It is not lent, he says. It is given. A given thing cannot be called back. You of all people should own one thing with that property.

Mara takes the coat. The weight of it settles onto her shoulders like a hand that asks permission.

The second thing is a packet. Oilskin, tied with tarred string, soft at the corners from years in a locker. She can feel through the skin of it that it is paper.

His discharge, Hendricks says. His book from the voyage. The parts he wrote before the fever are a sailor's parts — wages, weather, a girl in the Texel roads. The parts he wrote after are the islands. I have had six years to get them read to me and I have not done it, and I will tell you the truth of why, because you are leaving on the tide and the truth costs me nothing tonight: I have the fever parts by heart from his own mouth, and I did not want to learn that the writing was worse.

He puts the packet into her hands and closes her fingers over it with one blunt tap, the only touch he will ever give her.

Learn the words, he says. Dutch first; the wolves keep their books in it. When you can read this, you will know what is waiting for you east of everything — not the spice, not the money; the thing my brother saw and could not carry. Then burn it, or keep it. It is no use to me. It has been no use to me for six years, and it is the heaviest thing on this barge.

Mara holds the packet against her ribs, where later, in another life, she will carry duplicates of everything that matters, and some part of her is already learning the habit from the weight.

Then he does the last thing, the thing she will tell nobody about for twenty-five years, and then tell exactly one person. He takes the squat stone bottle from his coat, uncorks it, and holds it out.

Genever. Dutch physic. She has watched the English refuse it all night with their faces. She drinks. It goes down like a lit wick — juniper, fire, pine resin — and her eyes water, and on the deck above a sailor laughs, not unkindly, at her face.

For the voyage, Hendricks says, taking the bottle back and corking it with his thumb. Courage enough to reach Amsterdam. After that you will need your own. He looks at her from inside the squint, and for one moment the pricing look is gone and something older is in its place. You are sailing to my countrymen, he says. Know them for wolves and you will do good business. Know them only for wolves and you will become one. My brother could not hold both. Hold both.

He steps back to the sweep. Above them the deacon calls something about the tide, and the fluyt begins to gather herself, and the lanes of the Pool fill with the first full light.

Go up, Hendricks says. Cargo stows itself aboard, in my experience, when it is the self-loading kind.

Mara climbs. From the fluyt's rail she watches the barge fall away astern, the broad short figure at the sweep already turned to his own business, taking the river's next sentence without looking back — and she understands that this, too, was given and not lent: he has made the parting weigh nothing so that she does not have to carry it.

The manifest goes to the customs men at Gravesend with the fees attended to. Somewhere in its columns, between deals and battens, an entry rides in a clerk's small Dutch hand: *household goods, one lot, consigned*. Entered by her own instruction, which is a thing no ledger has a column for, and the beginning — though no clerk alive could read it there — of the language of paper.

All down the river to the sea, wrapped in a dead man's coat with a dead man's book against her ribs, Mara sits among the timber and collects Dutch off the wind the way gulls collect the wake. *Hout.* *Touw.* *Water.* *Thuis*, which the youngest sailor says to the horizon, twice, softly, and which she will learn by Amsterdam means home — a word she takes anyway, on account, against the day she has somewhere to spend it.

# The Steward's Search (1603)

> **1603**
>
> *“Legal eats less bread than theft.”*
>
> — Anne Bell, Shadwell, 1603

***

## I. Before the Knock

By the time Harcourt's steward reaches Bell's rooms, there is nothing in them that can hang a widow.

This is not luck. Luck is what men call a woman's work when they arrive after it.

Anne has had four hours.

The first hour belongs to the dead. Matthew comes home on a door lifted flat between two parish men, his cap under Arthur's arm and one side of his coat stiff where the blood has dried. The men set him on the bed he left before supper. They ask whether she has somebody to sit with her. Anne says she has two girls asleep through the wall and a neighbour already awake. This is not an answer to their question. It is the answer they require before they can leave.

Arthur stays.

He tells her Matthew spoke at the Pelican and that Tom heard him. He tells her about the copied page and the woman who carried the original into the Pelican. He tells her Silas Rook has gone. He begins to tell her what the room did right.

Anne stops him.

Did the man get away?

Yes.

Then tell me what the room did, not what it meant.

Arthur starts again.

At the end, Anne asks how many sheets exist.

Three in all. The original. One copy for the parish bundle. One for her. The original is beneath Maggie's bar unless the foreign woman has moved it since Arthur left.

The parish copy is already out of their hands. Her copy is still inside Arthur's coat.

Keep it there, she says.

It is yours.

It is a rope if it enters this house.

Arthur looks at Matthew on the bed. The ink bruise on his own finger is fresh enough to wash away.

Then what do I do with it?

Take me to the original.

The second hour belongs to the living. Anne wakes the neighbour and carries both girls across the landing wrapped in one blanket. The elder wakes enough to ask why men have put Father's boots by the door. The younger does not wake at all. Anne tells the truth in the smallest amount that will hold until daylight.

Your father is dead. You are safe. I am going out and coming back.

Joan, the elder girl, looks at her and understands that one of those statements is meant to carry the other two.

Anne gives the neighbour the last heel of yesterday's loaf and asks her to soak it in warm milk when the girls wake. She does not ask whether the neighbour can spare the milk. The woman has taken one look at Matthew laid across the bed and already put the pan on.

The third hour belongs to Harcourt.

Anne knows the shape of his search because men owing him money have come to Matthew's table for six years. They arrive believing a clerk's house must contain a clerk's work. They lift the mattress first. Then the loose board beside the hearth. Then the flour crock, because a poor family is supposed to hide its treasure in food. They ask the wife whether her husband brought home papers. If she says no, they ask the children separately.

She removes nothing valuable. There is almost nothing valuable to remove. She separates what is dangerous from what is merely poor.

Matthew's three scraps of copied names go into the fire. They are not proof of any whole account, only enough writing to let Harcourt say part of the stolen page passed through the rooms. A direction to the Dutch church goes after them. Anne watches every letter blacken before she rakes the ash apart and lays fresh coal over it.

The rent receipt, the baker's tally and the pawn ticket for her winter cloak remain in the small chest. They are not secrets. They are armour.

Inside Joan's primer she finds a narrow strip in Matthew's hand: four figures, two initials and a line drawn beneath them. The child must have taken it from his table to mark her place.

Anne carries it across the landing.

Did Father give you this?

The elder shakes her head.

Then it is not a keepsake. A keepsake is something the dead meant you to have.

She puts it in the neighbour's fire. The girl watches the paper curl.

I could have hidden it, she says.

I know.

They would not search me.

They will search whatever they can make smaller than themselves.

The girl looks down at the younger child still sleeping against her shoulder.

Anne crouches so their faces are level.

You do not carry paper for a dead man's courage. You carry your sister until I return. That is enough work for one morning.

The fourth hour takes her to the Pelican, where she sees the table, hears Matthew's name spoken as a name, refuses Arthur's copy and burns it herself. When she walks back into Shadwell, the steward is waiting at her door with two warehouse men.

The knock has not yet fallen.

***

## II. Authority

The steward turns when he hears her on the stair. He is a dry, well-kept man who has stood in these rooms twice before while Matthew fetched a debtor's file. Both times he accepted the cup Anne put before him and left it untouched, as though poverty might pass through pewter.

Mistress Bell. A grievous circumstance.

Yes.

Master Harcourt has sent us to recover private papers removed from his house.

Show me.

He glances at the warehouse men. They are built for lifting, not witnessing, and both suddenly find the stair rail interesting.

Show you what?

His authority.

The steward produces a folded instruction bearing Harcourt's mark. It requires the recovery of books, memoranda, copies, seals and other effects belonging to the merchant house and lately in Matthew Bell's possession. It does not authorize a search. It assumes one.

Anne reads slowly. Matthew taught her enough letters to find a sum and enough words to know when a sum has been hidden inside them. She reaches the end and gives the paper back.

It says you may recover. It does not say I must let you break my boxes.

If you obstruct the return of stolen property—

Then fetch a parish officer and call me a thief where my neighbours can hear. If you mean to search without one, enter and do it where my neighbours can hear.

The door opposite opens another inch.

The steward looks at it. Respectable power prefers an empty stair.

We need not make a spectacle of bereavement.

You brought two men to a widow's door before breakfast.

His mouth tightens.

Anne lifts the latch before he can knock.

You may look. You will say aloud what you take, and one of your men will write a receipt before it leaves. My husband's goods belong to his estate. My goods belong to me. Harcourt's goods may go to Harcourt. If you cannot tell the three apart, leave them where they are.

The rule is larger than the law available to her. She says it as if somebody has already judged in her favour.

The warehouse men wipe their boots.

The steward does not.

***

## III. The Rooms

Matthew lies behind the inner curtain with a clean cloth over his face. The steward pauses when he understands what occupies the bed. Anne watches him decide whether death makes the room private. Harcourt's instruction wins.

They begin with the table.

One horn inkwell, the steward says.

Bought from the stationer in Shadwell. Threepence. The charge is in the household book.

One bundle blank foolscap.

Six sheets. The elder girl practises letters on them. Lift the first and see.

Beneath the blank sheet, Matthew Bell has written the alphabet in a hand too careful for a child. The girl has copied each letter below it, heavy at the downstrokes. The steward puts the paper back.

One penknife.

Mine, Anne says.

It is a clerk's implement.

It pared onions before it pared quills. The notch in the handle is where I dropped it on the grate before I married him.

The steward leaves it.

The warehouse men open the cupboard. Two bowls, two wooden spoons, oatmeal, salt, a heel of hard cheese wrapped in cloth. One man lifts the flour crock. His whole hand reaches the bottom.

Nothing, he says.

Flour, Anne says.

He colours and sets it down.

They turn to the small chest. The steward finds the rent receipt first. He reads it twice, disappointed by its honesty. Then the baker's tally. Then the pawn ticket.

Where are your husband's private notes?

At his place of work, if they belong to his work.

We know he brought papers home.

You know he brought sums home. Men came here because your master preferred them ashamed at a clerk's table instead of angry at a merchant's door.

Did he discuss those accounts with you?

At supper. At night. While the girls slept. Whenever a man had stood where you are standing and Matthew wanted to call the account legal before he could eat beside it.

The steward closes the chest.

He lifts the mattress anyway.

The bed beneath Matthew's body cannot be searched without moving him. Harcourt's man stands at its foot, measuring the offence against the instruction in his hand. Anne says nothing. The dead are doing the only household labour death permits them.

He moves to the girls' pallet.

Anne lets him turn the blanket, lift the ticking and put his fingers through the straw. She lets him because the girls are across the landing eating warm bread and because there is nothing beneath their sleep that belongs to Harcourt.

The younger child appears in the doorway while he is still on his knees.

She carries half the milk-soaked heel in both hands. The neighbour reaches for her too late. The child looks at the mattress opened on the floor, then at the stranger with straw on his sleeves.

Are you looking for Father? she asks.

The steward stands.

For something your father took.

Anne crosses the room before he can ask the next question.

What is your name? she asks the child.

The girl gives it.

Who is your master?

The child looks puzzled.

Nobody, Anne says. What are you carrying?

Bread.

Who gave it to you?

The neighbour.

Does this man have a paper saying the bread is his?

No.

Then finish it.

The child eats, watching the steward over the crust.

He understands the lesson is not meant for her.

***

## IV. What He Takes

The search produces Matthew's old work satchel, empty except for a thread, a flake of sealing wax and the dust that gathers in corners of useful things.

Company property, the steward says.

Matthew bought it before Harcourt hired him.

Can you prove that?

Can you prove he did not?

For the first time, something like anger reaches the steward's face. He has spent a morning finding no stolen page, no secret duplicate and no frightened child willing to improve his knowledge. An empty satchel has become the only thing his visit can recover.

It bears the house mark.

Inside, stamped after he entered service. Take it if an empty bag is what Harcourt requires from my husband's death.

She makes one warehouse man sit at Matthew's table. He is slow with a pen. The receipt takes longer than the search.

One used leather satchel, empty. Claimed as the property of Master Harcourt. Removed from the rooms of Matthew Bell after his death by order of Harcourt's steward.

Put your name, Anne says.

The man looks at the steward.

Put mine, the steward says.

Your hand took nothing. His did.

The warehouse man signs by mark. The second man witnesses. The steward is forced to add his own name below both because a chain of authority that does not end at Harcourt is only three men in a widow's room.

Anne sands the receipt with a pinch from the household jar.

The steward watches her do it.

There was another copy, he says.

There was.

Where is it?

Burned.

He studies her face and believes her. This irritates him more than a lie.

And the original?

Not in my house.

That is not an answer.

It is the answer your paper allows you to collect.

The three men leave with the satchel. Anne closes the door after them. She waits until their steps reach the street, then draws the bolt.

The room has been opened, emptied, searched and left poorer by one useless bag. It still contains two spoons too few, rent through Candlemas, three days' flour, two living girls and one dead man whose account Master Harcourt cannot find.

Anne looks at the receipt.

Legal eats less bread than theft.

Until this morning it was something she said at supper to a husband who wanted the word *legal* to end an argument. Now it is a procedure.

***

## V. The Door After

The girls help put the pallet back together.

Anne makes the elder say what the men searched. Table. Cupboard. Crock. Chest. Bed. Pallet. Hearth. She makes her say what they took. One satchel. She makes her find the three signatures on the receipt, though the child can read only the first letters.

Why remember it if we have the paper? the girl asks.

Because paper can leave.

Why keep the paper if we remember?

Because men who do not believe girls sometimes believe their own names.

There is another knock.

Both children freeze.

Anne opens the door herself.

Maggie stands outside with a covered pot under one arm, a loaf beneath the other and no speech prepared. She looks past Anne at the straw on the floor and the cupboard doors left open.

Harcourt? she asks.

His steward.

Did he find anything?

His own name, by the end.

Maggie gives the pot to the elder girl. Mutton broth, onions, barley. Enough for two days if water is added tomorrow and nobody objects to honesty in soup.

I did not ask for charity, Anne says.

It is not charity.

Then what is it?

Breakfast is past. Call it dinner.

The girls have already lifted the lid. Steam reaches the room before either woman can turn the food into principle.

Anne sets four bowls on the table. There are only two spoons, so the girls take those. Maggie drinks from the bowl when Anne gives her one. Anne does the same. Nobody speaks of forgiveness. There is too much broth and not enough language for it.

When they have eaten, Maggie asks whether Anne wants the original brought away from the tavern.

No. Keep it where merchants do not look.

And if they search the Pelican?

Make them name what they are taking. Make them write their names beneath it. Make the search cost them daylight.

Maggie considers this. I have a mallet.

Use the receipt first.

The elder girl listens from the pallet. Name. Master. Thing. Authority. Receipt. She repeats the five words under her breath, fitting them to her fingers.

Anne hears her.

Again, she says.

The girl says them again.

The younger joins her without understanding. By the third repetition both children know the order.

Outside, carts begin the day's passage toward the river. Harcourt's warehouse opens. Arthur sits down in Bell's chair. Tom walks east with a signed paper in his coat. Mara is already beyond the rooms any English steward knows to search.

In Shadwell, a widow bolts her own door and teaches two girls how to make a lawful man account for himself.

# The Language of Paper (1603–1604)

> **1603–1604**
>
> *“A true copy is a second witness, and the second is harder to kill.”*
>
> — Mara, Bantam, 1604

***

## I. What the House Will Hold

Amsterdam receives Mara as London released her: in rain, among timber, under a description that is true only in the narrow place where truth and survival overlap.

The customs clerk at the quay reads *household goods, one lot, consigned* and looks past her twice. On the third look he finds a woman inside Pieter's coat and asks Joos de Vries whether the household is his.

It is now, Joos says.

The clerk has no box for that answer. He puts a line through the entry, writes another word above it and waves the consignment ashore.

Mara cannot yet read what he has made of her. This is intolerable.

She walks behind Joos through a city built like an argument against water. Canals hold the grey sky in strips. Bridges rise under handcarts and settle again. Houses stand shoulder to shoulder with their upper storeys leaning over the street, each one lifting a beam and hook from its gable so that cargo may enter by the window and never trouble the family door. Everywhere there are ledgers: chalk figures on casks, tally cuts in timber, bills nailed beneath oilskin, boys running folded instructions from men who own ships to men who will die in them.

The Company is not two years old and has already learned to look ancient. It has rented rooms in the old Bushuis on the Kloveniersburgwal, an armoury being taught commerce one locked door at a time. Pepper is weighed under guard. Men enter carrying samples and leave carrying permissions. In one room the directors decide where twelve ships will go. In another, somebody counts spoons.

Joos does not take Mara through the directors' door. He takes her to the rooms where victuals, bedding and human beings are entered for the fleet.

There are three lists on the desk.

Sailors. Soldiers. Merchants.

The clerk taps each heading with the end of his quill while Joos translates into Portuguese. There is no list for a woman. In the margins of earlier pages Mara sees the occasional short entry attached to an officer's name: a wife, a boy, each one an exception answering to a man already entered above.

Which man? Mara asks.

Joos's mouth tightens.

That is the difficulty.

No, she says. That is the price. Which man?

The clerk does not understand the Portuguese, but he understands a woman looking at his page too directly. He lays a blotting sheet over the lists as if she might steal a name with her eyes.

Joos leads her back into the yard.

There is a surgeon's widow sailing with one small son, he says. Her brother is a junior merchant at Bantam. He has petitioned twice, her passage is charged against his account, and I have guaranteed what the account does not cover. The owners agreed before they understood how many arguments agreement would require. At present she is the only woman in the fleet. The clerk may enter one servant beneath her name if I guarantee that servant too. You can sew. You can reckon. You speak Portuguese.

And she owns me until Bantam.

She pays you nothing, Joos says. The passage is the wage.

Joos will not use the word *owns*. Mara notices. It does not change the price.

What will the paper say?

Household servant.

Whose household?

The widow's.

After Bantam?

Joos looks back at the door through which the lists have vanished. After Bantam, the paper ends.

All paper ends, Mara says.

Not all of it ends where the people named in it expect.

That is the first useful thing anyone in Amsterdam tells her.

She agrees on three conditions. Her full name is entered. No advance is paid to the widow or to Joos on her behalf. The packet under Pieter's coat remains her own property, unopened except by her hand.

The clerk refuses the first because a servant does not require two names, refuses the second because no wage means no advance, and laughs at the third because private papers are no concern of his. Then he says the widow's exception has already consumed the Chamber's patience. If Joos wants another argument, all three may watch the fleet leave from shore.

Mara waits.

Joos puts his purse on the desk. Not payment: bond. If Mara deserts her service before Bantam, damages Company property or consumes stores beyond the allowance, the Chamber may recover the cost from him. The clerk names a sum designed to make principle expensive.

Joos agrees.

Only then does the clerk write *Mara de Sousa*, draw a hard dash through the advance column, and add *one packet of private papers* under effects.

Then he turns the book for Joos to witness.

Mara puts one finger on the page.

Read it to me.

Joos reads every word. The clerk's annoyance grows line by line. When he says *dienstmaagd* — maidservant — Mara sees again the word the customs man wrote above *goods* at the quay. Two strokes have carried her from cargo into service. She files the shape of them. Then she listens until the entry in the book and the entry in her head agree.

Only then does she let Joos sign.

***

## II. The Texel Roads

The widow's name is Lysbeth van Aalst. She is thirty, which grief has made look like forty, and has the dry, competent hands of a woman who assisted a surgeon because illness did not stop at the edge of his licence. Her son Willem is seven. He wets his bed when he is frightened and has learned that the death of a father is a promotion, so he tries to command everyone beneath four feet tall.

Lysbeth looks at Mara's coat, her ruined shoes, the packet she keeps against her ribs, and asks in slow Dutch whether she has ever cared for a child.

Mara understands *child*. She understands the question from the boy.

No, she says.

Lysbeth closes her eyes for a moment.

Can you keep him alive?

That depends what tries to kill him.

Joos translates. To Mara's surprise, the widow laughs.

They go north by lighter with the fleet's final stores, through cold water widening toward the island of Texel. The twelve ships wait in the roads beyond the shallows because Amsterdam's wealth is too deep in the hull to pass safely through Amsterdam's water. From a distance their masts resemble a leafless wood. Closer, they become a moving town: boats crossing between hulls, barrels going up, letters coming down, officers shouting about water, powder, biscuit, vinegar, cordage, late men and missing hens.

The ship assigned to Lysbeth is not *De Hoop*. Mara had not expected it to be, but she searches every stern until there are none left to search.

Joos brings her aboard and spends an hour arranging with the under-merchant what the clerk in Amsterdam has already arranged in ink. The under-merchant dislikes additional mouths until he sees the entry. Then he dislikes them officially, which is safer.

At the rail, Joos gives Mara a slate, a chalk stub and a thin sheaf of waste paper stitched through one corner.

For Dutch, he says.

For what else?

Joos considers this and adds a pencil.

He has done enough. Both of them know it. That is what makes the account dangerous.

What do I owe you? she asks.

Nothing.

That is not an answer.

It is the answer I would prefer.

Then it is the answer I refuse.

Willem chases a chicken past them. Somewhere below, a carpenter strikes the same fastening three times and swears at it in three different measures.

Joos rests both hands on the rail.

Learn what is in Pieter's book, he says. If it contains the name of a man who should answer for something, send the name home. If it contains only a sick man's fear, send nothing. That will close it.

That will tell us the cost, Mara says. It will not close the account.

He looks at her, and this time his face permits the shame.

Yes, he says. It will tell us that.

The boat takes him away before the fleet sails. Mara watches until he is a dark mark among other dark marks and then opens the waste-paper book.

On the first page she writes the four Dutch words she owns.

*Hout. Touw. Water. Thuis.*

Below them she copies her own name from memory, the letters as Bell made them on the page in Wapping and the clerk made them in Amsterdam.

She copies it twelve times, until the hand belongs to her.

***

## III. East of Everything

The voyage teaches by subtraction.

First the shore goes. Then fresh bread. Then privacy, clean water, uncracked lips, the expectation that a body carried aboard will be carried off again. The fleet loses sight of itself in weather and finds itself by signal. Sails split. Men fall. Biscuit moves when broken. A sailor who gives Mara the Dutch word for gull dies before they cross the line, sewn in sailcloth, weighted and committed to the water.

Mara learns Dutch because ignorance is the only danger aboard she can reduce by sitting still.

Willem begins with objects. Cup. Spoon. Shoe. Mast. Rat. Lysbeth supplies verbs. Eat. Wash. Hold. Sleep. The sailors contribute the language of weather, work and complaint. The under-merchant adds numbers when he discovers she can total a ration issue faster than his assistant. Portuguese has already given her the letters. Repetition gives them a new language.

The numbers become useful before the words do.

Every morning a bell calls the ship to provisions. The sound travels through plank and bone, reaching Mara before the light does. Men rise from hammocks slung so close that one man's shoulder lifts another man's back. Shoes are found by touch. Belts are fastened beneath blankets if there is room, and without them if there is not. The air below tastes of wet wool, old breath and the bilge moving under everything.

Lysbeth and Willem have the narrow berth built against the after bulkhead. Mara sleeps beneath it on two folded coats and a square of sailcloth, with Pieter's packet under her head. When the ship heels, her hip meets the chest. When it rights, her shoulder meets the wall. Privacy is the moment after a person turns away and before someone else turns back.

At the bell they carry their three cups and a wooden plate into the passage. The households wait behind the officers. The sailors wait behind the households, each mess with its sack. Biscuit comes first, struck against the edge of the cask so that the loose inhabitants fall out before the eating begins. Willem examines every piece.

That one moved, he says.

They all move.

Not that much.

Mara breaks the biscuit along a dark tunnel and holds the two halves toward him.

Meat, she says in Portuguese.

Willem laughs in spite of himself. Lysbeth does not. She has spent the first week insisting that her son eat around the grubs, the second picking them out, and the third watching him chew without asking what remains.

The rationer's call is faster than any lesson. Three mouths. Three biscuit shares. Three measures of small beer while it lasts. Water at morning and sunset, one measure to each name on the passenger entry. The assistant repeats the numbers and marks his slate without looking at the cups.

*Een. Twee. Drie.*

Mara says the words under her breath. One. Two. Three. The sound differs; the quantity does not.

The beer grows sour first. A rope of something pale clings to the dipper and stretches before it breaks. The water follows more slowly. It takes the colour of the cask and a flavour of green wood, then of wood shut too long in heat. Men hold their noses and drink. Willem asks whether rain tastes of barrel in Java. Mara tells him that rain tastes of roof, leaf, dust, whatever it touches before the mouth. He considers this proof that nothing in the world arrives clean.

On the fifth morning of contrary wind, Mara wakes because Willem is pressing two fingers into her shoulder.

Do not tell, he whispers.

There is no light. The berth above her is still, but the smell has already spoken. Willem stands in his shirt with both fists held against his belly. Behind him, the wool blanket is dark from the middle to the edge.

Mara sits up. Her head strikes the underside of the berth.

Your mother knows.

She is asleep.

Lysbeth's voice comes from above them.

His mother is not.

Willem closes his eyes.

There is no room in which to hide the linen, no spare blanket to put beneath him, no hour when the deck belongs only to women and frightened boys. Lysbeth lifts the wet things without accusation. Mara takes the lower corners. Together they carry the bundle through men dressing for the watch. Nobody needs to ask. A sailor makes space without meeting Willem's face. Another does meet it, then looks away because he has children somewhere or remembers being one.

They lower a bucket over the lee side. Seawater strikes the planks as Mara drags it aboard. The first washing takes away what can be seen. It leaves salt in the wool and stiffness in the linen. Lysbeth rubs the cloth between finger and thumb.

It will scour his skin raw.

Water, Mara says.

Fresh water is locked beneath an officer's key. Cooking receives it. The sick receive it when the surgeon insists. Bodies receive it through cups measured twice a day. Laundry receives none.

Lysbeth takes their morning measures before Willem can drink and pours one into the bucket. The boy watches the clear stream disappear into grey cloth.

I am not thirsty, he says.

It is a poor lie. His lips are split at both corners.

Mara catches the cup before Lysbeth empties the second.

One.

Not enough.

One first.

She has not yet learned the Dutch for waste. She does not require it. They knead the linen in the little fresh water, roll it until their wrists ache, pour the rinse over the blanket and wring both against the rail. Willem stands between them holding the dry corners out of the dirt. When the sun comes, it finds three people guarding a line of damp wool as if it were a sail keeping the ship upright.

The under-merchant finds them first.

Whose water?

Lysbeth answers before Mara understands the question. Ours.

Drinking allowance is not washing allowance.

It was issued to us.

It was issued for drinking.

Mara knows *water*, *one* and *ours*. She knows the shape of the man's displeasure from every quay she has crossed. It is the pleasure of discovering that a rule can be made expensive for somebody below it.

The assistant is sent for. He arrives with the ration slate against his chest. The under-merchant points at Mara, then at the bucket, and says Joos de Vries's name. The rest comes slowly enough for her to assemble it: stores beyond allowance; servant; guarantee; charge at Bantam.

Willem goes white beneath his sunburn.

No, Mara says.

The under-merchant glances at her as if a cup has objected.

Lysbeth touches Mara's arm. Let me.

But Mara is looking at the assistant's slate. She has watched it every morning. A row for each mess, a stroke for every measure claimed from the cask, a cross when the issue is complete. The marks say that the widow's household receives six measures each day: three at dawn, three at sunset.

The dipper says something else.

Mara takes her own slate from the pocket of Pieter's coat. She draws three mouths at the top, not faces, only circles. Beside them she puts six strokes.

*Drie*, she says, touching the circles. *Zes*, touching the strokes.

The assistant frowns. She draws the rationer's dipper, a square with a handle, and beneath it five strokes. Then she points to yesterday on his slate, and the day before, and the day before that. Each day his hand has entered six. Each day the rationer has filled five, explaining the missing measure by spillage, settling or the cask's reluctance to give up its last inch. Mara has not understood the explanations. She has counted the pours.

Five, she says in Dutch. Five. Five. Five.

She lays four rows of five on her slate. Beside them she lays four rows of six. She points at the difference, then at the wet linen.

One, she says. Ours.

The under-merchant studies the figures. His anger changes direction without diminishing.

The assistant says the loss belongs to the cask.

Does the cask drink? Mara asks in Portuguese.

Nobody understands the sentence. Willem does. A laugh escapes him, small and treacherous. He shuts his mouth with both hands.

Mara points at the cask, opens her mouth, and mimes drinking. The sailors nearest the rail understand at once. One coughs into his sleeve. Another becomes urgently interested in the horizon.

The under-merchant does not laugh. He asks the assistant whether six has been entered against the household.

Yes.

Whether five has been issued.

The assistant begins another explanation about the dipper.

Five or six?

Five.

Then the household has not exceeded its allowance.

He reaches for Mara's slate. She keeps hold of it. For one moment they stand joined by a small grey square, his hand on the frame and hers on the opposite edge. Then he releases it and tells the assistant to correct the issue.

The assistant scratches at the existing row until one stroke becomes pale dust. He does not make another page. He does not need one. The allowance has not changed; only the claim about what happened to it.

Put the washing under the servant's effects, he says. The rule still forbids it.

Lysbeth steps between him and Mara.

No.

The word stops even the sailors pretending not to listen.

The linen is my household's, Lysbeth says. The water is my household's. If you require a disobedient name, write mine.

The under-merchant looks at the blanket, then at Willem. Understanding arrives. The boy stares at the deck hard enough to split it.

Lysbeth sees the same understanding and does not retreat.

You will not turn my son's shame into her debt. You will not set it against the bond of a man ashore because that is the easiest line to reach.

The under-merchant's face reddens. He has been offered three choices: accuse the widow, name the child, or admit the ship owes what its slate says it issued. All three have witnesses.

No charge, he says.

The assistant rubs away the first mark he has begun beneath Mara's name.

The household receives the missing measure at sunset. Lysbeth divides it among three cups without ceremony. Willem drinks his too quickly and coughs half of it down his shirt. Mara waits for his horror, then holds out the wet cloth and considers it.

Laundry, she says.

His laugh comes out with the cough. Even Lysbeth smiles.

The blanket takes two days to dry. At night Willem lies on bare boards beside his mother, and Mara gives him one of the folded coats from beneath her hip. He refuses because it is hers. She tells him a loan is not a gift. He asks how she will know it is still hers while he is using it.

Mara puts the slate between them and draws one coat, one boy and an arrow that returns.

Mine, she says, then points at the arrow. Tomorrow.

Willem adds a second arrow, longer than the first, and points east.

Bantam.

She nods. He has understood more than the coat.

After that he brings her words that can be counted or kept. Half. Enough. Owed. Returned. He does not speak of the blanket, and she does not speak of the look on his face in the dark. The ship has too little room for secrets, so kindness consists mostly of deciding which truths need no witness.

Four weeks south, Willem finds Pieter's packet beneath Mara's folded coat and works one fingernail under the tarred string.

Mara catches his wrist before she knows she has moved.

The boy makes one small sound. Her grip is not Silas's grip — two fingers, no pain, released at once — but for a moment the hold is back around her: the dark, somebody deciding that what lies beside her body lies within his right, the old lesson arriving faster than the present can stop it.

Lysbeth crosses the cabin. Mara rises with the packet against her chest and waits to learn whether a servant may own the thing a child of the household wants.

Mine, she says.

It is the first Dutch word she has used wrongly on purpose. The grammar requires more. The word does not.

Lysbeth looks from her to Willem.

You heard her, she says. Ask before you touch what is hers.

The boy's face has gone red. Sorry, he says in Portuguese, one of the six words Mara has taught him. Then, in Dutch: I wanted to see the sailor's islands.

So do I, Mara says.

Lysbeth does not ask to inspect the packet. She does not remind Mara who obtained the passage, or who has the bunk and who has the floor, or what the paper in Amsterdam calls either of them. She takes Willem away by the shoulder and leaves the ownership standing exactly where Mara put it.

That evening she teaches Mara the missing grammar. *Van mij.* Belonging to me. *Eigen.* One's own. *Toestemming.* Permission.

Mara writes all three beneath *thuis*. Home. Ownership. Permission. The words look less related on the page than they are.

The next morning Willem brings the slate and asks her to write his whole name. She does. He copies it underneath with the letters leaning against one another for support. Mara makes him do it again, not because the first is unreadable, but because a name should learn early how to survive a poor hand.

By the Cape she can follow orders. By Madagascar she can follow lies.

After Madagascar the voyage stops pretending to be only a voyage. The powder entered so carefully in Amsterdam is brought up. Off Mozambique, Portuguese guns answer Dutch ones and the ship shudders through the soles of Mara's feet. She and Lysbeth wait below with Willem between them while dust shakes from the beams and men overhead shout distances into the smoke.

When Mara is allowed on deck again, a captured hull is burning to leeward. An assistant is already entering its cargo on a slate. The smoke has not cleared; the ownership has.

The fleet goes north to Goa with its guns visible, then to Calicut with gifts, letters and men dressed for a room. An agreement comes back aboard under careful hands. So do two wounded sailors, one Dutch and one Portuguese, because pain has crossed the language before Mara reaches them. Lysbeth works on the first. Mara holds water to the second man's mouth and translates his thanks, though nobody needs her to.

In the evening the same assistant who counted shot makes a fair copy of the agreement. The guns and the page are not opposites. They are the Company's two hands.

She opens Pieter's packet only when she can read the first page without asking for help.

His hand is small and crowded. Hendricks was right about the sailor's parts. Wind. Latitude. Beer. The price of oranges. A girl in the Texel roads who had one brown eye and one grey and promised nothing. Then the coast of Guinea, the Cape, Madagascar, Java. The entries shorten as the heat grows. The spelling worsens after Bantam.

Pieter never reaches the islands farther east. He knows them by what other vessels bring into Bantam: cloves drying, nutmeg split under a thumbnail, names passed west with the cargo. His own pages hold Bantam, Madura and Bali. Villages come out to trade and boats remain just beyond musket range. Captains make agreements in one anchorage and enforce different ones in the next. The book records what the voyage bought because that is what a sailor is meant to notice.

In the margins Pieter records what it took.

A hostage held until the price improves.

A storehouse burned after weights are disputed.

Two men struck from the ration list before they die, so the provision book will balance when they do.

Pieter never uses the word wolves. He does not need it. He writes the centre and the margin in the same ink, and the distance between them is the whole confession.

Near the end, fever loosens the hand but not the habit. One sentence repeats across three pages.

*Make another. Make another. Make another.*

Mara asks Lysbeth what he means.

The widow reads the pages at the stern window while the ship works under them. Not the sentence, she says. The account. He means make another copy. The under-merchant took his first one. Pieter tried to write it again from memory. That is why some pages repeat.

Why did the under-merchant take it?

Lysbeth reads farther.

Because he said everything written aboard belonged to the owners.

He wrote it.

That does not make it his.

Mara looks at the packet that crossed six years in Hendricks's locker and half a world under her coat because one frightened sailor wrote the same truth twice.

She thinks of Bell across the centre table and his copied page in the dark beneath the bar. One confession signed before the knife found him. A whole room nearly too late.

After that, she studies everything she is permitted to touch.

At first it is language work: ration headings, orders posted by the mainmast, the under-merchant's lists. She copies headings and practises their forms on waste paper, never whole documents, never yet a second account. She learns the difference between a writer's words and a clerk's hand. She learns which corrections require a line and which require a knife. She learns that a seal proves only that wax was warm near authority.

The ship carries one set of papers east.

Mara carries the knowledge that one set is only a single throat.

***

## IV. Bantam

Bantam arrives first as smell.

Not nutmeg alone, though nutmeg is there, warm and sweet over the water. Pepper. Woodsmoke. Fish drying. Coconut oil. Mud opened by the sun. Drains that meet the harbour without ceremony. Wet timber everywhere — hulls, jetties, warehouses, houses raised above ground that has never decided whether it is land.

Then sound: Javanese, Malay, Portuguese, Dutch, Chinese tongues, Gujarati; bells, bargaining, axes, prayer, gulls and men calling weights across water.

Then the shore itself, low and green behind a roadstead crowded with vessels from more nations than the clerk in Amsterdam had columns.

The Dutch *loge* is costly stone pretending to permanence: warehouse below, sleeping rooms above, a fenced yard, armed men at the gate and damp in everything. It is not the fortress the Company will build elsewhere when trade learns to call itself rule. The English call such a place a factory. Nothing is made there except advantage and paper.

Lysbeth's brother meets the boat late and angry. He has prepared for a widow and a boy. Mara is an extra problem until his sister shows him the Amsterdam entry.

Household servant, he reads.

The paper ends here, Mara says.

It does, Lysbeth says.

Her Dutch is careful, but it is Dutch.

He looks at her properly then.

What will you do?

Work.

At what?

What is misunderstood?

This appears to him an insolent answer. Before he can say so, a quarrel breaks in the yard.

A Portuguese-speaking broker has delivered twelve sacks of pepper and been receipted for ten. The warehouse clerk insists two were spoiled by river water and rejected. The broker insists rejected goods remain his goods. A Malay boatman insists the two sacks were carried inside with the others. The clerk has Dutch and a little market Malay. The broker and boatman share Portuguese enough for weights and anger. Nobody with authority has the two languages required; nobody without authority is believed. Everyone has armed friends.

Mara listens.

The Dutch clerk says *twelve received, two rejected*.

The Portuguese broker hears *ten received*.

The boatman says the wet sacks were opened, dried and mixed into another lot before weighing. He points to twelve tally cuts on the warehouse post. The clerk says the final two are from yesterday. The boatman shows him yesterday's cuts, dark with pepper dust; the last two expose pale new wood.

Mara looks at the cuts.

She translates each sentence without improving it. This is harder than it sounds. Men who want victory always offer the translator a better sentence than the one they said.

The factor comes down at last, coat unfastened, furious at being dragged into another quarrel in the wet yard. He orders payment for eleven sacks: ten sound, one allowed against the mixed pepper, the spoiled balance to be argued after the next tide. It is not justice. It is a sum everyone can survive until morning.

The armed friends move their hands away from their knives.

The factor asks Mara where she learned Malay.

I did not.

You spoke to the boatman.

He has Portuguese from Malacca. Enough for weights. Not enough for your clerk.

And Dutch?

On account.

The factor studies her. A Company house notices translation only when it fails. He offers her the lowest place in it: meals, a mat in an upper storeroom, no wage for the first month, and protection under the lodge's gate while she proves useful.

From whom? Mara asks.

He does not like the question.

From the town.

And inside the gate?

You will be under Company discipline.

That is not the same as protection.

It is what I can enter.

Mara looks at the broker, who is waiting for his receipt. At the boatman, who will be gone on the tide. At the clerk already gathering the disputed papers into one neat stack, where by tomorrow they will tell whatever story the top sheet requires.

One month, she says. Meals. A room with a bolt on the inside. And every translation bears my name.

Translators do not sign Company receipts.

Then the Dutch words are yours, and the Portuguese words are mine, and when they disagree you may explain it yourself.

The factor looks toward the broker.

Your name, he says.

Mara de Sousa.

He has the clerk add it beneath the settlement.

One month, the factor says. Meals, the bolt and your name beneath the work.

Mara translates the receipt aloud to the broker. Twelve delivered. Ten accepted sound. One credited from the mixed lot. One held in dispute. She makes him repeat the numbers back to her. Then she watches the clerk sand the page.

When he carries it upstairs for the factor's seal, she takes a clean sheet from the bottom of the stack.

With the original upstairs, she reproduces it from the page in her head: date, names, weights, settlement, her own new line beneath it. The clerk returns before she has finished and tells her the paper belongs to the Company.

The first does, Mara says.

She writes the final figure.

This one belongs to the man whose pepper it records.

She gives the duplicate to the broker. Not hidden. Not apologetically. In front of the clerk, the factor, the widow and the wet-timber yard.

The broker folds it into oilcloth and puts it inside his shirt.

That night Mara finishes Pieter's book by an oil lamp in a room whose bolt closes from her side. His final legible entry is not about islands. It is about paper.

*I told it once and they took the telling. I tell it again because the taking does not make it untold.*

Below it, written during the fever in a hand that barely reaches the end of the line:

*Make another.*

Mara closes the book. She does not burn it.

In the yard below, pepper is being moved before the rain. A clerk calls a number. A broker disputes it. Somewhere in the dark, a second copy waits against a man's skin.

The Company has brought Mara east as a servant because its book has nowhere else to put her.

Before her first day is over, she has begun another book.

# The Counter-Ledger (1603–1611)

> **1603–1611**
>
> *“The ships keep the wage. I keep the leaving.”*
>
> — Maggie, keeper of the Pelican

***

## I. What Men Leave

The first keepsake after Tom's thimble is a horn button.

It comes off a coat already missing two others. The boy worries it loose while he drinks, works the thread until it parts and puts the button on the counter beside his Company's paper.

Till I come back, he says.

Maggie looks at the coat. You will want that before Gravesend.

I've two left.

Then you are rich in buttons and poor in sense.

She takes it anyway.

The custom is older than the Company. Men have left pipe-stoppers, buckles, bent coins and once a good knife whose owner spent the whole passage borrowing worse ones. A keepsake is a wager small enough to fit in a fist. The house holds one end. The sea holds the other.

Before the charters and eastern voyages, most wagers returned within a season. A Baltic man came back with tar in his beard. A coastal hand came back without a boot and took his buckle home to mend the replacement. Sometimes a wife collected the object instead, face prepared before she entered, and Maggie fed her before explaining what the paid-off crew had said.

The new voyages take years. The objects remain long enough to acquire dust.

Maggie puts the horn button into the narrow fault beneath the bar. Matthew Bell's folded account lies against the back of the oak, his full name written outside in Arthur's careful hand. Tom's brass thimble rests beside it. Blood has dried at one corner of Bell's page. Ale cannot reach the gap, even when men spill a full jug directly above it. Maggie has tested this without meaning to.

Her hand goes to the sharp knife in her apron, then stops.

The keepsake records the leaving. A notch records the failure to return.

There is one exception already in the wood. Maggie cut Tom's mark before dawn, while his ship still waited and fear had not yet learned patience. Since then she waits. A vessel may be overdue, storm-damaged, in another harbour or gone. Rumour does not choose among them. When a man comes home, he may have his keepsake and no mark is cut. When the sea keeps him past every honest hope, she cuts once.

***

## II. The First Claim

The horn button's owner does come back.

He enters the Pelican fifteen months later with yellow skin, two fewer teeth and a purse containing less than his mother was promised. Maggie recognizes him only after he asks for the button. The boy who left had cheeks. This man has hollows and a habit of standing where he can see the door.

Food first, she says.

I can pay.

That was not the question.

She gives him broth with barley and the heel of yesterday's loaf. He eats too fast, stops when the stomach objects, waits and begins again more slowly. Only after the bowl is clean does she ask for the names.

Not numbers. Names.

The Company's paid-off paper says twenty-three deceased before the Cape and seven thereafter. He remembers nineteen Christian names, three nicknames and one man called only Welsh by everybody aboard. For the other seven he has faces, berths and counties, but no names he trusts.

Maggie does not supply dignity he did not have at sea. She enters Welsh in the room's memory and makes the returned man repeat the others until the regulars can carry them too.

Then she gives him the horn button.

He turns it over. You kept it.

That was the arrangement.

Did you cut one for me?

No.

You did not know I was coming back.

No.

Then why not?

Because I did not know you weren't.

The button was the wager. A cut would have been the verdict. The sea has returned his body. It does not return the months to his mother, the teeth to his mouth or the thirty men to anybody. No mark says only that he is here, not that the leaving has been cancelled.

The boy sews the button back onto his coat before he goes. It does not match the new ones.

Men sometimes ask Maggie which cut belongs to which sailor. She can point to many: Tom beside the knot in the grain, the one premature mark; a pair of brothers close enough that the knife broke the ridge between them. Others have lost their owners. She remembers a face but not its mark, or a mark but only that the boy's paper named a ship beginning with Saint.

This does not shame her. The counter-ledger does not pretend to the Company's exactness. The Company's book can give the month, wage and deduction while losing the person entire. Maggie's wood keeps the opposite truth: somebody sat here, ate, feared and did not come back. When memory can supply the name, the room says it. When memory fails, the cut does not acquire a convenient one.

An honest blank is still an entry.

***

## III. The Empty Stool

Barnaby keeps his promise by Whitsun.

The cough does not take him in the Pelican. It gives him that much. He dies in the narrow room behind a sail loft with a folded rag on his chest and two apprentices arguing in whispers over who must tell Maggie.

Both come. She gives them bread and dripping because they have been awake all night. Then she makes them begin again when they call him the old sailmaker.

Barnaby, she says.

Barnaby, they say.

The parish puts him in Wapping churchyard. Carter pays for enough ale that every man who carried the coffin can drink without converting grief into credit. Josiah drinks two shares and complains that a man ought to get a better plot after forty years in English canvas. Nobody answers. Elias puts Barnaby's cup upside down on the stool nearest the hearth.

The next evening a stranger from Limehouse reaches for that stool.

Taken, Maggie says.

The man looks at the empty seat. By whom?

Barnaby.

He waits for the joke. None comes. He chooses another place.

The cup is put away after a week, but the stool remains empty. It becomes awkward on crowded nights. Men stand with ale in their hands while good oak holds nobody. Carter suggests moving it against the wall until the mourning has had a decent term.

Who decides the term? Maggie asks.

Carter does not volunteer.

The room begins keeping things that are not inside the fault. A stool can be an entry. So can a name said properly. So can the dark shape in the grain of the centre table, scrubbed so often that only the person doing the scrubbing knows where to look.

Paper is not the only material that can hold an account.

That is fortunate. Paper belongs too easily to the man with the locked room.

***

## IV. The Fault

Twice a year, when the weather is dry enough, Maggie empties the gap beneath the counter.

She does it before dawn with the door bolted and the shutters fast. Bell's page comes out first. She unfolds it once, no farther than the old creases permit, and checks the blood-dark corner for damp. She cannot read every line, but Arthur read it aloud that first night, and memory has kept what schooling did not: Mara between cordage and spoiled timber; the Fletcher debt; account disputed; no discharge permitted.

She knows the page by position as much as words. Names above. Sums to the right. Bell's confession below, where his hand begins to slope because fear or haste has reached it.

The thimble leaves a small green shadow against the oak. Maggie rubs it clean with salt and a corner of her apron. The brass is plain, worn on one side by Tom's mother. Maggie never uses it. A kept tool is not therefore available.

Other objects move through.

A copper farthing with a hole bored through the king's face goes back to a sister after fever. A small key is collected by a man who returns to find the chest it opened sold for rent. A bone die remains three years, then five, until a widow recognizes the drilled corner and carries it home in silence. Maggie gives her eel pie before she brings the die out. Grief does not improve because the order is reversed.

The fault is not a grave. It is a harbour with a poor tide. Things wait there until somebody with a claim can take them.

Bell's account has no claimant who can safely keep it. Tom's thimble has one, but he is beyond the Cape or under the water or alive somewhere paper can reach and Maggie cannot. Those two stay.

When the gap is empty, she puts her fingers into the narrow dark and feels the oak. No rot. No wet. One splinter that catches the skin each time. She leaves it. A hiding place too smooth looks made.

Then everything goes back: page flat, thimble behind it, the temporary wagers nearest her hand.

She wipes the counter above them though it does not need wiping.

***

## V. Stone and Oak

The room changes by repair, which is how a room changes without permission.

Rushes near the hearth begin to hold more mud than they hide. Men come through the door in greater numbers now: Company crews, chandler's hands, porters from new warehouses, boys sent down from the City with sealed instructions and no money for supper. The rushes sour within a day. Maggie has them swept away and buys worn flagstones from a house being pulled down upriver.

The mason says the stones do not match.

They need to lie flat, she says. They do not need to agree.

The new flags reach from the hearth to the first tables. The old boards remain at the counter and beneath the centre table. Bell died on wood. Maggie does not preserve the stain as a relic; she simply refuses to let a builder improve the event out of the room.

The bar grows too short next.

On pay nights men stand three deep. Ale crosses above other men's shoulders. An elbow knocks a candle onto the floor and Ned stamps it out before the rush basket catches. Carter offers good oak at cost and a joiner who can replace the whole counter in two days.

Before the joiner arrives, Maggie puts Bell's page inside her dress and the waiting keepsakes in her locked flour chest. For two days the fault holds nothing but its own dark.

Extend it, Maggie says.

The joiner crouches beneath the old wood and finds the fault with his thumb.

This length is split.

It has held twenty years.

It will hold better replaced.

Then make the new part hold the old.

He looks at her, then at the mallet on the shelf, and chooses craftsmanship over argument.

The longer bar turns at the short end and adds room for six more men. For a year the new oak is pale beside the old. Sleeves, palms, spilled ale and Maggie's rag darken it by degrees. The seam remains visible if the light comes sideways.

Underneath, the original length stays exactly where it was. The notches acquire neighbours. The fault remains dry.

The Company would call this enlargement. More room, more custom, better return from the same house.

Maggie calls it six more men she can feed before somebody asks them to sign.

***

## VI. The Years in Wood

Pell ships again in 1608 on the *Ascension*.

He leaves no keepsake.

Already left two fingers in Bantam, he says, holding up the damaged hand. The sea has security enough.

The *Ascension* does not return when expected. Then it does not return when lateness becomes worry. Men bring rumours: sighted at the Cape; put into an island; taken by the Portuguese; safe in Bantam; all hands dead. Each account arrives confident and leaves contradicted.

Maggie enters none of them into the wood. A rumour does not earn a cut merely by crossing an ocean.

When the Company's own list finally places the *Ascension* among the ships not returned, she cuts Pell's notch. One loss. One mark. The exact manner can wait for a man who knows.

Elias dies the following winter, not at sea but of everything the sea left in him. His back fails first. Then his lungs. He spends his last month giving orders nobody needs and correcting knots tied by hands that have not touched rope in years. The room buries him near Barnaby.

His stool does not remain empty. There are too many old men and too few places near the fire. But for one winter every man who sits there is told whose back polished the rail. By the next, men tell newcomers that Elias would have judged the matter differently. His body has become a standard measure.

Arthur stops coming long before anybody declares him absent. At first his place at the end of the bar is saved through one drink, then half an evening, then not at all. His last chalked wager remains on the slate after both the named storm and the date have passed. Maggie leaves it until damp draws the figures into a white blur, then washes the slate herself. No answer comes back.

By 1611 the Company is no longer a desperate adventure men discuss as though it may vanish before the next rent day. Its carts claim the centre of Wapping Wall. Its warehouses make shadows where children used to play. Boys enter the Pelican carrying printed articles instead of promises copied for one voyage.

One of them sits at the long end of the new bar with rain in his hair and no money for food. Maggie puts stew before him. Only when he has eaten does she ask whose paper is inside his coat.

Company, he says.

Do you want the sea?

He gives her the answer hunger and a clerk have prepared together.

She makes him answer again after bread.

When he leaves, he puts a wooden toggle on the counter. Maggie places it in the fault. Her hand finds the knife, then leaves it in her apron. No news yet.

Her fingers pass close to one older groove, deep and pale once, dark now with eight years of ale and air. Tom's mark. The thimble waits behind it. No reliable word has reached the house from the man who left it.

It is the first mark she ever cut before the sea answered. She has not cut early since.

The room grows. The Company grows. Beneath the old length of bar, where neither enlargement reaches, the first account remains disputed.

# The Teak Desk (1605–1612)

> **1605–1612**
>
> *“The difference between a servant and a broker is who writes the rate.”*
>
> — Mara, Bantam, 1612

***

## I. Thirty-One Days

On the thirty-first morning, the factor discovers that a month has ended.

Mara has known since midnight.

Her mat is rolled. Pieter's coat lies across it, the book inside the coat, the slate and waste-paper booklet tied together with the pencil Joos gave her. On the floor beside them is her own account of the month: twenty-seven receipts translated, nine letters, four disputes in the yard, three sets of weights made to mean the same thing in two languages. Beneath each entry is the name the factor agreed would stand below the work.

Mara de Sousa.

The factor reads the total and asks why her room is packed.

The month is finished, she says.

We made no arrangement for a second.

Exactly.

He offers the same terms. Meals. The upper storeroom. The bolt. Protection inside the gate.

Those were the terms for proving useful.

You have proved it.

Then usefulness has a price.

The factor tells her there is no place for a female clerk on the wage book. Mara asks whether Dutch changes its meaning in a woman's mouth. He says that is not the point. She asks whether Portuguese does. He says she is being difficult.

She has learned this word already. In a Company house, *difficult* means the price has begun to move in the wrong direction.

Half a junior clerk's wage, she says. Paid weekly. Paper at cost. My name remains on every translation.

Impossible.

Then your Portuguese ends today.

She lifts the mat.

The factor watches her carry the room out in one load. That is the danger of giving a person nothing: nothing is quick to pack.

Outside the gate, Iskandar — the pepper broker from the wet-yard dispute — waits beneath the warehouse eaves with the first duplicate Mara made in Bantam folded inside his shirt. The settlement held. The Dutch clerk tried two days later to call both wet sacks spoiled; Iskandar unfolded the second receipt, found another Dutch reader and collected the missing credit.

Now he has a Portuguese debt note from Malacca, a Javanese pepper grower who will accept neither his figures nor the Dutch ones, and a Chinese buyer who speaks Cantonese badly enough to be dangerous and Malay well enough to deny it.

What is your rate? he asks.

Mara looks back at the gate. The factor has not followed her. He still believes the gate is the valuable thing.

She names a price.

Iskandar argues it down by one-tenth. Mara lets him, because a broker who accepts the first number is either desperate or lying, and she needs to know which kind he is. He pays half before she opens the debt note.

The coin buys seven nights in a room above a maker of scales, from a landlady who demonstrates the bolt and waits while Mara tests it herself. Mara pays in advance and asks for a receipt. The room is narrow, hot and entirely accounted for.

A rented door is still somebody else's door. But the key is in Mara's hand, the term is written, and no gratitude is owing on either side.

It will do for seven nights.

***

## II. What the Translation Buys

Seven nights become a month because the work returns before the rent is due.

Iskandar's pepper growers bring contracts in market Malay and arguments in three dialects of impatience. Portuguese merchants bring letters whose courtesies occupy half the page and whose demand sits in six words at the bottom. The English factory sends a boy with shipping notes whenever its factors suspect the Dutch version has improved Dutch interests. The Dutch send for Mara whenever the English version has done the same.

She does not learn every language in Bantam. Nobody does. She learns where each one stops.

Her Malay becomes good enough for weights, weather and refusal. Hokkien remains a fast current she can cross only by another person's bridge. Cantonese lets her deal directly with some southern merchants and tells her exactly when a Dutch clerk is calling two Chinese tongues one language for his own convenience. English comes first in scraps from the factory: headings, dates, the words *received*, *damaged*, *owing*. She acquires it in the old order — numbers before mercy.

Within the year, the factor who could find no wage-book place for her crosses the market and stands in the scale-maker's doorway.

He carries a folded Portuguese dispatch taken from a coastal boat. The seal has been opened and pressed closed badly. Mara reads the first lines without touching it.

It is a victualling account. Rice, fresh water, powder and shot. A Portuguese supply vessel is to leave Malacca on the next fair wind, pass south of Sumatra and make for the islands to the east. The name of the vessel is given. So are the master, the tonnage and the day on which a patrol waiting in the strait will know where to find her.

The factor watches Mara reach the date.

Well?

What will you do with it?

That is not translation.

No. It is the rate.

He understands. Military information costs more than pepper because pepper does not remember the translator's face.

Mara names the fee. He pays it from a purse kept for expenses nobody wants itemised in Amsterdam.

She translates the dispatch exactly. She makes two copies and has the factor mark both as received from her hand. One goes through the Dutch gate. One goes beneath Pieter's coat.

Three weeks later, a Portuguese vessel enters Bantam roadstead under Dutch guard with her mainyard damaged and dark scoring along one side. The captured casks come ashore first: rice, water, powder, shot, each one answering the line Mara copied. The crew follow in irons. One man is carried.

The factor finds her watching.

Your paper, he says, with the satisfaction of a man returning a tool to its maker. We had her before she saw us.

On the quay, one of the prisoners hears the Portuguese and turns. He looks at Mara, at the Dutch factor beside her, at the casks between them. He does not know what she translated. He knows enough.

Traitor, he says.

Mara owes Portugal nothing. Portuguese hands carried her from one sale to the next, Portuguese words recorded the prices, and no flag in that roadstead has ever claimed her except as cargo. None of this makes the man wrong.

The factor offers a second fee for examining the captured papers.

Mara takes it.

That evening Lysbeth finds her counting the payment.

You knew what the dispatch would do, she says.

Yes.

You knew they would take the ship.

Yes.

And the men aboard her.

Mara squares the coins with one finger.

Yes.

Why translate it?

Because I wanted what the money would buy.

Lysbeth waits. Mara lets her wait. There is no cleaner answer behind the first one.

That money buys the desk.

A joiner near the pepper market makes it from teak without carving: four square legs, a broad top, one shallow drawer for instruments and one deep drawer in which a page may lie flat. Mara rejects the first top because it rocks. The joiner planes one corner, sets it down again and waits while she tests it with both palms.

Iskandar watches four men carry it up the scale-maker's stairs.

That will be troublesome when you have to run, he says.

Yes.

Why buy it?

Because it will be troublesome when someone tries to take it.

The first paper Mara lays on the teak is her copy of the Portuguese dispatch. Independence bought with another man's irons is still independence. It is also still bought with the irons.

She keeps both entries.

***

## III. The Westbound Account

Lysbeth builds a smaller trade with the skills nobody entered on her passenger line. Sailors come to her for fever, merchants for the complaints they do not want in a Company's surgeon's book, women from the market for everything European men have trained themselves not to see. She charges those who can pay and does not discuss the others with Mara. Willem carries water and cleans instruments.

By September, two Dutch ships are preparing to sail west.

Lysbeth's brother offers what he believes is generosity. If she stays, Willem may remain under his account and, when he is older, be found a place in the loge. A clerk's stool. A Company's training. A future already reduced to a line the boy has not asked to read.

Lysbeth books passage on the first ship.

I meant to bring one man east and buried him before we sailed, she tells Mara. I brought one boy east to keep him alive. I will not let another man call his future a place found for him.

The return entry names Lysbeth as a paying passenger and Willem as her son. It contains no advance, no service owed on landing and no claim on the boy's future wages. Mara reads it twice. Lysbeth reads it once and then reads it aloud to Willem, because a child who has crossed the world inside other people's paper is old enough to hear what the return journey says.

He signs his own name.

The letters no longer lean against one another. Mara looks for the boy's old uncertain hand and cannot find it.

I practised, Willem says.

I can see that.

It is the closest she comes to praise. He understands and stands taller.

On the morning they sail, Mara sets two oilskin packets on the teak and gives Lysbeth one. Each contains the fever pages from Pieter's book in Mara's exact copy, a letter for Hendricks and a witnessed account showing the day Mara's bonded service ended. Joos can place that account in front of the Amsterdam Chamber and recover what remains of his guarantee.

Two packets? Lysbeth asks.

One goes with you. One leaves on the next English ship.

You do not trust me.

I do not trust water.

Lysbeth smiles. She has known Mara long enough not to ask for a softer answer.

You could come west, she says. There would be room.

In whose house?

Mine.

Mara looks at Willem holding his own articles, at the roadstead beyond him, at the ships turning on their anchors toward every place she has survived.

No, she says. But I know what the offer is worth.

Lysbeth accepts this too.

Willem stops in front of Mara with his arms half-raised.

May I?

Mara considers the question as seriously as he asks it.

Once, she says.

He hugs her. Mara stands rigid. Then one hand comes up and rests between his shoulder blades, once, like a seal applied to something already agreed.

When the ship is under way, Lysbeth remains at the rail. Willem holds up the packet. Mara does not wave. She stands beside Iskandar until the hull becomes one more dark mark among dark marks and the water takes it west.

Pieter's original does not travel. It goes to a place neither Company gate nor teak drawer knows.

His truth travels twice.

***

## IV. The Rate

The years after Lysbeth leaves begin to arrive as cargo.

Cotton and steel from the Coromandel coast. Lists of cloth wanted in Pattani, entrusted to Chinese merchants whose own books are older than the Company employing them. Diamonds sent through Bantam in packets small enough to vanish into a cuff and valuable enough to make six men watch the cuff. Pepper always: dry, wet, short, adulterated, promised, stolen, taxed twice and described as honest in every language available.

Each cargo brings paper. Each paper brings Mara another hand.

The city supplies the harder education. In 1608 men from the court kill a governor and seize the young ruler and his mother; offices change hands, seals change owners and agreements written the week before discover that accuracy does not make them sovereign. In 1609 the new regent orders Chinese debtors released from English custody despite the bills the English chief waves in answer. Mara reads the English debts and the Malay order. Both are true. Only one commands the gate that morning.

The English chief comes to the teak desk with three signed bills and asks Mara to find the error in them. She finds none. Names, amounts, dates, marks: the debts are properly recorded.

Then the release is unlawful, he says.

Under whose law?

The bills are valid.

Yes.

The order contradicts them.

No. The bills say money is owed. The order says the men are released. You want the first paper to prevent the second from existing.

What use is a true debt if the debtor walks out of the gate?

Mara thinks of a room in Wapping that found a murderer truly and let him walk out of its door.

It remains a true debt, she says. Paper records power. It does not replace it.

The Englishman pays for the reading and leaves with all three bills still true and none of the debtors still behind his gate. He never thanks Mara for the distinction. He brings her work again the following month.

Paper can prove what was promised. It cannot decide who must care.

By 1612, nobody in Bantam agrees what Mara is.

The Dutch call her an interpreter when entering her fee and an outside person when objecting to her copies.
