The English call her a broker when they need Dutch correspondence read and a Dutch agent when her reading does not favour them. Portuguese merchants call her Portuguese until she charges them, then discover objections in her history. Iskandar calls her Mara and sends the difficult work first.

The harbour's officials know her more accurately: she is the woman at the teak desk whose two versions agree.

The desk has moved out of the room above the scale-maker and into a ground-floor counting room of its own, midway between the Dutch loge and the English factory and belonging to neither. The shallow drawer holds pens, knives, sand and seal impressions that prove only what warm wax looked like on the day it was pressed. The deep drawer holds copies. The originals do not stay together and do not sleep where Mara does.

She buys the silk from a merchant newly in from Macao. Dark, almost black until the sun finds blue in it, plain enough not to advertise the price. The merchant offers it in gratitude after Mara finds a missing zero in a Dutch order. Mara pays him anyway. Gifts between friends may be given. Gifts between merchants are unfinished accounts.

The coat is cut loose enough for the heat and close enough at the sleeves not to drag through ink. Dark silk does not show the day's small stains. This is the practical reason. By the third time a client waits rather than interrupt while she writes, it has become something else.

A new Dutch senior merchant arrives in the wet season and sends a boy to summon the translator.

Mara sends back her rate.

The boy returns with the message that Company business takes precedence.

Mara sends back the rate again.

At noon the senior merchant crosses the market himself. He finds an English factor already waiting, Iskandar in the second chair and a clerk from the harbour master's office watching Mara compare two measures of pepper without improving either one.

I sent for you, the Dutchman says.

I received it.

You were employed by our loge.

For one month.

The Company brought you east.

The Company entered me east. The distinction has made you money for seven years.

He looks at the desk, the silk, the three men whose work is ahead of his.

Whom do you work for now?

The parties named on the page.

He puts a Portuguese letter on the teak. Mara does not reach for it.

Your rate is excessive.

Then the letter is inexpensive.

He sits.

Two copies, Mara says. My name beneath both. Changes after my hand are marked by the person who makes them.

Why two?

One for you. One for what you later say I wrote.

The Dutchman looks at Iskandar, who makes a sympathetic face and offers no help.

You have become very useful, the Dutchman says.

Useful is your word.

What is yours?

Broker.

What is the difference?

Mara draws the Portuguese letter toward her.

I write the rate.

Outside, the wet market carries on in several languages she can work in and a dozen she cannot. A ship out in the roads fires a salute. Nobody in the room looks up.

Mara is not safe. Safety is still a door and every door still has a keeper. But the teak desk is too heavy to run with and too heavy for one man to take.

She sits behind it in dark silk while the next man waits.

# The Return (1611–1612)

> **1611–1612**
>
> *“Don’t come back until you remember what a human life is worth.”*
>
> — Maggie, keeper of the Pelican, 1612

***

## I. Outside Surat

Tom buys the blue cotton because it is the wrong size for everything.

Too small for a dress. Too fine for a working cloth. Too large for a kerchief and too bright for any table in Wapping that has men cutting tar on it. It lies folded on a Gujarati broker's mat outside Surat, blue beneath red climbing flowers, one edge printed unevenly where the block took more dye than the next.

The imperfection makes him stop.

Around it, the broker has arranged lengths made to invite certainty. White calico by the bale. Chintz whose colours answer English orders exactly enough to travel. Coarse cloth for sailors, finer pieces for merchants' wives, all measured and priced according to uses Tom can name.

The blue square has no declared use.

The broker watches Tom unfold it.

For a child? he asks in Portuguese.

No.

A wife?

No.

Mother?

Tom folds the cloth along the wrong crease.

The broker takes it back and folds it properly.

You have somebody, he says.

That was not one of the choices.

It was all of them.

Tom dislikes him at once.

The broker's name is Ratan. He deals in cloth the English factory buys by the thousand and in small pieces English sailors buy when the thousand has made them feel rich. He knows the difference between cargo and an object a man will carry himself.

Tom asks the price.

Ratan names one fit for a wife.

Tom offers one fit for a rag.

They meet at a number fit for neither.

Ratan wraps the cotton in oiled paper and asks where it is going.

England.

That is the ship. Where after the ship?

Tom thinks of the Pelican's long counter, Maggie's rag over one shoulder, the pale brass thimble he left in her hand eight years ago. He has sent no letter because every first sentence sounds like an excuse and every second sounds like a report.

I don't know.

Ratan binds the packet with thread.

Not knowing where a thing goes is different from having nowhere for it, he says.

Tom pays before the broker can improve the observation.

He puts the packet inside his sea chest beneath a spare shirt. That night he moves it above the shirt in case water comes through the lid.

***

## II. The Climbing Flower

Tom returns to Ratan two days later because the Company has rejected three bales.

The factor says the blue is inconsistent and the red borders have wandered. He wants a reduction large enough to make the whole order profitable twice. Tom is sent with the rejected samples because the factor has discovered that a boatswain's coat looks more threatening than a clerk's and costs nothing extra to deploy.

Ratan examines the cloth.

He does not defend it. He asks Tom to come with him.

They leave the merchants' mats and pass into lanes where the finished bales become work again. Cotton drying beneath awnings. Pots of dye sunk into packed earth. Blocks carved with flowers, vines and small repeating geometries, each one dark at the edges from years of colour. Men print long lengths against low tables. Women rinse cloth in water already turned blue by previous rinsing. Children carry finished pieces to lines without stepping on the wet ones underfoot.

The climbing flower on Tom's square appears on one table.

A printer sets the block, strikes once and lifts. The next impression begins where the first ends. If his wrist turns by the width of a grain, the stem breaks. If the cloth stretches, the flowers climb at different speeds.

Ratan puts the rejected Company sample beside the printer's work.

The colour changed because rain entered one dye yard. The border wandered because the cloth shrank after printing. Both defects are real. Neither makes the cloth useless.

The factor ordered equal, Ratan says.

Then make it equal.

The printer looks up although he cannot understand the Portuguese. Tone crosses languages cheaply.

Ratan answers without heat.

Make the rain equal first.

Tom looks again at his own square. One red flower is heavier than the next. He bought it because of that difference without asking whose hand made it.

What happens to the rejected bales? he asks.

If your factor pays less, the loss travels backward until it reaches somebody too poor to send it farther.

Who?

Ratan points to the printer.

Tom knows this machinery. Harcourt's debt travels the same way in the other direction, always stopping at the man who cannot pass it on.

He returns to the factory with a smaller reduction than the factor demanded and an account separating rain damage from usable variation. The factor calls the distinction sentimental.

Tom opens every bale and makes him identify which lengths he will refuse to ship.

The factor refuses none.

The order is accepted at a reduced price, but not the ruinous one. Tom tells himself the correction matters. It does. The printer loses less. The Company still receives cloth at a price fixed by men carrying boatswain's coats into dye lanes.

That night Tom takes out the blue square once more.

The uneven flower is no longer merely the reason he stopped.

It is a hand he cannot name.

***

## III. One Monsoon

The water comes through everything.

Rain begins as weather and becomes architecture. It closes the road, fills the compound drains and lays a second roof upon the warehouse roof, louder than work and more persistent. Cloth swells inside bales. Leather turns white at the edges. Powder must be moved twice. Men sleep in wet shirts and wake to find the walls wet too.

Tom's sea chest stands on two bricks.

The first night, water reaches the lower one.

He moves the chest to the warehouse loft.

The second night, wind lifts three roof tiles and rain falls through in straight silver cords. Tom moves the chest beneath a stack of sailcloth.

The third night a factor orders every private chest removed because Company cotton must occupy the dry space.

Tom carries his down.

There are men in the yard trying to raise bales above the flood. Local porters work barefoot in water to the calf. English sailors drag pallets into place. A clerk stands beneath an umbrella and calls the tally, protecting the page before any of the people whose work it records.

Tom puts his chest on the only empty platform.

The factor points at it.

Company goods.

My tools are in there.

Then take out the tools.

Tom opens the chest. Spare shirt. Knife. Needles. A coil of small line. The packet of blue cotton.

The factor sees it.

Private trade?

One piece.

Entered?

It does not require entry.

Everything aboard requires entry.

We are not aboard.

The factor is too wet to enjoy the distinction. He tells Tom to move the chest or lose it.

Tom takes out the tools and the packet. He puts the tools in his belt, the cotton inside his coat and carries the chest into the rain. A bale takes its place before he has stepped off the platform.

By morning the chest has split along one lower seam. The spare shirt is soaked. The small line smells of mud. Tom unwraps the packet.

The oiled paper is damp at one corner.

The blue remains dry.

Ratan sees him checking it beneath the eaves.

Your nobody must be important.

It is only cloth.

Then let it get wet.

Tom wraps it again.

The monsoon spends weeks trying to enter. The packet moves from coat to hammock, hammock to provision chest, provision chest to a hook beneath the driest beam. Tom tells himself he protects it because he paid too much.

This explanation survives exactly as long as the oiled paper.

***

## IV. The Roadstead

By the time the Portuguese ships come off Swally, the cotton has crossed from private object into habit.

Tom checks it when he checks his knife. Moves it when stores move. Feels for the flat packet inside his coat before climbing. He has stopped deciding what it is for. Decision would make it possible to fail.

The English ships lie off the coast with the river behind them and Portuguese sails appearing where sea glare ends. The factors call the fight trade by other means. Sailors call it what comes after the guns begin.

Tom is boatswain now.

The rating belonged to an older man until fever emptied it. Tom inherited the whistle, the responsibility and none of the years that would make the crew forgive his voice. He earns obedience by being right faster than resentment can become mutiny.

Clear the boats.

Wet the decks.

Shot to the guns.

Hands off the bights.

The Portuguese cannon speak first. Water rises beside the hull in white pillars. One ball passes through the upper works and fills the air with splinters fine enough to enter skin like needles. Another strikes low with a sound deeper than impact, timber remembering the tree it was.

Tom moves men where the ship will need them next.

This is his gift and the beginning of his damage: he can look at frightened people and see positions.

The English guns answer.

Smoke removes distance. Ships appear and vanish inside it. Orders travel without their speakers. Tom's whistle is more useful than his voice until soot and powder foul the opening. He puts it between his teeth and tastes metal.

A Portuguese vessel comes close enough for men to become faces.

Hooks strike the rail. Lines tighten. For several minutes the battle loses its nations and becomes hands trying to pull one deck against another. Tom sends axes to the grapnels and men to the forward rail.

The first attacker over is carrying a short boarding pike.

Tom catches the shaft beneath one arm. The iron point passes his eye and opens the eyebrow to bone. Blood enters his sight at once, hot and complete. He does not feel pain. He feels the left half of the deck disappear.

The Portuguese sailor pushes.

Tom turns with the shaft, drives the man against the rail and shouts for an axe. Somebody cuts the grapnel line instead. The ships fall apart by yards. The sailor loses his footing and goes backward between them, still holding the pike.

Tom keeps the bloodied length of wood until it tears from his hand.

The packet inside his coat presses against his ribs.

The fight continues without granting him time to inspect either wound.

Smoke. Guns. Rigging cut and renewed. A man with no lower jaw trying to stand. A powder boy carrying charges with both hands and his eyes shut. Orders that save six men and expose three others because a ship cannot be defended everywhere at once.

When the Portuguese finally draw away, victory looks like work unfinished.

Tom sits while the surgeon closes the eyebrow with three stitches. Blood has run down his face, neck and inside his coat. The surgeon cuts the shirt collar to reach the wound.

What's that? he asks when the knife touches oilcloth.

Mine.

The surgeon has seen men protect tobacco, dice, hair, teeth and letters written by women who no longer remember them.

Keep pressure on the dressing, he says.

Tom waits until darkness before opening the packet.

Blood marks the outside.

The blue cotton remains dry.

***

## V. Four Months

The voyage home takes four months around the Cape.

Tom measures it in watches and inspections. The scar closes badly, a jagged white line through the eyebrow. Salt finds it whenever spray reaches his face. Men begin reading the mark as proof of the victory before the wound has stopped waking him.

The Company measures the passage in cargo retained.

Cloth, pepper, letters, reports. The news from Swally is wrapped more carefully than any private parcel because victory loses value if another ship carries it first. Factors have already written what the fight means: Portuguese authority broken, English trade secured, opportunity widened.

Tom remembers the man falling between the hulls with both hands on the pike.

No report needs him.

The blue cotton moves into a new wrapping. Sailcloth outside, oiled paper within. Tom stitches the edge himself. The scar pulls when he looks down.

A sailor asks whether he has begun making flags.

No.

What is it?

Table cloth.

This answer arrives without warning.

For what table?

Tom sees tar cut directly on Maggie's counter, tankards leaving rings, Arthur's weather book open beside spilled ale, Bell dying on the centre table because no clean surface existed for the work. There is no table in the Pelican fit for blue cotton.

One that doesn't have men cutting tar on it.

The sailor accepts this as homesickness and leaves him alone.

Tom is not sure it is homesickness. Home has become several incompatible places: his mother's two rooms, his father's grave, the Company deck, Maggie's bar. The cloth belongs nowhere because Tom does.

At the Cape, rain enters the boat carrying stores. Tom lifts the packet beneath his shirt while two water casks are rolled aboard. North of the equator, damp blooms through the sea chest again. He sleeps with the cotton beneath his head until the planks dry. In the Channel, winter condensation runs down the beams and he moves it once more.

Four months of protecting a square of cloth from the same water keeping the ship alive.

When England rises grey ahead, the packet is dry.

Tom is not.

***

## VI. The Changed Room

Nine years is a long time on the river. In the ledgers of the East India Company it is an eternity.

By the winter of 1612 the Pelican has changed without anyone quite watching it happen. New flagstones near the hearth. The rushes swept away for good. A longer oak bar, to hold the swelling tide of seamen and dockers. The Company that hired boys out of this room for a desperate venture is a power now, trading in silk, indigo and blood. News of Swally has come upriver ahead of the fleet, and the Portuguese grip east of the Cape has begun, audibly, to crack.

While the Company grows, Wapping pays the toll.

Elias, the old boatswain whose word was once law, gave the sea forty years and the quay whatever was left, and was buried three winters past. Barnaby kept his promise: Wapping churchyard by the Whitsun of 1604, and his stool by the fire has stood empty ever since, by a treaty nobody ever said out loud. Arthur no longer drinks in the Pelican. Within a week of Matthew Bell's murder he accepted a copying place in Harcourt's house, meaning at first to learn what Bell had learned and do something cleaner with it. The Company discovered what an exact man was worth. Now he has a desk in Leadenhall Street, where he drafts the exact species of paper that traps poor boys in endless debt — timelines, penalties, wage assignments, exact to the penny. A murder opened the place. Nine years later Arthur is still filling it. Only Josiah remains precisely as he was: at the end of the bar, still complaining about the Portuguese and the Dutch, a fixture the room has long stopped hearing.

Maggie stands behind the counter, slower on her feet than she was a decade back, wiping the wood with a rag. Beneath the bar the hidden crowd of pale notches has multiplied — dozens of cuts now, one for every boy who signed a paper and never came home. Matthew Bell's folded account remains behind them, his full name on the outside, Tom's inherited debt buried inside.

There is a meat pie in the oven behind the bar — mutton and mace, the way one particular boy liked it. Word came up the river this morning that the Company fleet is in and paying off. Maggie has told herself the pie is for the house. The house knows better than to touch it.

***

## VII. The Boatswain

The heavy oak door opens, bringing in the sharp chill of the Thames.

The man who walks in moves with the heavy, rolling balance of a deep-water sailor. Thirty-one, his face weathered to old leather by the Indian sun, a jagged white scar through one eyebrow. He wears a heavy boatswain's coat.

Maggie stops wiping the bar. She looks at the scarred man, searching the hardened face for a naive twenty-two-year-old who once stood between a lynch mob and a terrified girl.

Tom, she says. Her voice is perfectly flat. Her hands grip the rag tight enough to whiten the knuckles.

Maggie, Tom says. His voice has dropped an octave, scraped raw by years of shouting orders over the ocean.

He does not walk to the bar. He crosses the new flagstones and sits down, without asking, on the stool nearest the fire. Barnaby's stool. The room lets him, and both of those things mean something.

He sets a square parcel on the bar. The oilcloth is salt-stiff and darkened at one corner by old blood. Inside, the blue cotton is dry, its red climbing flower uneven where the printer's hand set one block heavier than the next.

Maggie touches the uneven flower with one finger.

Table cloth, Tom says.

For which table?

One that doesn't have men cutting tar on it.

Then you have brought it to the wrong house.

She folds it again, more carefully than he did, and puts it beneath the counter. Neither says thank you. They have both understood.

You survived, Maggie says, coming down the length of the counter.

The Company pays me to survive now, Tom says, touching the boatswain's whistle at his neck. I keep the rigging tight and the men moving. Just like my father did.

And Pell? Her eyes go to the doorway behind him, as if the quiet sailor might yet be shaking out his coat. Did he come through the Portuguese guns?

Tom looks at the fire. The flames stand in his hardened eyes and find nothing to catch. Pell shipped out on the Ascension, four years back. They never saw battle. The scurvy got him before the Cape. His jaw tightens. They put him over the side, and the purser entered him in the ledger as cargo. Just like he said they would.

Maggie closes her eyes, one brief second. Another notch under the bar.

And your father?

Tom takes the boatswain's whistle from his neck and lays it on the counter. Not his own, Maggie sees now. The silver is worn thin on one side where another man's thumb rubbed it for years.

Dead these four winters. The knee went black at the end. Harcourt sent a physician and put the fee on the account.

Of course he did.

My mother buried him with the old whistle. Tom turns the one on the counter. This was the spare in his chest. He made me promise I'd use it better.

Have you?

Most days.

Maggie goes to the oven and brings out the meat pie. She sets it in front of him hard enough to make the crust settle, then puts down a knife and no fork, because the boy she remembers used his hands. Tom looks at the pie and then at her.

For the house? he asks.

The house has eaten.

He cuts it. Steam carrying mutton and mace reaches him and takes nine years out of his face. The first mouthful is too hot. He eats it anyway.

While he eats, Maggie notices his hands: rope-burn scars, a crushed nail grown crooked, two pale circles where fever sores healed. He notices hers: the swollen joint of the thumb, the way she braces one palm against the counter before lifting a full pitcher. Neither comments. Affection in this room has always preferred an inventory to a declaration.

The upper hinge of the storm door complains when a gust worries it. Tom hears it, looks up and stops eating.

Been doing that long?

Only when the wind blows.

So every day.

He finds the tool box beneath the stairs without asking where she moved it, because she did not move it. The pin in the hinge is too small. Tom draws it, wraps it once in a shaving of oak and drives it home with Maggie's bung mallet while she holds the door square. They work without speaking, each knowing when the other needs the weight shifted. Nine years ago he stood against this door while Silas Rook tried to make the room a gallows. Now he repairs the same iron with his father's whistle against his chest.

When the hinge sits true, Maggie opens and closes the door once.

Better, she says.

It is the nearest she comes to telling him what she thinks of the boatswain's coat.

***

## VIII. The Boy in the Thin Coat

An hour later the repaired door opens again. A boy steps inside, shaking freezing rain from a thin coat. Sixteen at the most, shivering, looking around the Pelican with wide, terrified eyes. He looks exactly the way Tom looked nine years ago, except that Tom at twenty-two had a coastwise sailor's hands and the stubborn confidence of a man who had already met weather. This boy has a cooper's cuts across his knuckles and the soft balance of somebody who has never stood on a moving deck.

Tom stands up from the fire and gestures to the empty stool beside him. Come sit by the hearth, Jack. Warm your bones.

Maggie frowns, looking from the scarred boatswain to the shivering boy. Who is this, Tom?

Jack looks at Tom before he answers, which tells Maggie almost everything.

Jack, he says.

Only Jack?

Jack Mercer. My father works the seasoning sheds at Carter's yard.

Worked, Tom corrects gently. Until the accident.

Jack's face closes. The accident was six months ago and has already been reduced, in Carter's books, to a date beside a loss. A stack of green timber shifted when a sling parted. Jack's father lived, which meant the yard had somebody to charge for the pieces that went into the tide. He can lift a spoon with his left hand now. Nothing heavier.

Mercer, Maggie says. Henry's people?

His brother's son, Jack says.

Maggie's rag stops on the counter. Nine years the cooper has sat at the far end of this room — one of the two tar men who lifted a hanging rope once, and set it down in time. Now the paper has reached into his brother's rooms and picked out the boy. The room's own blood, walked in with a Company berth already half around him. She says none of it aloud. The look she gives Tom says the arithmetic instead.

Tom signals for two pints. Maggie sets one before Jack and puts water in the other place.

He is sixteen, Tom says.

Ale won't make him older.

Jack takes the water and drinks it because he is thirsty enough not to defend himself.

Tom has spent the walk from Carter's yard telling him what a boatswain needs. Men who can learn. Men who obey the first order because at sea the second order may arrive after the mast has killed somebody. Jack has repeated this to himself until obedience sounds like a skill rather than the absence of one.

Show her the knot, Tom says.

From his pocket Jack brings a short length of tarred line. He makes a bowline slowly, tongue against one tooth, then another faster. The second is neat and holds when Tom puts his weight through it.

Learned this morning, Jack says.

He learned three knots, Tom says. Quicker than half the men the Company gives me.

Maggie examines the bowline. Who told you to come here?

Tom did.

Who told you to sign?

Jack looks toward the fire. Nobody had to tell me. There's six of us in two rooms. My father's account is four pounds and growing. Carter's steward says they will take the bed if there is nothing against it by month's end.

And you want the sea?

The question surprises him. Adults have asked whether he will sign, whether he is sound, whether his teeth are firm, whether he understands that advances may be retained. Nobody has asked what he wants.

I want the wages, he says first.

That was not my question.

Jack turns the line round his fingers. I want to see it. The other side. Tom says the water at Bantam is green enough to see the anchor go down.

It is, Tom says.

And he says a man can come back boatswain if he learns.

Tom looks at his father's whistle. Maggie looks at Tom.

Can you swim? she asks Jack.

No.

Most sailors can't, Tom says. It only makes the dying take longer.

Jack laughs because Tom does. Maggie does not.

## IX. With or Without Me

Carter comes in while Jack is practising a rolling hitch on the leg of the table. The chandler has grown prosperous without growing quiet. His coat is good broadcloth now. Tar no longer lives beneath his nails; three other men carry it for him. He sees Jack, then Tom, then Maggie's face, and wishes with visible force that he had chosen another door.

The boy is not mine, Carter says before anybody accuses him.

No, Maggie says. Only the debt.

His father spoiled twelve lengths of Riga pine.

His father was under them when they fell.

And was paid through the quarter while he healed. Carter removes his gloves one finger at a time. I have a yard to keep. If I forgive every loss because the man beneath it was hurt, I will have no yard and none of them will have wages.

There it is, Maggie says.

There what is?

The room inside you where grief becomes use.

Carter does not yet know how many forms that room can take. He only knows she has found something inside him and dislikes being found.

Tom steps between them, not physically — he learned long ago that standing between is sometimes the easy part — but with the calm voice he uses when two watches are close to knives.

Jack goes to the City tomorrow to sign for the Company's next fleet. I told him I would find him a berth on my crew. His wages go against the yard account until it is clean.

All of them? Maggie asks.

Half. Same as mine were.

Were?

Tom's eyes flick down. The original eleven pounds is nearly paid.

Nearly.

There were additions.

Physician's fee, she says.

And rent advanced while I was away. Burial. Interest when a voyage ran beyond the year.

Nine years at half wages and Harcourt still owns a piece of the month.

Not for much longer.

Maggie points at Jack. Put his father's four pounds against your half.

Tom stares at her.

If you are keeping him alive, she says, buy the chance with your own wages.

That is not how the books work.

Books are made by hands.

I have my father's account.

And Jack has his. That is very convenient for every man whose wages remain his own.

Jack has gone still. The knot lies unfinished between his hands.

Tom lowers his voice. He will sign with or without me. On another deck he gets a boatswain who takes his advance, sells his rations and beats him for not knowing work nobody taught him. On mine I can keep him alive.

There is always a *with or without me*, Maggie says. It is how the man holding the paper makes the man holding the boy believe there was never a choice.

This is not Harcourt.

No. Harcourt knew what he was doing.

The words land harder because she does not raise her voice.

Tom turns away, then back. You think I should leave him to whichever ship takes him?

I think you should stop calling the taking a rescue.

Pell would have lived with a better boatswain.

Pell had Lancaster.

Pell had scurvy.

And Jack will have fever, flux, rotten water, Portuguese guns and whatever order comes down from a merchant who has never tied the knot in his hands. Which of those are you?

The man who can teach him when to duck.

Then teach him. Pay him. Bring him home. But do not take half his wage for Carter and name yourself kind because another man might take all of it.

Carter puts both gloves on the table. The debt is lawful.

So was the woman in Bell's book.

That silences him.

Jack looks from one adult to the next, seeing perhaps for the first time that the arrangement presented to him as weather was made by people in rooms. He pushes the unfinished knot away.

I asked Tom for the berth, he says.

Maggie's face softens, which is not the same as yielding. I know.

My father needs it.

I know that too.

Then why are you angry with him?

Because he knows all of this and has learned to speak as though he does not.

The silence that falls over the bar is absolute.

Maggie stares at Tom. At the whistle. At the scar. At the competence she was proud of an hour ago and the use he has chosen for it. To survive the Company he has become part of its machinery. That does not make every thing he does wicked; it makes every wicked thing easier to describe as necessary.

Maggie does not pour the ale.

She reaches under the bar, into the dark gap beside the pale notches, and brings out a plain brass thimble. She walks around the counter, and drops it on the flagstones at his feet.

You're not a sailor anymore, Tom, she says, and her voice shakes with a sudden, profound disgust. You're Master Harcourt. You're Silas Rook. You're the room telling itself the next death was already decided. Another big man using paper to send somebody else's boy where you would not send your own.

I haven't got a boy, Tom says. It comes out small, and human, and too late.

No, Maggie says. The sea made certain of that too.

Tom looks down at his mother's thimble shining in the firelight. He does not pick it up.

The door is behind you, Maggie says, turning her back on him. Don't come back until you remember what a human life is worth.

The door opens. The door closes. The cold moves through the room and settles.

Carter collects his gloves.

What happens to the yard account? Maggie asks.

The chandler looks at Jack, and for a moment the man who put a belaying pin beneath Silas Rook's boot stands inside the merchant's expensive coat.

If the boy signs, half wages until it is discharged.

And if he does not?

Carter's answer takes too long.

The steward will proceed against the rooms.

Maggie nods. Not approval. Entry.

## X. The Meat Pie

Jack is still standing near the threshold, dripping, too frightened to sit. Maggie looks at him for a long moment. Then she goes to the oven and brings out the meat pie she has been telling herself all day is for the house, and sets it on the bar with a pint beside it, and nods the boy toward both. Eat, she says. Not a suggestion. The boy will sign in the morning whatever anyone in this room does tonight; the paper has him, the way papers do. But tonight he will eat like a guest in this house, not like cargo.

The pie has a quarter missing where Tom ate. Jack notices and understands who it was baked for. He sits anyway.

Maggie cuts him a piece large enough for a working man and watches until he takes the first mouthful.

Is he a bad man? Jack asks.

Tom?

Jack nods.

No.

You called him Rook.

Bad men seldom disappoint you, Maggie says. They arrive complete. Eat.

Jack eats. Halfway through, his shoulders come down from his ears.

Maggie takes the tarred line from the table and undoes the rolling hitch he left unfinished. She does not know the knot. She knows what unfinished work looks like. When Jack has cleaned the plate with bread she gives the line back.

Tom will be at the hiring hall tomorrow, she says. If you still mean to go, make him show you the articles before you put your name under them. Ask what is held back. Ask who pays if your kit is lost. Ask whether the surgeon's fee comes out of your wage if another man breaks your head. Ask twice when the first answer has no number in it.

Will it change anything?

Perhaps not.

Then why ask?

So the clerk has to hear himself answer.

She finds a scrap of paper and has Arthur's successor at the end of the bar write the questions because Jack can recognize his name and little else. The clerk writes reluctantly. Men who make their living from paper dislike being asked to make paper troublesome.

Jack folds the questions into his coat.

At the door he turns. If Tom takes me, will you put a mark under there?

Maggie does not look toward the hidden notches.

Only if you do not come home.

Then don't cut one yet.

I never do.

After he has gone, she stoops, picks the thimble off the flagstones, and puts it back in the gap behind the old wood, in among the notches, where she keeps what the sea owes her.

Outside, Tom is standing beneath the eaves across the lane. He sees Jack emerge and does not call to him. Jack sees Tom and, after a moment, crosses the mud of his own accord. They walk toward Ratcliff together, the boy half a step behind until Tom slows enough to put them side by side.

## XI. The Articles

The Company's hiring room is full before daylight. Boys, men, wives, creditors, two surgeons and a row of clerks behind a rail. Wet coats steam in the press. Every person in the room has come carrying a different necessity, and the clerks mean to turn all of them into the same form before dinner.

Jack stands in line with Maggie's questions inside his coat. Tom waits beside him in the boatswain's coat and discovers that rank is a kind of door: the men at the rail call him sir, though nobody aboard will, and boys in the line look at Jack as if he has arrived with the answer.

At the third desk sits Arthur.

Nine years have improved his coat and drawn the rest of him narrow. No weather book. No wagers. His fingers carry ink in the same permanent bruise Bell wore. Tom has not seen him since Arthur went back upstairs to take the vacant chair.

Arthur sees the scar through Tom's eyebrow, the whistle, the boy at his side. His eyes move once toward the folded questions showing from Jack's coat.

Name? he asks.

Jack Mercer.

Age?

Sixteen.

Arthur looks at Tom.

Sound enough for foremast work, Tom says. Learns quickly.

Arthur begins the entry. Advance to the household account of the father, four pounds and—

How much is the kit? Jack asks.

The pen stops.

Reasonable cost as supplied, Arthur says.

That is not a number.

The line behind Jack becomes attentive.

Arthur looks at the paper in the boy's hand. Who wrote the questions?

A man in the Pelican.

Which man?

Did not ask his name.

This answer pleases Maggie at a distance.

Arthur sets down the pen. Cost varies by need. A man who owns a sea chest is not charged a sea chest. A man requiring shoes is charged shoes.

At whose price?

The purser's.

Can the purser charge anything?

No.

What can't he charge?

Arthur has copied the clause a thousand times. For the first time somebody has asked him to locate its edge.

The clerk at the next desk tells Jack to make his mark and clear the rail. Arthur ignores him.

Tom, he says, you brought the boy.

I brought him to read the paper.

Yesterday you brought him to sign it.

Yesterday was longer than I expected.

The line behind them shifts. A woman holding a toddler asks the clerk at the next desk what burial costs are recovered if her husband dies before wages accrue. The clerk does not know. A tarred man at the back asks whether a lost knife is valued new or used. Necessity has begun asking questions in several voices. The room will not finish by dinner.

Arthur draws the articles back. He adds a ceiling to the kit charge: twelve shillings unless Jack separately marks an additional item at issue. He writes that replacement goods are to be valued at their condition when lost, not at the cost of new stores. These are small alterations. They do not make the voyage safe or the debt just. They make two empty spaces less hungry.

Harcourt's steward, waiting behind the rail for the assignment, leans over Arthur's shoulder.

You have no authority to vary the master's recovery.

I have authority to state what the Company will deduct before it pays the assigned half. Your master may sue the boy's injured father for anything beyond it.

With what result?

Arthur looks at the steward. A public hearing of the account.

The steward straightens. Four pounds will suffice.

Tom hears Maggie in the silence after that: books are made by hands.

Two shillings from my monthly wage against the same account, he says.

Jack turns. No.

Instruction allowance, Tom says to Arthur. My expense for training an unready hand. Credit it to Carter until the four pounds close, then pay it to the boy.

You don't owe it, Jack says.

No. That is why it can be given.

Arthur writes the allowance. The ink turns a late thought into a term other clerks must obey, at least until somebody higher notices. He has used Bell's chair for nine years. This is not redemption. It is one line leaning the other way.

Jack makes his mark. Then, embarrassed by the shape of it, asks Arthur to write his whole name beside it. Arthur does, slowly, and reads every altered clause aloud.

At the door Jack asks Tom whether they are going back to the Pelican.

You can.

Are you?

Tom looks west. The walk is not far. The distance is exactly one sentence spoken by a woman who knows him.

No.

Why?

Because changing one paper is easier than admitting why it needed changing.

Jack understands none of this. He is sixteen and has a ship to see. They turn east together.

Fourteen years will pass before Tom comes through that door again.

# The Boy in the Rigging (1613)

> **1613**
>
> *“He kept me alive once with his hand and every day with his voice.”*
>
> — Jack Mercer, Deptford, 1626

***

## I. The River Lets Go

Jack's first work aboard is to carry his own debt down a ladder.

The sea chest costs nine shillings under the limit Arthur wrote into the articles. Inside are two shirts, one pair of stockings, a wooden bowl, a spoon, a blanket that smells of another man's sleep and a knife valued as new though its handle has been cut with somebody else's initials. The purser points to each item while a clerk enters it. Jack keeps Maggie's questions folded inside his coat and asks the price twice.

The purser looks at Tom.

Answer him, Tom says.

The second price is eightpence lower than the first.

Jack does not feel victorious. He feels the ship noticing him.

Men notice a boy who arrives beside the boatswain. They notice more when the boatswain makes the purser account for a spoon. By the time Jack has dragged his chest below, he is Tom's pet, Tom's clerk and Tom's little wife, according to three men who have not asked his name. One steals the blanket before dusk. Jack finds it beneath a gunner's mate and pulls until the man opens one eye.

Go ask your father for another.

Tom is six feet away, inspecting a frayed stay. He hears. Jack knows he hears because nothing said on deck escapes him. Tom does not turn.

Jack pulls again. The mate sits up and gives him the blanket with a smile that promises the matter is not finished.

Later, when the watch changes, Tom finds Jack trying to tie his chest shut with the bowline he learned in Wapping.

Wrong knot.

It holds.

So does a locked door. Can you open it under load?

Jack cannot.

Tom shows him a slipped hitch and makes him tie it until his fingers find the turn in darkness. He does not mention the blanket.

You heard him, Jack says.

Yes.

You could have made him give it back.

Yes.

Why didn't you?

Because tomorrow I may be on the other end of the ship.

Jack pulls the hitch free. He understands the lesson and hates the man for making it true.

The vessel drops downriver with the morning tide. Wapping passes as wet roofs, smoke and a confusion of stairs. Jack stands at the waist searching for one sign among a hundred. He cannot see the Pelican. He sees Carter's yard, or thinks he does: timber stacked behind the seasoning sheds, cranes black against the low sky, the place where his father lies in two rooms with a hand that can lift a spoon.

Four pounds. Two shillings from Tom every month. Half Jack's wage until the account is clean.

Numbers behave better ashore. On the moving deck they refuse to stand still.

At Gravesend a boat brings the last letters. None is for Jack. Tom receives a folded page bearing the Company's seal, reads it once and puts it inside his coat.

From Maggie? Jack asks.

No.

Did you tell her we sailed?

She knows what ships do.

The river widens. England lowers behind them. Jack waits for the moment the sea begins and cannot find it. Brown water becomes grey, grey becomes green, and the land is simply smaller each time he looks.

***

## II. Above the Deck

The first time Tom sends Jack aloft, the ship is barely clear of the Downs.

The order is to loose the foretopsail. Jack knows every word separately. Together they mean climbing into a wind that has already taken his cap.

Up, Tom says.

Jack puts one foot on the shrouds.

Not the ratline with your hands. The shroud. Ratline parts, you fall. Shroud parts, we all have larger trouble.

Jack changes his grip.

Up.

The word follows him. It is not encouragement. Tom has a voice made to cross weather, low in ordinary speech and enormous when lifted. It finds Jack through canvas cracking, blocks knocking and forty men pretending not to watch the boatswain's boy discover height.

At the futtock shrouds the climb turns outward. The deck is no longer a place but a shape below him, narrow and unreasonable. Water moves on both sides. Jack locks both arms and stops.

Keep moving, Tom calls.

I can't.

Nobody on deck laughs. Tom's silence prevents it.

Jack, look at the mast.

He presses his face to tarred rope.

Not down. Not out. One hand. One foot. Then the others.

Jack moves one hand.

Again.

One foot.

Again.

The voice builds a ladder where the ratlines have failed. Jack reaches the top, crawls over and lies against the platform with his eyes shut.

No sleeping, Tom calls. Loose the sail.

A man beside Jack shows him where the gasket lies. Jack's fingers are too stiff to free it. The man reaches across, then stops when Tom shouts from below.

Let him find it.

Jack finds it.

The sail opens with a violence no cloth should possess. It bellies, strikes him with its edge and nearly tears the line from his hands. He swears. The men laugh now, because the danger has become ordinary enough to permit it.

Coming down is worse. Gravity knows which country it belongs to.

At the deck Tom examines Jack's hands. Tar in the palms. One split nail. No rope burn deep enough to cripple him.

Again at the afternoon watch, Tom says.

Jack thinks this is cruelty until the second climb frightens him less. By the fifth, his feet begin to know where the ship will be before the roll arrives. By the tenth, another new boy watches him for the route.

Tom does not praise him. Praise makes a sailor listen for praise when he should be listening for wind.

He corrects everything.

Your thumb outside the coil.

Never stand inside the bight.

Feel the line before you look at it.

Tie it again.

Again.

Again.

Jack hears Tom in his sleep. He hears him while eating, while pissing over the lee rail, while hauling until the skin opens beneath his fingers. On the seventh day he turns at another man's shout because all voices have begun to sound like orders.

On the ninth, he makes his first dangerous mistake.

A buntline jams while the foretopsail is being taken in. Jack climbs to clear it, folds himself across the yard and puts his weight on the jammed line where it has worn pale against the block. Tom sees the colour from the deck.

Off that line! he roars.

Jack freezes.

Move!

The urgency fixes him harder. Tom is up the shrouds before Jack understands he has begun climbing. He comes over the top, works himself astride the yard and strikes Jack once across the shoulder with an open hand hard enough to break the grip fear has made.

Jack folds across the spar. Tom catches his coat, drags him inward and wraps Jack's arms round the yard.

The pale buntline parts on the next roll.

One end whips away into empty air.

Tom's face is close to Jack's. Not angry. Worse: afraid, and already turning the fear into instruction.

When I say move, you move.

You hit me.

Yes.

You could have said the rope was bad.

I did not have time to improve the order.

Below them the sea passes, green-black and without opinion.

Tom makes Jack climb down first. On deck he puts the severed rope into the bosun's store and enters it against ordinary wear. He does not charge it to the boy who stood on it.

***

## III. The Voice

There are kinder men aboard than Tom.

The cook gives Jack the bottom of the porridge pot, where the oats have caught and taste almost roasted. The old quartermaster teaches him to judge speed from the bubbles passing the hull. The sailmaker lets him sleep in the loft one wet afternoon while pretending not to see him.

None of them keeps him alive as often.

Tom's voice stops Jack before a block drops where his head is going. It sends him across the deck before green water comes over the waist. It tells him when to flatten, when to climb, when the familiar line in his hand has become the wrong line because darkness and panic have altered everything around it.

The voice is not kept for Jack. It works the entire ship.

Men hate it, obey it and repeat it in smaller voices down the watches. Tom sees the flaw in a knot from twenty feet. He smells loose wet hemp before it parts. He can wake from the deck beneath a coat and know by the changed pitch of canvas that a sheet is running.

He can also make a grown man feel six years old for coiling against the lay.

When a sailor named Pritchard drops a marline spike from aloft and misses Jack by the width of one hand, Tom has Pritchard at the mast before the spike stops rolling. He does not strike him. He makes him stand the boy's next two watches and carry Jack's work as well as his own.

Pritchard takes the punishment out on Jack in words.

Boatswain's little debt, he calls him. Two shillings buys a loud father.

Jack has not told anyone about the allowance.

He finds the purser's book open that evening while stores are issued. Beside Thomas Fletcher is the monthly deduction, entered exactly as Arthur wrote it. Beside Jack's name is the assigned half wage. The whole ship knows because books have no privacy from the men who keep them.

Jack waits until the night watch.

I told you not to pay it, he says.

Tom is splicing a length of line beneath the stern lantern.

I heard you.

They call me bought.

Men aboard call the moon a whore when it gives poor light. The moon keeps its account.

I'm not the moon.

No.

Then stop it.

Tom draws the splice tight. I can stop one mouth. The others will learn to whisper. Which do you prefer?

Jack has no answer that does not sound like surrender.

Tom gives him the line.

Find the join.

Jack runs both hands along it and cannot.

That is what good work looks like, Tom says. Not the man who paid for the rope.

It is almost comfort. Tom ruins it by making Jack splice the next length three times.

Most of the voyage is not weather or punishment. It is one watch followed by another, each made from small discomforts that nobody will remember if the ship arrives.

Jack wakes for the forenoon watch already wet.

The damp has come through the deck above, gathered on a beam and fallen steadily onto his chest. His shirt holds a dark patch from collar to belly. The hammock sags beneath him, one shoulder pressed against the ship's side and one knee against the sleeping man beside him. On the other side a pair of bare feet rests level with Jack's face. Their owner has wedged himself between two guns and snores whenever the hull rolls to larboard.

Jack tries to sit. The hammock folds him in half.

Tom strikes the sole of his shoe.

Watch.

I'm awake.

Then begin resembling it.

There is nowhere to dress except where he has slept. Jack works damp arms into his coat while the next hammock brushes his hair. Somebody has taken his left stocking from the line and left a strip of oakum in its place. He finds the stocking under Pritchard's head. Pritchard does not wake while Jack pulls it free, though his smile suggests a complicated kind of sleep.

The way forward is blocked by men leaving the morning watch and men rising for the day, all of them with the same needs and none with room to meet them. At the beakhead Jack waits behind six sailors while the sea appears and vanishes through the rails. Privacy consists of facing outward. The man ahead finishes, ties his breeches and warns Jack to hold the stay when the bow drops.

Jack thinks this advice insulting until the bow drops.

He returns with both shoes wet and one hand smelling of tar. A sailor at the pump tips a bucket of seawater across the deck and catches Jack to the ankles.

Now the rest matches, the sailor says.

Jack scoops a double handful from the scupper and throws it back. The ship rolls. The water misses the sailor and strikes Pritchard in the thigh.

Pritchard looks down at the spreading mark.

That boy's aim will make a gunner.

It is the first joke of the watch, and because Jack has helped make it, he is allowed to laugh.

The ration issue begins beside the main hatch. Each mess brings its wooden tally and whatever cups it has not lost. Biscuit is tipped from a cask onto sailcloth. Some pieces are whole, some split, some reduced at the edges to meal and droppings. The purser's assistant weighs the share for each mess, calls the number of mouths and makes a mark in the book.

When Jack first comes aboard, he thinks a ration belongs to the man who eats it. After a month he understands that it belongs first to the page. A man may be standing at the cask with an empty hand, but if the page says sick, absent, punished or dead, the biscuit can pass through him without stopping.

Tom gives Jack their mess tally.

How many?

Jack counts the men. Pritchard; the two at the pump; the lookout not yet relieved; Tom; himself; five more waiting with cups or still working.

Eleven.

Again.

Jack counts again. Eleven.

The assistant calls ten.

Eleven, Jack says.

The assistant does not look up. Ten on the mess.

Tom takes the tally, turns it over and puts one black thumbnail against a cut near the end.

That is eleven.

That is the boy.

I have observed him.

Half wage, the assistant says. It is entered.

Jack feels every man in the line hear it. Pritchard's name for him returns before Pritchard can speak: boatswain's little debt. Two shillings buys a loud father.

Tom's voice remains level.

The wage book pays Carter. The victual book feeds Jack. Unless the Company has found a way for half a boy to do a whole watch, weigh eleven.

The assistant looks toward the purser, but the purser is occupied arguing over a cheese that has become liquid inside its rind. He adds the biscuit.

Jack carries the share away.

Does half wage mean half everything? he asks when they are beyond the line.

It means half your wage.

And the two shillings?

Mine.

The food?

The ship's expense. It must keep alive what it intends to use.

Jack turns this over. It sounds less like kindness than arithmetic, which makes it easier to accept.

Their beer comes next. The first casks were small and good enough to make men sing after the evening issue. This cask gives a brown, sour drink with threads that cling to the cup. The assistant calls it beer because that is what the book calls it. Pritchard calls it rope with ambitions.

One of the pump men lifts a thread on his finger.

Splice it, Tom.

Too weak, Tom says.

The men laugh. Jack laughs late because he is surprised Tom has made the joke.

Water is worse in a quieter way. The cask smells of wet cellar when the bung comes free. The first dipper carries black specks. The second carries a pale shaving of wood and something soft that nobody examines. Men complain, but they hold out their cups. The good water is already behind them in the river mouths of England. This water has crossed every mile they have.

The cook takes his share to the galley and sets barley in it before the taste can become an argument. He salts the pot lightly and keeps the broth thin enough to pour. Jack crumbles his biscuit into a cup and waits while the pieces surrender. Hard biscuit breaks teeth; softened biscuit reveals inhabitants. Three small beetles rise to the surface.

Pritchard leans over his shoulder.

You have more meat than the rest of us.

Jack lifts one beetle with the spoon and drops it into Pritchard's cup.

Half wages, he says. Full meat.

The two pump men laugh first. Then the lookout, climbing down hungry from the mast. Pritchard looks at the beetle, looks at Jack, and drinks without removing it.

Waste nothing, he says.

That is the next joke of the watch. This time Jack knows in time that he belongs inside it.

Tom makes him count the water measures before he eats. Ten are drawn. The eleventh comes up short because the cask gives only a wet knock when the dipper reaches the bottom.

Put the shortage in the book, Tom tells the assistant.

The cask is empty.

That is the shortage.

It issued full when opened.

It does not issue full now.

The assistant says evaporation, leakage and bad cooperage as though three explanations add up to one measure of water. Tom waits. Waiting is the quiet form of his voice. At last the assistant writes beside the issue.

Jack watches the pen.

What happens if he doesn't write it?

At the next reckoning, the book has more water than the hold. The book wins until men become too thirsty to agree.

And if he writes it?

Then the cask loses the argument.

Tom tips half his cup into Jack's.

I have mine.

No, you have counted yours. Drink.

Jack drinks. The water tastes of the cask, the dipper and Tom's refusal to discuss what he has done.

The watch works forward from the ration line. First the pumps. Jack takes one handle with Pritchard opposite him. The pump wheezes, catches and brings bilge water up in brown surges that smell worse than the cask water and are easier to trust. Nobody has entered this water as drink. Nobody asks it to be anything but foul.

They pump until the carpenter calls the well low. Then buckets come over the side and the deck is flooded. Men scatter sand. Others push flat stones back and forth on their knees, scouring grease, blood, spilled broth and the black tracks of bare feet from the planks. Jack works a stone no larger than his palm. The sun climbs. His wet shirt dries stiff with salt while his knees become wet instead.

Pritchard crawls past and brushes Jack's back. Men begin smiling at nothing.

Jack keeps scrubbing. A twist of oakum swings behind him from the band of his breeches, tarred at the end like an animal's tail. He sees its shadow each time the sun clears the sail. He lets it remain.

The old quartermaster asks whether London boys grow that way or only shipboard ones.

Only the handsome ones, Jack says.

The tail survives another length of deck. Then Jack crawls beneath a wet line, catches it in one hand and, while Pritchard reaches for the bucket, tucks it into the back of Pritchard's belt. The exchange passes without anyone naming it. Two minutes later the quartermaster asks Pritchard the same question.

Pritchard looks behind himself and finds the oakum.

The deck waits.

He bows.

Improved by sea air, he says.

Even Tom's mouth alters at one corner.

The laughter does not excuse the dropped marline spike. It does not erase the names or the debt. It only gives Jack a place among men who have too little room, too much work and four hours in which to become tired of one another. Belonging aboard is not being spared. It is being answered.

When the deck is clean, Tom sends Jack to the weather shrouds with a serving mallet, spun yarn and a pot of tar. A length above the deadeyes has begun to chafe where rope works against rope. The damage is no longer than Jack's thumb. Left alone, Tom says, it will become the length of his arm and then the height of the mast.

So replace it.

With what?

New rope.

Where does new rope come from?

The store.

And when the store is empty?

Jack looks east, where the sea has no warehouse in it.

Tom puts the spun yarn in his hand.

Preserve first. Replace when preservation fails.

Jack lays the yarn against the shroud. Tom makes him begin again because the turn is loose, again because he has trapped water beneath it, and once more because the serving runs against the rope's lay. On the fourth attempt the mallet draws each turn close and hard. Tar coats Jack's fingers and works under his nails. The ship rises beneath him in a rhythm his body now anticipates.

Below, the ordinary life of the watch continues. A bucket travels aft. A sail is patched on deck. The cook insults the purser's cheese. Somebody sings three lines of a song and cannot remember the fourth, so the entire waist supplies a different one. The versions disagree about whether the woman in Plymouth owns a tavern, a husband or a knife.

Jack looks down at the serving.

The turns sit together so closely that he cannot see where the last begins.

Find the join, Tom says.

Jack runs one tarred finger along the work. This time he can.

There.

Better.

It is the smallest word Tom owns that can mean praise.

Near noon the heat thickens. The deck seams soften underfoot. Men who complained of cold rain in the Channel now work bare to the waist and curse the sun. Jack's mouth dries before he finishes one coil. He glances toward the water cask, though he knows by the bung lying beside it that there is nothing left until another is brought up.

Tom sees the glance.

Finish the coil. Then shade.

Jack coils with the lay, each circle flat inside the next. Pritchard passes and does not disturb it. This restraint is almost an apology.

The bell ends the watch. The relieving men rise from the dim places below, marked by hammock rope and sleep. They blink at the white deck. Jack looks toward the hatch and thinks of his own hammock, the wet patch perhaps shifted from chest to face, the feet waiting beside it. The space now seems less like punishment than permission to become unconscious.

Food comes first. The cook ladles the last thin barley broth into mess cups. Jack drops in the final piece of his biscuit and presses it down with a spoon. Around him men eat quickly before the next order can claim their hands.

One sailor does not.

The man sits in the full noon sun with both arms crossed over his chest. His biscuit remains untouched beside the cup. Broth soaks halfway through it, leaving the upper edge dry.

Jack points at the biscuit.

If you don't want that, Pritchard will eat it and the beetles.

The sailor looks at him without understanding. His face is grey beneath the heat.

Cold, he says. God help me, boy. I am cold.

Jack looks at the sun. Tom looks at the untouched biscuit.

***

## IV. Nineteen

By evening the sailor is shaking beneath two blankets. By morning four more men cannot stand. The surgeon calls it the flux and orders the sick separated, an instruction for which the vessel has no space. Men sleep between guns, beneath boats, against bulkheads wet with their own breath. Separation is a word belonging to houses with spare rooms.

Tom clears the sail room and slings hammocks close enough that the sick turn together when the ship rolls. He orders vinegar splashed on the boards. He burns foul bedding until the captain objects to Company property going over the side as smoke.

Dead men draw no wage, Tom says. Choose your account.

The captain allows the fire.

Jack carries water. At first this seems easier than going aloft. Then the men begin asking him to stay.

One is nineteen and has a sister in Limehouse. One has a wife who believes he is on a coastwise run to Bordeaux. One took the Company's advance, lost it at dice before sailing and now laughs whenever the fever climbs because he remembers the expression on the clerk's face. Their names are Ellis, Brown, Cooper, Venn. Jack repeats them until sleep rubs the edges off.

The surgeon moves from hammock to hammock with a lancet and a basin. Tom watches him bleed the first three and stops him at the fourth.

It is the accepted treatment, the surgeon says.

They have lost water from both ends for two days.

The fever must be relieved.

Relieve one more into that basin and I will make you drink it.

The surgeon appeals to the captain. The captain looks at the men able to work, counts how many answer to Tom's voice and tells the surgeon to try water first.

Tom has no medicine. He has observation and the memory of every voyage that killed men in the same order. He has the cook keep the thinnest barley broth on the fire, salted lightly and carried warm from hammock to hammock in cups. Some men keep it down. Some do not. Nobody calls this knowledge because no gentleman wrote it in a book.

On the fifth day Jack wakes cold beneath the tropical sun.

Tom sees before Jack speaks. He takes the bucket from Jack's hand and presses two fingers beneath his jaw.

How many stools since dawn?

Jack is too tired to be embarrassed. Three.

Tom puts him in the sail room.

I can work.

No.

You said men obey the first order.

That was the first order.

Fever strips the ship to sound. Timbers complain. Water moves in casks. Men breathe as though hauling something uphill. Jack lies among them and understands for the first time that a vessel can continue while the people inside it stop.

Tom brings the water himself.

Jack tries to sit. Tom puts one hand against his chest and holds him down. The hand that struck him on the yard is careful now, which makes Jack angrier than the blow did.

Don't, Jack says.

Drink.

I don't want your two shillings.

Drink.

I don't want—

Tom tips the cup against his mouth. Jack drinks or drowns in a very small quantity of water. Most of it stays down.

Why me? Jack asks when he can speak.

You were nearest the bucket.

The answer is a lie and a kindness and Tom's refusal to name either one.

Men die around them.

The first is Ellis. The surgeon's mate sews him into his hammock with a round shot at the feet. Tom makes the clerk enter the full name before the body goes over. The clerk says the muster number is sufficient. Tom stands beside the book until the letters are written.

Ellis.

Brown.

Cooper.

Venn.

The list reaches nineteen before the fever releases the ship.

Jack is not on it.

On the first morning he can stand, he climbs to the foretop without being ordered. Halfway up, his legs begin to shake. Tom's voice rises from the deck.

One hand. One foot. Then the others.

Jack does not look down.

***

## V. The Next Weather

The ship changes after nineteen burials.

Hammocks hang farther apart because there are fewer men to fill them. Watches lengthen. Boys do work that belonged to able seamen at the Canaries. Nobody calls Jack Tom's pet now. The word requires an innocence the voyage has used up.

Pritchard gives Jack a strip of leather for his palms and claims it was spare. The cook stops pretending the burnt oats are an accident. The quartermaster lets Jack cast the log and accepts his count without checking it twice.

Tom gives him nothing except more work.

At dusk he takes Jack to the foremast and makes him lay one hand against the wood. The vibration changes as the ship rises. Rigging speaks through the mast: every stay carrying its own degree of strain, every sail pulling at a different height.

What do you hear? Tom asks.

Nothing.

Feel, then.

Jack closes his eyes. Beneath the ordinary shudder is a small repeated knock.

Block at the foretop?

Tom waits.

No. Lower. The brace.

Which brace?

Jack follows the tremor through the mast and points.

Lee fore brace.

Tom nods once.

They renew the worn strop before darkness.

Weeks later the African coast lies somewhere beyond the western horizon. The master speaks of passing Madagascar before the monsoon turns. Heat sits on the deck after sunset. Flying fish strike the hull in the night. Clouds build where the sky has been empty all day.

Jack has learned enough to watch Tom watching them.

Bad weather? he asks.

Weather, Tom says.

That is not an answer.

It is the only one the sea has given.

Tom checks the braces himself before the watch goes below. Jack follows without being told.

The boy who left Wapping could tie three knots and could not swim. The boy in the rigging can feel a worn line through the mast and knows that neither fact makes him safe.

Above them, the first high cloud draws a white hook across the dark.

***

## VI. The White Hook

The white hook in the western sky is gone by morning.

In its place stands a wall.

It is too far away to seem large. A low darkness below, a hard white edge above, the whole shape sitting where sea and sky have spent weeks refusing to admit they are separate. The master studies it through a brass tube and says the weather will pass astern.

Tom looks at the sails.

Jack looks at Tom.

This has become another instrument aboard: the angle of the boatswain's head, the amount of time he spends answering a simple question, whether he inspects a line with his eyes or puts his hand on it. This morning Tom touches everything.

He checks the foremast wedges. He sounds the standing rigging with the handle of his knife. He makes Pritchard climb to the foretop and examine the topmast cap, then goes up after him and examines it again. He orders the fore braces cleared from the pins and recoiled where they can run without catching a man's ankle.

The master watches from the quarterdeck.

You expect trouble, Fletcher?

I expect rope.

We have rope.

Then I expect it to work.

The master does not enjoy the answer. He enjoys less that Tom has already made the men shorten the loose ends of their clothing and move spare spars off the forward deck. Preparedness can look like insubordination to a man who has just predicted fair passage.

By noon the wind falls.

Canvas hangs against the masts. The sea becomes long, dark glass lifting under the hull without breaking. Heat gathers beneath the courses and stays there. Men speak quietly because the absence of wind has taken away the sound that usually hides them from one another.

Jack is sent aloft to stop a block knocking against the foretopmast. He carries a short lashing between his teeth and climbs through air thick enough to drink.

From the foretop he can see Madagascar as a low bruise to the west. It is the first land in weeks, if land is what it is. The coast shows itself only when the ship rises: a dark line, then nothing, then a dark line again.

Below him the vessel is stripped of illusion. Seams, patched canvas, pale scars where fittings have moved under strain. Nineteen fewer men. A ship does not become whole because land is near.

Jack secures the block. Before descending, he looks toward the weather.

The wall has moved.

No longer sitting at the edge of the world, it has eaten half of it. The white upper edge is gone. Beneath the black cloud, the sea has turned the colour of lead freshly cut.

Jack starts down.

Tom calls from the deck. Stay there. What do you see?

Rain.

Where?

Everywhere.

That is not a bearing.

Jack braces one arm round the mast and forces himself to look again.

West by south. Moving north.

The master lowers his tube. Passing astern, he says.

Tom does not answer him. Watch the sail, he calls to Jack.

There is hardly enough wind to shape one.

That makes the small movement easier to see.

Jack watches the foretopsail. The lower edge hangs slack. One corner lifts, falls, then lifts again though the deck beneath it has rolled the other way.

The clew is moving, Jack calls.

Which?

Weather clew.

The master turns toward the binnacle.

The wind has not reached the deck. It has reached the upper canvas.

Tom's voice crosses the ship.

All hands. Shorten sail.

The master says, I have not given that order.

Tom looks at the black wall, then at nineteen empty places where men should be running.

Give it now.

***

## VII. Before the Wind

The order comes late and everybody obeys it quickly.

Men spill out of the waist and up the shrouds. The courses are clewed. Topsail sheets are eased while clewlines and buntlines come in. Canvas that hung dead a moment before begins breathing in short, irregular pulls.

Jack remains at the foretop because Tom told him to watch. Pritchard climbs past and throws him a gasket.

Move, boy.

Tom told me—

Tom wants the sail in before he wants your opinion.

Jack crawls onto the yard. Six men lie across the spar, its timber taking their weight while their arms gather canvas in hot, heavy folds.

The first wind arrives from the wrong direction.

It touches the back of Jack's neck. The ship is still moving on the old air, such as it was, while the new air comes across the quarter. The topsail shivers between the two.

Jack looks down.

Tom stands beside the foremast, one hand on the stay. His face is turned upward.

The sail fills backward.

Not fully. One deep inward breath, enough to put every seam in relief and make the yard kick beneath Jack's chest. The men swear and flatten themselves.

Then it empties.

Jack sees the mast move.

All masts move. Tom taught him this. A rigid mast is already broken. But the foretopmast does not return along the same path. It bends away and comes back with a small second motion at the cap, like a loose tooth tested by a tongue.

Jack shouts.

The wind takes the word.

He shouts again. Foretopmast! At the cap!

Tom has seen it.

Down from the yard! he roars. Lay down! Move!

The men come off the spar. Jack reaches the top and stops because Pritchard is below him on the futtock shrouds.

Tom points.

Backstay. Slide it.

Jack throws one leg over the aft lead of the rigging and descends more by controlled falling than climbing. Tar burns through the leather on his palms. His feet strike the deck.

Tom catches his shoulder, turns him and puts a line into both hands.

Lee fore brace. Take it aft. Do not belay until I tell you.

The line is already under strain.

Why me?

Because you felt it before. Go.

Jack runs aft with the brace, threading between men hauling the fore course up. He reaches the station beyond the mainmast where the old quartermaster and two fever-thinned sailors wait for hands enough to control the yard.

Ten seconds is not a measure anybody makes during danger.

Later Jack will build it from actions.

He takes the brace.

One.

The quartermaster tells him to keep his thumb out of the coil.

Two.

Tom orders the weather brace eased.

Three.

The first rain hits, drops large enough to burst in the dust between the deck planks.

Four.

The foretopsail fills backward again.

Five.

The yard tries to swing. Jack and the quartermaster haul the lee brace to bring it round.

Six.

Somebody forward shouts that the cap is opening.

Seven.

Tom looks up.

Eight.

He takes one step away from the mast.

Nine.

The wind arrives.

Ten.

The foretopmast goes.

***

## VIII. The Going

It does not snap like a stick.

It comes apart as a system.

The cap tears loose first. Iron leaves timber with a scream. The topmast bows forward under the backed sail, carrying yard, canvas, blocks and a confusion of rigging that for one suspended instant still resembles a mast.

Then the whole weight falls toward the bow.

Men run without choosing directions. The sail catches wind on the way down and drags the broken spar sideways. A stay rips through the foretop rail. Blocks strike the deck hard enough to bury their corners. The yard lands across the forward boats and breaks one clean through.

Jack is aft of it because Tom put him on the lee brace.

The brace jumps in his hands.

The quartermaster lets go. One of the other men does not. The line strips skin from his palms and pulls him face-first across the deck. Jack sees the running coil open beside his ankle.

Thumb outside, Tom's voice says inside him.

He steps clear as the line tears past.

Forward, Tom is not clear.

A parted backstay lashes round his left arm and tightens as the fallen topmast goes over the side. Tom is dragged to one knee. The next pull will take him against the rail; the pull after that will take the rail with him.

Jack has Tom's knife.

He does not remember taking it. The handle is in his hand because Tom gave him the lee brace and the knife hangs beside every order Tom gives. Jack runs forward while other men run aft.

The deck changes angle beneath a wall of rain. He falls, keeps the knife and loses a piece of skin from one knee. The backstay tightens. Tom's shoulder strikes the rail.

Cut it! Tom shouts.

The line is tarred hemp thick as Jack's wrist.

Jack cuts.

The blade makes almost no mark.

Not across. With the lay!

Jack turns the knife and drives its point between the strands. The rope opens enough to show pale fibres within. He saws through one bundle, then another. Every movement of the wreck in the water closes the rope against the blade.

Tom's arm has gone dark below the turn.

Jack puts one foot against the rail, holds the knife in both hands and pulls upward through the last strand.

The stay parts.

The severed end goes over the side with such force it cuts a smoking line across the wet rail. Tom falls backward onto Jack. For a moment they are both on the deck beneath water that is rain and sea together.

Then Tom is moving.

He does not thank Jack. There is no time in which gratitude would not be another obstruction.

Clear the wreckage! Axes forward! Hands off every line until you know where it leads!

His left arm hangs badly. His voice does not.

The fallen mast pounds the hull. Each strike shakes the deck. If it is allowed to remain, the broken spar will drive through the planking or drag the ship broadside to the wind.

Men take axes to the surviving rigging. Every cut releases a new violence. One block flies upward when its load goes and catches Pritchard beneath the jaw. He disappears without a sound under the wrecked sail.

Jack sees his boot.

He starts toward it.

Tom catches the back of his coat with the good hand.

No.

He's under there.

The mast is over the side.

He's alive.

You do not know that.

I know his boot.

The ship rolls. The broken spar lifts in the sea and slams back against the hull. Beneath the canvas, the boot moves.

Jack tears free.

This time Tom follows.

They crawl beneath the edge of the sail while the quartermaster and three others haul it clear by inches. Pritchard lies between the fallen yard and the splintered boat, blood running from his mouth. Jack gets both hands beneath his shoulders. Tom grips the coat at the waist with his good hand.

Together they drag him out before the mast strikes again.

The axes finish the last stay. The topmast, yard and sail fall away into grey water and vanish behind the ship, still joined, still briefly looking like something built by men.

The foremast remains standing below the top.

Nothing above it does.

***

## IX. Under Bare Poles

The squall passes in less than an hour.

That is the insult of it.

The black wall goes east. Rain thins. A broken sunlight opens across the water and makes every wet surface shine: the severed rigging, the split boat, the blood at Pritchard's collar, the empty place above the foretop where half the mast used to be.

The sea looks refreshed.

The ship looks robbed.

Pritchard lives. His jaw is broken and three teeth lie somewhere beneath the forward gun. The surgeon binds his head and says he may never chew on that side. Pritchard asks whether the lost teeth are to be charged as Company stores. The words are mud in his mouth. Jack laughs before he can stop himself.

Pritchard does too, then regrets it.

Tom's shoulder is not broken. The backstay has cut a dark groove round the upper arm and pulled the joint until every movement makes his face empty. The surgeon offers a sling. Tom uses it for one watch, then takes it off because a boatswain pointing with his right hand sends men toward the wrong rope.

The master holds an inquiry before the deck is dry.

He asks why sail was not shortened sooner. He asks whether the foretopmast was unsound. He asks who last inspected the cap. Each question travels carefully around the fact that he said the weather would pass astern.

The clerk sits at the cabin table with his pen waiting.

Tom gives him no shelter.

The upper wind changed before the wind at deck level. The foretopsail backed twice. The topmast moved out of true at the cap. I ordered men down and put Mercer on the lee brace. The mast failed before the sail could be taken in.

Mercer saw the movement? the master asks.

Yes.

A boy?

A lookout aloft.

Was the cap defective?

It is in the sea.

Then we cannot know.

We know it failed.

The master turns to Jack. How long between your reporting the movement and the loss of the mast?

Jack looks at Tom.

Answer the question, Tom says.

Ten seconds.

You counted?

No.

Then how can you state ten?

Jack gives the actions. The line in his hands. The quartermaster's warning. The first rain. The orders to ease and haul. The shout from forward. Tom stepping clear. The wind.

The clerk stops writing midway through because actions do not fit the narrow space left for the answer.

Approximately ten seconds, he writes.

The master asks whether Jack would have remained aloft without Tom's order.

Yes.

And been lost with the mast.

Yes.

The question waits for gratitude. Jack has gratitude. He also has Tom's blood beneath his fingernails from opening the backstay.

Tom would have gone over if I hadn't cut him loose, he says.

The clerk looks up.

The master does not want this second truth. It complicates the shape of command. A boatswain saves a boy; a boy does not save a boatswain. One is discipline. The other looks like dependence.

Tom puts his injured arm on the table.

Enter it.

The clerk enters both.

***

## X. A Shorter Mast

For nine days the ship moves under reduced sail while the crew builds what the sea allows.
