## II. The Signature

The Company sends a new form in 1621.

It is called a declaration of ordinary loss. Masters, pursers and boatswains are to sign at the end of every passage, confirming that shortages in water, stores, rope and sail arose from weather, necessary consumption or the natural dishonesty of sailors rather than negligence by Company officers.

Tom reads it twice.

The clerk waits with sand ready.

Where are the amounts?

Attached after landing.

Then the declaration comes after landing.

Procedure requires signatures before cargo discharge.

I cannot declare a number I have not seen.

You are not declaring the number. Only the nature of any loss.

Any loss has not happened on this page yet.

The clerk points to the signature line. Every other officer has signed.

Tom's hand refuses to move.

It is not fear of writing. He signs wage books, orders, inventories and punishments. His name exists in Company rooms from London to Bantam. The aversion belongs to the white space above the line, the place where another man's meaning can be entered after Tom's hand has made it true.

He remembers Bell across the counting-room table, asking whether Tom would sign if he knew the debt was designed never to close.

Tom signed the next morning.

His father's ruined knee became rent, physician, burial and interest beneath that signature. Jack's father's broken body became four pounds. A hand does not need to understand a machine to start it.

Tom turns the form over and writes on the back.

*No declaration made until quantities are attached and read.*

He signs beneath that sentence.

The clerk refuses it.

Tom leaves the paper on the desk.

For three days his pay is held. On the fourth, the chief factor summons him and explains that administrative order requires trust between servants of the Company.

Trust whom? Tom asks.

The process.

Which man is that?

The chief tells him not to be insolent.

Tom touches the scar.

The ship cannot clear without a boatswain's declaration. The factor cannot replace Tom before the tide. Power is not always the larger office. Sometimes it is the man whose missing name stops the page.

The amounts are attached.

Tom finds two coils of sound rope entered as ordinary loss. The purser meant to sell them privately after discharge. Tom removes the coils from the shortage, signs the corrected declaration and watches the clerk sand his name.

The ship clears.

The next form arrives six months later with a new clause permitting estimated quantities before landing.

The machine has learned from his refusal.

***

## III. Shore Leave

Tom takes shore leave once in six years.

Not market leave, when the ship remains inside every decision. Not the walk between factory and landing. A full day with no watch, no tally and no man expected to answer his whistle.

He wakes before the watch changes anyway.

The hired room is quiet. A bed, a basin, one chair, a door with a wooden bolt. Tom lies listening for rigging that is not there.

Without the ship's sounds, his body supplies them. A block knocking at the foretop. Water moving in casks. Canvas beginning to flog. He knows none is real. He listens harder.

At dawn he bolts the door, then unbolts it because sleeping behind a barrier makes the room feel smaller. He places the chair where he can see the entrance from the bed. When the keeper brings breakfast, Tom is already standing.

Food first, the keeper says.

She means before payment. The words strike another room inside him.

Mutton and mace. A rag over one shoulder. A blue square folded beneath a counter. Maggie asking whether he has used his father's whistle better.

Most days, he told her.

He has spent years proving the answer in situations she will never see and all that time knowing proof is not what she asked for.

Tom eats.

The bread is flat and fresh. The fish has been fried with spice. He tastes meat pie.

He leaves half the food and pays for all of it.

On the stair, the sole of his left boot opens at the toe.

It does not tear cleanly. The stitching has rotted under salt and lets go one hole at a time, so that the leather mouth opens and shuts with each step. Tom stops, lifts the foot and presses the sole back with his thumb.

The keeper watches from the doorway.

There is a cobbler two lanes inland, she says.

I have cord.

You have a boot that has already refused cord.

Tom looks at the broken stitches. She is right. This irritates him more than the boot.

In the street, men move without watches. A father carries a sleeping girl. Two women argue over fruit. A carpenter planes a door while the owner holds it upright. Ordinary life surrounds Tom with the intimacy of a language he once spoke and can now only understand when others use it.

He finds the cobbler by the boots hanging beneath an awning. The shop is open to the lane: a low bench, knives, lasts, pegs, a pot of blacking gone soft in the heat. The cobbler takes one look at the loose sole and holds out a hand for the other boot.

Only this one, Tom says.

Both.

The right is sound.

The cobbler bends it once. Three stitches lift along the welt.

Both, he says again.

Tom understands enough of the local trade speech to buy rope, water and a day's labour. Boots have not been necessary to that education. He points to the sun, then to the work, asking how long.

The cobbler points to noon.

Tom looks at the lane behind him. A man without boots cannot choose a direction quickly. He removes the left, then the right, and places them side by side on the bench rather than surrendering them directly. The cobbler gathers them without comment and points to a stool beneath the awning.

The stool faces the street. Tom turns it until he can also see the rear door.

The cobbler cuts the old thread. He does not hurry and does not explain. Each bad stitch comes free separately. The failure that looked sudden on the stair has been travelling around the sole for months: leather dried hard by salt, holes enlarged by strain, thread dark where water entered and pale where friction ate it.

Tom knows this kind of damage. A line parts at the final fibre and begins parting long before.

How much? he asks.

The cobbler names the price.

Tom pays half. The man counts it, puts it beneath a stone and continues. No gratitude passes in either direction. The rate has made room for silence.

For ten minutes Tom sits.

A handcart loses a pin outside the fruit stall. The owner replaces it with a nail and keeps going. A woman lowers a basket from an upper window on a cord polished by years of the same journey. Two boys carry a door between them while the carpenter walks backward, shaving one last high place without making them set it down. Somewhere behind the shops, somebody beats dust from a mat in a steady rhythm that becomes, in Tom's body, men hauling together.

He makes himself stay on the stool.

The awning line above him is wrong.

It has been taken twice around a peg and finished with a half-hitch that draws tighter whenever the cloth fills. The free end is already pinched flat. One gust will not bring the awning down. Twenty ordinary gusts will make the knot impossible to release, and then someone will cut good line because a bad turn has eaten it.

Tom looks away.

The cobbler draws waxed thread through the first sole.

Tom looks back.

The peg wants a round turn and two hitches, he says.

The cobbler follows his pointing finger, looks at the awning, then at Tom's bare feet.

Boots, the man says, and returns to them.

Tom lasts another minute.

Then he stands on the warm boards, reaches above the doorway and takes the strain of the awning across one forearm. The cobbler says something sharp. Tom holds up the flattened end, points to the place where it has begun to fail and reties the line so the load sits on the peg instead of the finish.

The awning lifts one inch and lies still.

The cobbler examines the knot. He pulls the free end. It releases. He ties it again himself, slower, and leaves it.

Tom returns to the stool.

The man works on the boots. Tom watches the street. Neither calls what happened payment.

At midday a food carrier passes with flat bread under cloth, a pot of lentils and a jar of sour milk. The cobbler buys one portion and divides it beside the blacking stone. He pushes half toward Tom.

I ate, Tom says.

The cobbler points at Tom, then at the food, dismissing the claim as irrelevant.

Tom pays for his portion. The man returns one of the coins.

Awning, he says.

Tom takes the coin back. Refusing a fair account would only make another kind of debt.

They eat without conversation. The bread is hot enough to soften the hard place at the corner of Tom's mouth. The lentils are plain. The milk is cool. Around them the lane continues its unrecorded exchange of work, food, carrying, waiting and repair. Nobody is being saved. Nothing is being proved.

At noon the cobbler gives Tom both boots. The left sole lies close. The right has been restitched before it could open. Tom puts them on and walks the length of the lane, testing the work.

He could return to the hired room. The chair still faces the door. The bed is paid through morning. A whole afternoon remains in which no man expects his judgement.

He goes to the river.

Boats require rope. Rope requires inspection. Within an hour he is helping a boatman reset an eye in a worn painter. Before the afternoon is half gone he has found a cracked block. By late light he has arranged replacement line through a broker who owes the factory nothing.

The times no longer agree because leave has no bell to make them agree. Tom notices only when the light changes. The painter leads to a frayed lashing at the thwart. The lashing leads to the cracked block. The block leads to a length of line stored in shade and sold without Company measure. Each repair names the next. By doing them, Tom turns an afternoon without orders into a watch only he knows he has taken.

At sunset he returns to the ship relieved.

The relief shames him less than the room did. A ship does not ask whether a man can sit still inside his own life. It gives him a line under strain and accepts the answer of his hands.

The sea carried home in him: door, food, thimble, blue cloth, Maggie's exact refusals.

Home carried back to sea: his habit of repair, his place at the threshold, the rule that a hungry boy eats before he answers.

Neither place releases the other.

***

## IV. The Letter Not Sent

Tom writes to Maggie seven times.

The first attempt begins in the Surat factory after his mother's parish letter arrives. He writes Maggie's name, then nothing beneath it. News of the death feels like a claim: feed me, forgive me, tell me I still belong somewhere. He folds the blank page around the name and puts it in his chest.

The second begins with Jack.

*The boy came home alive.*

This is true. It also arranges Jack's survival as evidence for Tom's defence. He hears himself entering the nineteen dead in smaller writing below it. He tears the page.

The third says the debt was corrected.

The fourth explains the paymaster's collar.

The fifth contains only the words *You were right*, which appear humble until Tom reads the demand hidden inside them: now tell me I am no longer wrong.

He burns that one.

The sixth is a proper letter. Weather. Surat. Cotton. His mother's death. Jack's work in Deptford, learned from a sailor newly arrived from England who saw the boy outside a rigging shed. Tom writes two pages without once saying why he has written them.

He signs late.

The signature makes the letter into something that may travel. Until then it was only thought arranged on paper.

Tom carries it to the packet clerk.

Which ship? the clerk asks.

Tom names the westbound vessel.

Fee?

The clerk names it.

Tom pays, then takes the letter back before the clerk can enter it.

Not ready?

No.

The clerk has handled merchants' promises, dying men's instructions and confessions addressed to wives who will receive them after the sender has found courage elsewhere. He does not ask.

The seventh letter begins in 1622 after the boy at the companionway reties the coil and goes to eat.

Tom writes:

*I remembered before I knew what remembering required.*

He sits with the sentence until dawn.

Then he adds:

*I do not know whether that matters to you. It matters to the orders I give.*

For once the page does not offer survival as acquittal. It asks for nothing. That should make it sendable.

Tom folds it.

Does not sign.

The unsigned letter remains beneath his mother's parish notice. Two messages in the same chest: one that travelled too late and one kept from travelling at all.

Silence is not the absence of an entry.

It is an entry renewed every time the ship sails west without the page.

***

## V. The Thimble

The thimble appears when Tom is most tired.

Not as a vision. As an error of the hand.

He reaches into a pocket for his knife and expects brass beneath his fingers. Opens his sea chest and looks toward the corner where his mother kept thread. Sees a glint beside a deck bolt and bends before recognising a washer.

The real thimble is in Wapping.

Unless Maggie sold it.

She would not.

Unless the Pelican burned.

He would have heard.

Unless she threw it into the Thames after he left.

She picked it off the flagstones.

Tom does not know this last fact. He remembers the sound of brass striking stone and the door closing behind him. Everything after belongs to hope pretending it is inference.

At night the scene replays with changes.

Sometimes he picks it up. Maggie tells him the wager is closed and turns away.

Sometimes Jack picks it up and carries it aboard. The thimble rolls loose during the Madagascar squall and disappears through a scupper.

Sometimes Mara stands behind the bar in dark silk, though Tom has never seen her in dark silk and has not seen her at all since the night she vanished. She puts the thimble beside Bell's page and bolts the door from within.

Tom wakes touching the scar.

He begins carrying a brass sailmaker's palm in his coat. It is larger than a thimble, flat and practical, its dimpled metal set in leather. He tells himself the familiar brass stops the hand searching.

It does not.

Men think the palm belonged to his father. Tom never corrects them. A false explanation can become privacy if nobody profits from it.

***

## VI. The Echo

In 1622 a young sailor freezes in a doorway.

The ship is taking water at anchor after a cargo boat strikes the quarter. Not enough to sink her. Enough to make every order urgent. Men run pumps, shift stores and rig a fothering sail over the damage.

The boy stands at the companionway holding a coil, unable to decide whether the order sends him below or aft.

Tom sees the hesitation.

Move!

The boy does not.

Tom crosses the deck and stops before striking distance.

Years ago he would have used the hand. Jack on the yard, his weight on the pale buntline. Fear broken by force before the rope broke itself. That blow saved Jack and remains a blow.

Tom points with the whistle.

Look at me. Which line?

The boy names it.

Where does it lead?

Aft.

Then go aft.

The boy moves.

Tom hears his own voice and his father's inside it. Beneath both, Maggie: do not call the taking a rescue. Beneath Maggie, Bell asking one quiet question across an unsigned page. Beneath Bell, a woman behind a door who has no English word available and a room deciding what her silence means.

None of these people is present.

All of them alter the order.

By midnight the leak is controlled. The boy brings back the coil properly flaked and waits for praise.

Tom examines it.

Tie it again.

The boy's face falls.

Tom touches the first turn.

This one crosses. Under load it binds. Again, and then eat.

The boy reties it. The line runs clean.

Good, Tom says.

The word still feels expensive. He spends it.

The boy goes to the galley.

Tom takes the doorway and watches the ship settle around the repair. He is forty-one. The scar aches before weather. His name stands beneath more Company orders than he can remember. Somewhere west, a woman he loves may still hate what he became. Somewhere east, other ledgers are closing around names he has not yet heard.

The sea repeats nothing exactly.

It carries the sound forward changed.

# News from the Sea (1614–1623)

> **1614–1623**
>
> *“I ask whether he is alive. I do not ask whether he remembers me.”*
>
> — Maggie, keeper of the Pelican

***

## I. Paid Off

The sea never brings a man home alone.

It brings the dead in his mouth, the missing in his pauses and every officer he hated still giving orders inside the story. A crew pays off at Blackwall and reaches the Pelican by degrees: first the men who kept enough advance for ale, then the men whose wives have counted the wage, then the one who spent an hour outside the pay room arguing over a charge and needs to tell somebody who knows the price of an empty number.

By 1614 the order is settled: Maggie waits until they have eaten.

Hunger edits toward the next plate. Ale edits toward courage. She gives a returning man stew, bread and one pint, then asks the same questions in the same order.

Which ship?

Who did not come off it?

Names.

After the names, she asks about the living.

The questions after food are smaller than the questions before it.

Not what great weather they survived. What they ate when the salt beef stopped surrendering to the pot. Who slept beneath the leaking seam, mended another man's shirt, cheated at cards or still picked onions from the common bowl. The Company asks what labour came home. Maggie asks how the men lived while they were still ordinary enough not to know which of them would.

One November evening three paid-off hands arrive together and stop just inside the room, not from fear but because each expects one of the others to choose the table. A year of answering the same orders has made choice feel like work. Maggie points them to the long bench, sets down one pot and three bowls, and cuts the loaf before any man can say he has coin.

The oldest pushes his bowl away when he sees peas.

I cannot look at them, he says.

Then look at the bread.

He does. He eats half the loaf while the others empty the pot. Maggie brings him broth from the smaller pan and asks no question about waste. A man home from the sea may refuse one thing without being called ungrateful for the rest.

The youngest still holds his spoon as though another hand will claim it between mouthfuls. He finishes first and keeps scraping after the bowl is clean. The third man puts aside every piece of carrot, arranging them along the rim by size. His mates watch him do it and begin laughing before Maggie understands.

Every mess, the oldest says. Fair weather, foul weather, fever through half the lower deck. He sorts the carrots.

They are not all the same, the man says.

They are all carrots.

That is why God did not make you a purser.

The laughter is not loud. It does not need to be. The joke has crossed two oceans in a wooden bowl and reached Wapping without an officer improving it. Maggie lets it run until the youngest asks whether there is more. She fills his bowl. Only then does she ask the ship.

They give it. She asks who did not come off.

This time the names arrive without speeches. One man lost to the flux before the Cape. One taken from the foretop by a line that carried away clean. Two buried on an island while the ship watered. A boy who seemed to mend, asked for biscuit, and died before the cook had soaked it soft enough for him. The oldest gives each surname if he knows it. The carrot-sorter supplies a parish for one and the name of a sister for another. The youngest knows the boy only by the place his hammock hung.

What was he like awake? Maggie asks.

The youngest looks startled.

The boy.

Sang badly.

What song?

All of them.

The oldest says the boy tied his shoes together round a stanchion every night after a rolling sea stole one. The third remembers that he saved the burnt skin from the porridge pot and claimed it tasted of Sunday bread. None of these facts assists a widow's claim. Maggie keeps them in the room anyway.

She learns the voyage by asking about habits no manifest keeps. Who wakes before every watch? Who can mend a cuff without looking? Who sings the fourth line wrongly until the whole mess adopts it? Which man gives away his onions and steals every carrot? Three sailors correct one another until a person appears where a rating stood.

The ordinary work matters because kindness hides there without acquiring a witness. A topman goes aloft twice for a hand shaking with fever. A cook saves the bottom of the barley pot for the boys and calls it burnt. Another hand silently takes a sick man's turn at the pump, then denies he did more than wanted doing.

Maggie asks who did those things. Often nobody knows. A ship can remember an order by the man who shouted it and forget the hand that carried it out.

When the three men have finished, the youngest gathers the bowls and carries them to the bar without being asked. The oldest folds the unused end of the loaf in the cloth it came in. The carrot-sorter slips his row back into the pot when he thinks Maggie is not looking, unwilling to eat them and equally unwilling to leave food visible on a table.

They have been ashore six hours. Their bodies are still keeping a mess.

Maggie gives them one pint each. Now ale may edit what remains, but hunger has already told its honest part.

Tom's name reaches her in the summer of 1614 from a topman who served the homeward passage. Fletcher, the sailor calls him, because shipboard rank makes surnames useful. Boatswain. Scar through the brow. Brought the ship round the Cape under a jury mast and took a paymaster by the collar when the books swallowed a boy's wage.

Alive? Maggie asks.

Alive when I left him.

Where?

At the Company's door.

Going in or coming out?

The sailor considers. In.

Maggie puts another piece of bread on his plate. She does not ask whether Tom spoke of the Pelican. She does not ask whether he touched the scar while deciding. Those questions would make the sailor invent kindness to pay for his meal.

News is expensive enough without buying the false part.

***

## II. Jack

Jack comes from Deptford three months later.

The boy's thin coat has been replaced by a rigger's short jacket, shiny at both elbows and honest about hard use. He is broader. Two fingers on the right hand close crookedly around his cap. The sixteen-year-old who left the Pelican knew three knots. This man smells of tar, warmed hemp and the low shed where he is learning the rest.

Maggie puts mutton broth before him.

I have money, he says.

Then you may pay after.

He eats before she asks anything. Jack understands the order better now.

Are you going again?

No.

Not even for better wages?

Especially not for better wages.

She looks at the hand. Jack turns it palm down. Maggie lets him. A person may refuse a question before it is spoken.

The account? she asks.

Closed.

Properly?

It took some opening first.

Tom?

Some.

Jack says no more. He has not come to make one man stand trial in front of another man's absence. He tells her he has a month's place with a Deptford rigger and a fixed tool charge. At the end the tools become his. He made the master put that on the page.

Maggie gives one small nod.

Did Tom send you?

No.

Did he give you a message?

No.

The second no costs Jack more. Maggie sees him prepare to add something merciful and stops him by carrying away the bowl.

He knows the road, she says.

Jack comes back at intervals after that. Sometimes Carter has sent him to inspect a hoist. Sometimes a Deptford boatman has given him a word for somebody in Wapping. Once he comes because the Pelican's sign rope is chafed and he has noticed from the road.

He takes the old line down, splices a new eye and charges Maggie the proper rate after she refuses friendship's price.

The sign hangs true.

The crooked fingers hold.

Neither of them calls that an account closed.

***

## III. A Man in Pieces

Other crews bring Tom back in pieces.

One sailor says he is the hardest boatswain east of the Cape. Another says he delays a cargo boat because local hands read danger on the Surat bar, and the boat sent out against their judgement overturns before noon. A third swears Tom can see private trade through a sealed provision chest. A fourth calls him Company to the marrow and means it as praise.

Did he sell the men's ration? Maggie asks.

No.

Take their advance?

No.

Strike them?

When they were slow.

Slow from fear or slow from hunger?

The sailor has not divided men that way. Maggie makes him try.

In 1619, a warehouse hand newly paid off remembers twelve new boys arriving at Surat two years earlier, thin from the passage. The factor begins examining them for work. Tom stops the examination and orders food.

Before he asked what they could do? Maggie says.

Yes.

Before he put them on a ship?

No. Ten went aboard after.

Then keep both parts.

The man looks offended. I only said he fed them.

And now you have said what the feeding was for.

Maggie is not collecting evidence for Tom's acquittal. That is what paid-off men think at first. They see her stop wiping when his name comes, see the blue cotton folded on a high shelf beneath the counter, too fine for the room and too carefully kept to be forgotten, and decide they can purchase favour with a good report.

She makes them give the bad part too.

The hostile men make the opposite mistake. They think one hard order will buy them a free pint if they describe the boatswain as a tyrant. Maggie makes them name the weather, the rope and the men under it. Sometimes the order remains cruel after the facts. Sometimes it becomes the only one that brought a deckhand home.

She keeps both.

The Company sends fair copies. The sea sends contradictions.

Maggie trusts the contradictions more.

***

## IV. The Name Missing

In 1620 a guard newly home tells the room about a stolen bale of calico that was never stolen.

He remembers the English factor who wanted a porter punished, the two tallies that disagreed and Tom laying the pages together until one missing stroke returned the whole bale to the warehouse. He remembers the new double count afterward because it shortened his watch.

What was the porter's name? Maggie asks.

The guard does not know.

You watched them hold him.

I watched the gate.

What did they call him?

Porter.

And the man who found the error?

Fletcher.

There is the Company's arithmetic even inside a sailor's memory: the English officer keeps his name; the man almost punished becomes his labour.

Maggie gives the guard no second pint until he remembers more. A red headcloth. A room near the west warehouse. Four seasons at the landing. A first sound, perhaps Yus or Yusuf, offered without confidence.

She does not cut the name into the bar. A doubtful word does not become true because oak is harder than air. She says Yusuf once, gives it to the room for the night and lets uncertainty stand beside it.

Jack is there, delivering a sling for Carter's cask hoist. He listens from the short end of the counter.

Tom saved him, he says when the guard has gone.

For how long?

Jack looks at her.

That is an unfair question.

Most exact ones are.

The new tally prevents another false charge. It also moves more cloth. The correction and the use arrive in one story, as they so often do now when Tom's name travels west.

Jack turns his ale between both hands.

He was like that aboard, he says. Fix the rope and the ship goes faster into the same weather.

Maggie could ask then. Did he say my name? Does he hate me? Why did he choose the Company again when the road west was open?

She asks whether Jack has enough work for winter.

He does.

She feeds him anyway.

***

## V. No Letter

By 1620 Tom is easier to locate and harder to know.

His procedures travel on ships that do not carry him. Double tallies. Raised cotton platforms. Water allowances copied in a boatswain's hard hand. Men who have never served under him complain about his rules in the Pelican. Men who owe their full wage to one of those rules defend him with equal ignorance.

Maggie asks whether he is alive.

The answer keeps being yes.

She does not ask when he is coming home.

The Company packet office is less than an hour's walk. For a fee and the right ship she could send a page to Surat. Jack offers once to find a clerk who will write it plainly. Maggie refuses before he finishes.

What would it say? he asks.

The door remains where it was.

That sounds like asking him back.

It is not.

What, then?

The truth.

Jack waits.

Maggie wipes the counter. A truth sent for the purpose of producing an answer is already becoming a bargain. She put Tom out because he had learned to call the taking of a boy inevitable and his own place in it kindness. If he returns because loneliness has made the sentence cheaper, neither of them will know what has been repaired.

So she sends nothing.

No page comes from him.

Absence therefore remains equal on both sides, which is not the same as equal cost.

Packet days announce themselves in the room. Sailors carry folded pages for wives, mothers, creditors and women whose names they will not say aloud. Maggie keeps letters behind the bottles until their owners come. She never asks whether one arrived without a name for her. A clerk who had such a page would know the house. Every packet that empties without it is answer enough, and not an answer at all.

***

## VI. The Next Handhold

Early in 1623 a paid-off sailor freezes just inside the Pelican door.

The room is warm. Nothing threatens him. His body has mistaken the dark entrance for a companionway on a leaking ship, and for several breaths he cannot decide whether to come in or run.

Maggie does not touch him.

The hearth is six steps, she says. Can you walk that far?

He looks at her.

Yes.

Then that is all you are doing.

He reaches the fire. She puts bread and stew into his hands and waits until the shaking leaves enough of them to hold the spoon.

The sentence was Tom's. The sailor heard it from him in another doorway after an urgent order became too large to obey. Tom made him name the line, name where it led and take only the next part. Later he made the boy tie the coil again.

Then eat, the sailor says. That was his next order after the handhold. Eat.

Did he strike you?

No.

Did he praise you?

The sailor smiles into the bowl. One word. Cost him more than the food.

Maggie turns away to draw ale. The counter does not need wiping, but she wipes it.

Jack comes in before the sailor leaves. He has a wife now, mentioned not as news but as the person who says he must stop carrying wet rope against his good coat. He hears the end of the story and looks at Maggie.

Anything to send east? he asks.

No.

Anything if he comes west?

Maggie looks toward the door. Tom stood against it once, repaired its hinge once and last passed through it with his mother's thimble on the stones behind him. The wood has held every version.

Food first, she says.

Jack nods. It is not a message. He does not carry it.

By year's end the sea has sent Maggie a hundred sentences about Tom. Not one is from Tom. She lodges none beside Bell's page. Hearsay is not a true copy, however badly the heart wants to keep it.

The blue cotton remains folded beneath the counter. The thimble remains inside the fault. His old notch darkens with the bar.

The silence is intact.

# The Intersecting Web (1614–1623)

> **1614–1623**
>
> *“A net catches what enters it. A ledger tells you who tied the knots.”*
>
> — Mara, Bantam, 1623

***

## I. The Second Desk

The second teak desk is smaller than the first.

Mara orders it in 1614 from the same joiner who built the heavy one in her counting room. He remembers the woman who rejected a rocking corner and tests this top before she arrives. Four square legs. One drawer. No carving.

Where will it stand? he asks.

Amboyna.

He looks toward the roadstead as though the island might be visible beyond the ships. It is not. Amboyna is a name moving west in bales and east in orders: cloves, soldiers, rice, powder, debt. A place may be absent from the horizon and still occupy half a counting house.

The desk goes aboard a Javanese trader with two packets of paper, a chest of plain cotton and a letter to a woman Mara has never met. The woman's name is Aminah. Her brother carries cloves between Hitu and the market at Ambon; her husband died owing money to three merchants, each of whom owns a different account of the sum. Aminah speaks Malay and the island tongues Mara does not. She reads the figures the Portuguese leave behind and distrusts every seal she has not watched being pressed.

Iskandar found her by sending the same disputed receipt east by two ships and asking who returned it corrected.

Both copies came back in Aminah's hand.

Mara's letter offers a monthly rate, paper at cost and half the fee on work Aminah originates. It requires two copies of every page. One travels by the quickest safe ship. The other stays behind the false bottom of the new desk.

Aminah sends no answer in words. Four months later the first packet returns: clove weights from three villages, Dutch advances against the next harvest, Portuguese prices whispered at Banda and the names of two shipmasters taking private cargo beneath Company stores. Every figure is copied once in Malay and once in the merchant's own language. Corrections are crossed through, not scraped away.

At the bottom Aminah has written her rate.

It is higher than Mara offered.

Mara pays it.

The next desk goes to Makassar. That one belongs to a Chinese bookkeeper in Makassar who writes a narrow merchant's Malay and five kinds of number. The third is not a desk but an oilskin box under the floor of a Gujarati broker's godown at Masulipatam. A Tamil ship-chandler keeps another packet in a rice jar nobody in his household would mistake for rice. In Patani, a widow who supplies sailcloth remembers reports instead of storing them until two different ships have carried the words away.

Mara does not own these people. That is the first rule.

She buys their work. She writes the term. She pays when the page arrives, including when the news has become useless during the voyage. Information that loses a race with the monsoon is still labour. Companies forget this whenever delay gives them an excuse to keep the coin.

The pages begin to cross on her teak.

A Portuguese captain buys rice at one port after denying a shortage at another. A Dutch merchant orders more chains in the same month he promises a village its growers will remain free. An English factor complains that cloves cannot be had and quietly asks what a private parcel would cost. No single page proves the shape. Laid together, they show it.

Iskandar comes in one morning and finds string stretched across the room.

One end is pinned beside a copy from Amboyna. Another reaches a freight list from Makassar. A third joins an English order to a Dutch insurance account. The string does not mark sea routes. It marks agreements men believe are separate because they signed them in separate rooms.

You have made a fisherman's net, he says.

A net closes around the thing inside it.

Does this not?

Mara looks at the strings. No. It shows where the thing may pass.

What thing?

Power.

Iskandar considers the answer and points to the cord running from Amboyna.

And who holds that end?

For now, Mara says, Aminah.

***

## II. The Price of One Tree

The Dutch do not want cloves. They want the sentence *all the cloves* to become true.

The difference arrives gradually in Mara's papers and all at once in the villages. Contracts become quotas. Quotas become inspections. Trees outside the approved ground become unlawful trees, as though a root can trespass against a charter signed on another continent. Where growers sell elsewhere, soldiers come with axes. Where island leaders object, the next agreement contains fewer island names.

Aminah records what the official reports call pacification.

Twenty-seven trees cut on one holding. A storehouse opened under guard. Two brothers taken for questioning and one returned. Rice withheld until a village produces the man accused of selling ten katties of cloves to Makassar. The reports give no speeches. Aminah has learned that adjectives are where a hostile reader begins his attack. She gives dates, weights, names and who was standing close enough to see.

Mara sells some of the information to English factors.

She knows what they want. Not the growers' freedom. Entry. A share. Proof the Dutch monopoly can be broken wide enough for an English ship to pass through. English outrage grows sharpest where English cargo has been excluded.

She raises the rate.

An English chief protests that the information concerns Dutch abuses.

Does that make your silver less heavy?

It makes us the natural buyer.

There is no natural buyer. Only the buyer named on the page.

He pays. Mara sends half east to Aminah and divides the remainder among the hands whose reports made the pattern visible. The chief assumes he has purchased a source. What he has purchased is one reading.

The same week, Mara refuses a Dutch request for the names behind it.

We pay you, the Dutch merchant says.

For stated work.

This is Company security.

Then the Company should have written secrecy into the first agreement.

The merchant puts both palms on the teak. You cannot trade between us forever.

Mara looks at his hands until he removes them from her desk.

No, she says. Nothing is forever. That is why the term has an end date.

In 1621 the packets from Banda stop.

The first missed ship means weather. The second means fear. The third brings a Dutch list of confiscated property and an English letter full of words such as outrage, massacre and opportunity. None names the woman who remembered reports in Patani or the men who carried Banda figures through Makassar. Mara lays the pages side by side and finds six familiar hands absent.

A Company report gives totals without people. Villages reduced. Leaders removed. Islands secured.

Mara takes out the earlier copies and writes the missing names on a separate sheet. She cannot prove every death. She will not turn absence into certainty because certainty would make a stronger sentence. Beside each name she writes the last date on which that hand was known alive.

The page has no buyer.

She pays for it herself.

***

## III. What Distance Costs

The network acquires a reputation before it acquires a name.

Men come asking for Mara's correspondents as though they are ships she may charter out. A Dutch factor wants a hand inside an English warehouse. An English chief wants the price paid to a harbour clerk at Makassar. A Portuguese captain wants to know which Javanese master carries Dutch letters without entering them on his freight list.

Mara offers information. They ask for people.

The distinction irritates them.

If the report is sound, the Dutch factor says, why conceal who made it?

Because the report must arrive again next month.

Then you expect us to trust an unnamed source.

You trust the weights because they agree with the cargo. Trust the page the same way.

He calls this evasive. Mara calls it the rate and waits until he chooses which word costs him less.

Distance protects the hands, but it also eats them.

A boat overturns in the Sunda Strait with two months of the Makassar bookkeeper's figures sealed inside a bamboo tube. The tube comes ashore. The boy carrying it does not. Mara knows his name only because the bookkeeper enters courier fees precisely: Rafi, seventeen, half before sailing and half owing to his mother.

Iskandar takes the coin to the kampung himself.

The mother asks whether her son died for the Dutch or the English.

Neither, Iskandar says.

Then who?

He tells Mara later that he could not answer. The boy died for weather, for wages, for a packet belonging to merchants who will never know what his face looked like. Every answer makes the death smaller or makes their work larger than it is.

Mara enters the remaining half under Rafi's full name.

Not courier, she says.

The account needs a purpose.

The account has one. Payment owed.

Iskandar watches her write. You say paper does not replace power.

It does not.

But you keep writing the names.

Yes.

Why?

Mara sands the line and returns the excess to the pot.

Because power should have to step over them.

The network changes after Rafi. No packet travels alone when two routes are available. No courier carries both a report and the key needed to read it. Fees due to families are paid before the next commission, not after. The rules make every page slower and more expensive.

Clients complain.

Mara raises the rate.

She also learns the other cost of distance: a person far away may use her name for something she would refuse nearby.

A Portuguese merchant in Makassar produces a letter guaranteeing his honesty under Mara's seal. The seal is a good copy. The promise is not. He has bought pepper on credit from three growers and put the same expected cargo against all three debts.

Mara could deny him by letter. By the time denial arrives, the pepper will be loaded and the growers will hold three papers against one ship.

She goes herself.

The passage takes twelve days outward and nine back. She spends two weeks putting the three accounts on one table, makes the merchant sell a parcel of cloth to cover the shortage and leaves each grower with the same witnessed schedule. The settlement costs Mara more than the false guarantee earned him.

You might have let them discover the forgery, Iskandar says when she returns.

They did discover it.

They might have discovered it was not yours.

That is a different discovery.

She has a new seal cut. Not more elaborate. More inconvenient. The mark now includes one tiny broken line no copyist would preserve unless told it matters. The Makassar bookkeeper knows the break. Aminah knows it. Four other hands know it. Nobody is told all the places where it must appear.

Trust, Mara understands, is not one rope drawn tight across the sea. One cut would finish it. Trust is an intersecting web: each strand proves where another has held, and no single hand owns the whole.

***

## IV. The English Hand

Daniel Vale first crosses Mara's desk as a correction.

The year is 1622. An English account from Amboyna charges a parcel of rice twice, once to the factory and once to a private mess. Aminah's clerk has marked the duplication in Malay. The English copy carries a cramped note along the margin:

*Not received into our store. Ask Mr Vale, who witnessed the unloading.*

Beneath it stands a small D and a surname written hard enough for the nib to catch.

Mara has seen thousands of names. Most pass under her hand once and return to being other people's lives. She copies this one because the dispute requires it.

Daniel Vale.

The answer comes two ships later. Vale confirms that six sacks went directly to the sickhouse after rain entered the lighter. He gives the names of the two porters, the condition of the seals and the hour the cargo came ashore. He does not improve the English factory's case. He does not improve the Dutch warehouse's. He states where the rice went.

Mara circles the name.

Why him? Iskandar asks.

He names the porters.

Honest men name witnesses?

Men who expect a page to be challenged do.

She sends the corrected account onward. With it she sends a question: whether Mr Vale will copy future disputed landings before surrendering the originals to either factory.

His reply is cautious. He has Company duties. He cannot undertake private employment prejudicial to his masters. The sentence is the English Company's sentence. Below it, in smaller writing, he lists the cost of paper at Amboyna and asks whether duplicates may be entered as an office expense.

Mara smiles without showing Iskandar.

Tell him no, she says. If his masters pay for the copy, his masters own the copy.

What do I tell him instead?

The rate.

For the next year Daniel's hand appears irregularly among Aminah's packets. Warehouse landings. Japanese soldiers' rice allowance. Repairs charged to the English factory for a roof the Dutch also claim to have repaired. His pages are not secret intelligence. They are ordinary accounts made dangerous by being exact.

Once he sends only a question.

*If two true reports name different owners of the same store, which report do you keep?*

Mara writes beneath it:

*Both. Ownership is the dispute. Do not edit it out.*

She makes a duplicate before the answer goes east.

***

## V. Closed Water

Early in 1623, the Amboyna packets change.

Ships are searched twice. Letters arrive with seals that have been warmed and pressed back crooked. Aminah begins writing market figures across harmless family messages, then sends the key by another vessel. Dutch orders speak of vigilance. English letters speak of insult. Japanese mercenaries at the fort appear in both sets of paper: wages in one, suspicion in the other.

Mara stretches new string across the counting room.

An order for extra lamp oil. A request for a Japanese interpreter. A prohibition on boats leaving after dark. A list of English stores copied by a Dutch hand. Alone, each page is administration. Together, they are a door being bolted.

She writes to Aminah: leave the second desk. Take the copies that name people. Send nothing through the fort.

The warning leaves on a Makassar prahu whose master has carried her paper for nine years. A contrary wind holds him east of Java. By the time his vessel reaches Amboyna, the desk is empty.

For six weeks, nothing comes back.

Then three accounts arrive on the same afternoon.

The English version says its factors have been seized on a fabricated conspiracy with Japanese soldiers. The Dutch version says a plot against the castle has been discovered and confessed. A harbour list records English prisoners transferred from the fort to other custody. The names are abbreviated, the condition of the men omitted.

Mara reads all three before she touches the food Iskandar has brought.

Eat, he says.

After.

Before. A dead broker reads nothing.

It is an old rule between them: food before argument, food before bad news, food before any decision a frightened body will pretend is reason. Mara eats three spoonfuls of rice while the papers wait. Then she draws the harbour list close.

Towerson is there. So are men whose hands she knows from English freight accounts. The Japanese names have been reduced to marks beside numbers. Daniel's name is not among the condemned.

It is not among the released.

Mara checks the second Dutch sheet. At the bottom, below powder issued and a boat ordered provisioned, stands a line entered by a junior clerk who expects nobody outside the castle to care:

*One English factor retained for examination and onward account. D. Vale. To Batavia by first secure passage.*

The line has no reason attached. No judgement. No mercy. Daniel has become movement between two columns.

Iskandar reads it over her shoulder.

You know him?

His hand.

That is not the same.

No.

Mara takes out Daniel's question about the two true reports. Beneath her answer, his cramped acknowledgement is still visible: *Both retained.*

She lays it beside the transfer line.

Around them, the counting room holds eleven years of crossings. Amboyna to Makassar. Makassar to Bantam. English store to Dutch fort. Rice to sickhouse. A witness into a ledger. Every strand is ordinary until somebody needs to know where it leads.

Mara removes the string pinned beside Amboyna and fixes its loose end beneath one word on the wall.

Batavia.

Then she begins another page.

# The Factor (1621–1623)

> **1621–1623**
>
> *“Not received into our store. Ask Mr Vale, who witnessed the unloading.”*
>
> — a margin note in an English account, Amboyna, 1622

***

## I. The Story Worth Telling

Daniel Vale goes east because he is good at sums and tired of being paid like a man who is not.

He is twenty-six, a merchant's clerk out of Rochester with four years of a London counting house behind him, a quick exact hand, and a wife of one year named Nell who can read his letters without help — a fact he boasts of, in the counting house, more often than he boasts of anything about himself. The Company is hiring factors for the eastward stations under the new treaty with the Dutch. The wage is triple. The advancement is real. The stories that come back off the ships are the kind men lean toward across tables.

That is the part Daniel will not say aloud, and Nell says for him, the last night, in the room over the chandler's.

You want to come back worth a story.

I want to come back worth a house.

The house is the story you tell me, she says. Go and get the other one. Write everything down. You are no good at telling; you are very good at writing down.

He promises a letter by every ship. It is the one promise the sea will let him keep for two years, and then not.

The voyage out is eight months of learning that a passenger-factor is cargo that dines with the captain. Daniel does not learn the ropes. He learns the manifests, which is his rigging, and by the Cape he can tell from the water book alone which cask was stowed green. At Bantam the Company sorts its new men the way a keeper sorts fish, and because Daniel's copies are clean and his totals are the kind that agree with other men's totals, he is given the posting the treaty has just opened and older men have just refused.

Amboyna.

The island comes up out of the east as a green steepness with its head in cloud, and the smell reaches the ship before the anchorage does — cloves, drying in the sun by the hundred thousand trees, a sweetness so complete it is almost a weight. Below the cloud stands the castle: coral stone, pale as bone, Dutch colours over the gate, guns counting the roadstead. And below the castle, in its shadow the way a chick stands in the shadow of a kite, the town — and in the town, renting its rooms the way the Company rents everything under the treaty, the English house.

The treaty is two years old. On paper, the two Companies have stopped burning each other's boats east of the Cape; on paper, the English take their third of the cloves and pay their share of the garrison that watches them take it. Daniel has read the articles on the passage out. They are drafted the way all good articles are drafted, each clause reasonable, the sum of them belonging entirely to whichever side holds the guns — and the guns, Daniel sees before his sea chest is ashore, are all one side's.

The English house holds a dozen men. The castle holds four hundred, and a company of Japanese soldiers in Dutch pay who drill on the strand in the evening, matchlocks and pikes moving in the clove-scented air like a sentence in a language nobody in the English house has troubled to learn.

Standing at the rail, the new factor does the only arithmetic available.

Then he goes ashore and reports to the man he has been sent to serve.

***

## II. The Best Man

Gabriel Towerson has been in the east longer than some of his clerks have been alive, and it shows the way weather shows in a sea wall: not decoration, structure.

He reads Daniel's letters of introduction standing, the way busy men read. Then he does the thing Daniel will remember at the end, in the room, when remembering has become the only country left him. He puts the letters down and looks at the man attached to them, longer than a chief looks at a junior, and says: What did you count on the way in?

Daniel tells him. The guns of the castle. The boats at the clove quay. The Japanese soldiers at their drill, and the number of them.

Any man counts the guns, Towerson says. Why the soldiers?

Because their wages are on somebody's book, Daniel says, and I have not seen the book.

Towerson smiles, on the side of his face away from the Dutch interpreter.

You will do, Mr Vale.

The work is a factory's work, and Daniel takes to it as a starved man takes to bread. Cloves in from Hitu and the leeward villages, weighed under Dutch eyes, divided under English ones, the third and the two-thirds entered in two houses whose books must agree and whose interests never do. Rice and cloth out. The garrison account, calculated in Amsterdam, disputed in Amboyna, paid in arrears everywhere: the English share of walls the English are not allowed to man, of powder pointed, as the assistants say after supper, in a direction nobody names.

Towerson runs the house the way Daniel will later understand the best men run anything caught between two powers: exactly, calmly, and out loud. Every dispute goes to paper. Every courtesy is returned. Twice in Daniel's first season the chief walks up to the castle over some quarrel of weights or wharfage, and comes down again having lost the point and kept the peace, and says the same sentence both times, hanging his coat on the peg.

The treaty holds, gentlemen. We are merchants.

The younger men repeat it as a joke when he is out of the room. Daniel does not. He has noticed the order of the words. Not: we are merchants, so the treaty holds. The treaty holds, so we are permitted to be merchants. Towerson knows which clause carries the weight. He has simply decided that a man cannot live crouched, and stands up straight inside the arithmetic, and teaches his clerks to do the same.

Going by lengths, Daniel writes to Nell, he is the best man I have served under. Then he crosses out going by lengths, because she will ask what the other measures are, and he does not want to write the castle into a letter she will read by a Rochester window.

***

## III. The Margin

The rice teaches Daniel what he is.

It is an ordinary cargo in the wet season of 1622: rice up from the coast for the factory and the garrison both, landed in a rain so hard the lighters cross the bay half-swamped. Daniel stands the landing because standing landings is his week's duty, and watches six sacks come ashore dark with bilge-water, and makes the decision a junior factor is paid to make: spoiled rice to the sickhouse, where men too fevered to taste will get the good of it before the rot does. He notes the hour, the porters — the tall one and the lame one, whose names he asks and writes — and the condition of the seals, and goes in out of the rain.

Two months later the account comes round for witnessing, and the six sacks are in it twice.

Once to the factory's store, at the landed price. Once to a private mess, at the landed price again. The same rice, eaten by fevered men in the sickhouse, has been sold to two different columns, and both columns expect Daniel's initials, because Daniel stood the landing.

The steward who kept the book is a heavy, friendly man who has been east eleven years and has a proverb ready: the wet season eats a little of every account, Mr Vale, and it is nobody's business to starve arguing with the rain.

Daniel looks at the page for a long time.

He is not brave. He will say so himself, years on, to anyone who tries to make him otherwise. What he is, is unable — physically, the way some men cannot carry a tune — to initial a number he watched be false. The two talents look identical on paper. They are not the same talent, and the difference will cost him more than bravery ever cost anybody.

He does not strike the entry. Striking it would start a war with a man he messes with. He writes, small, in the margin, in the cramped hand that presses too hard on the downstrokes:

*Not received into our store. Ask Mr Vale, who witnessed the unloading.*

And signs it — the little D, the surname driven into the page — and lets the account go forward to Bantam with the fleet's papers, where somebody, he supposes, files it, and nobody, he supposes, reads it.

Somebody reads it.

The answer comes back two ships later, by way of the Bantam market rather than the Company's bag: his margin note returned in a fair copy, the duplication marked in a strange neat hand, and a question attached that no Company clerk would think to ask. Whether Mr Vale will, in future, copy disputed landings before surrendering the originals to either factory.

There is no name on the question. There is a rate.

Daniel takes the paper to Towerson, because taking it anywhere else has a smell to it.

The chief reads it twice. You know what this is?

A broker.

The broker, Towerson says. Bantam prices her copies like physic and they cure like physic. He hands the page back. The Company will not pay for you to answer this, and I will not order you to. I will tell you what I have learned in thirty years of the two things, and you may do your own sums. Paper the Company owns tells the Company's story. Paper a man buys with his own coin is the only place his eyes are the ones that were there.

Daniel writes back cautiously — he has Company duties; he cannot undertake private employment prejudicial to his masters — and beneath the caution, in smaller writing, the truth of him: the cost of paper at Amboyna, and whether duplicates may be entered as an office expense.

The answer, when it comes, is six words and no comfort.

*If his masters pay for the copy, his masters own the copy.*

Daniel reads it in the doorway of the English house, in the smell of cloves and rain. Then he walks into the market and buys, with his own wage, a quire of paper and a stone bottle of ink, and the lame porter watches him carry them home under his coat like a man who has bought something it would be better not to drop.

***

## IV. The Names of Porters

After the rice, Daniel's pages change.

Not what they record — landings, weights, repairs, the garrison account, the small tides of a small factory. How they record it. Every entry now expects to be challenged. Every landing has its hour. Every dispute has its witnesses, named — porters, boatmen, the lame man and the tall man, people no Company account has ever bothered to make room for, because a company believes its own seal outweighs any number of hands that were actually there.

Daniel has stopped believing that, quietly, the way a man stops believing in a bridge he has watched crack.

The assistants chaff him for it. The steward has not forgiven him and shows it by praising his exactness to visitors, in the tone a man uses to praise a dog's hearing. Towerson says nothing, but the disputed landings begin, without any order being given, to fall on Daniel's weeks.

Copy them first, the strange neat hand had asked. He does. The copies go into a deal box with a plain lid, and the box goes under his bed, and some months, when a trusted ship is in, a copy goes west to the Bantam desk at the rate, and coin comes back that Daniel enters, in his private book, under the only heading that is honest.

*Paper of my own.*

The letters to Nell go by every ship, as promised. They are true, and they are edited, and he learns on the island what every man east of the Cape learns about the two: that a letter can be both, if it is written by a careful man, and that the caution is a kind of lie that mails itself. He writes her the green steepness and the clove sweetness and the sea like lit glass in the evenings. He writes her Towerson's jokes. He does not write the castle. He does not write that the boats are counted, or that the drill on the strand has moved, this season, from the evening to the morning, which a man who counts cannot help noticing means the soldiers now drill fresh instead of tired.

When do you come home? Nell writes, at the bottom of every letter, under her name, like a sum waiting to be carried.

After the next harvest, he writes back. He has written it three times now. The harvests come and go, and the third of the cloves goes into the English books at a profit that reads well in London, and Daniel Vale, who came east to be worth a story, sits up at night by a paid-for candle, copying disputed pages nobody has asked him to copy, in a house below a castle, on an island that smells of sweetness, adding himself line by line to the only account he fully owns.

He came east to be worth a story.

He is becoming worth a margin.

He does not yet know that of the two, it is the margin men are killed for.

***

## V. The Audit of Small Things

The closing begins so quietly that only a counting man could see it, and the counting man spends half a year telling himself he is counting shadows.

The first entry is the searches. English boats have always been boarded at the castle's pleasure — once, lazily, a formality with the manners of a toll. In the dry season of 1622 the boarding becomes twice, and the second search is not lazy. Bilges are sounded. Rice is run through fingers. A soldier turns Daniel's landing-book over, holds it upside down, and shakes it, watching not the book but Daniel's face.

The second entry is the seals. Letters from Bantam have always come with the wax a little tired from the passage. Now the wax comes warmed and pressed back crooked, the impression doubled at one edge like a stammer. Daniel holds a cover to the light and understands that somewhere between the ships and the English house, a patient man with a knife and a candle is reading the Company's mail, and not troubling to hide it well — which is its own message. We read what you write. Write accordingly.

The third entry he finds inside the castle's own housekeeping, because the garrison account still crosses his desk for the English share. An order for lamp oil, doubled. A request, sent through to Batavia, for an interpreter of Japanese. A prohibition, posted at the water stairs without explanation, on boats leaving the roadstead after dark.

And once — brought to him folded in a fish basket by the lame porter, who will not say what he was paid or by whom — a list, in a Dutch clerk's hand, of the English house's stores. Their own powder, their own muskets, eleven of them, their own beer and rice and candles, counted to the piece by somebody who had stood inside their storeroom and taken his time.

Daniel sits up that night and lays the pages side by side on the deal box, in the light of his own candle.

Alone, each page is administration. Oil is bought. Interpreters are hired. Harbours have rules. Together, on one table, at one midnight, they are something else, and the something has a shape, and the shape is a door being bolted from the other side.

Eleven hundred miles west, though he will never know it, a woman he has met only as a rate and a neat hand is laying the same pages side by side under strings, and reaching the same sentence.

He takes the sum to Towerson in the morning.

***

## VI. Reasonable Explanations

The chief hears him out entire. That is Towerson's way; he interrupts nobody, which the assistants call patience and Daniel has come to recognize as bookkeeping — the man lets every account be rendered in full before he audits a line of it.

Then he takes the entries one at a time, and one at a time they die.

The searches: there has been private trade in the roadstead, English private trade at that, and the castle is within its paper to look for it. The seals: the mails have been read since before Daniel was born; a crooked impression means only that the reader has grown careless, or busy. The lamp oil: the governor is building a new gallery, and galleries burn light. The interpreter: the garrison's Japanese soldiers have wages, grievances and contracts, and the castle has nobody who can hear them out; an interpreter is housekeeping. The prohibition on night boats: smuggling, Mr Vale, the answer to every harbour rule ever posted.

The stores list is the only one that slows him.

He reads it twice, and his face while he reads it is the face of a man doing sums he had hoped to be spared. Then he folds it and gives it back, and gives Daniel the sentence with it.

Any single page has a reasonable explanation. You have shown me six reasonable explanations standing in a row, and I will grant you that six is a number with a smell to it. But consider what you are asking me to do with your sum. There are twelve of us and four hundred of them, and the treaty is the wall we live behind — the only wall we have. If I act as though the treaty is dead, I kill it. I will not spend twelve men's safety on the arithmetic of lamp oil.

The treaty holds, gentlemen, he says, though only Daniel is in the room. We are merchants.

Daniel goes back to his desk. He does not argue, then or ever after — a fact he will turn over in the coral room, and in the cells at Batavia, and at a desk in Surat, and on a stool in Wapping, for the rest of his life, without once finding the version of himself that argues and is believed.

Instead he does the only thing his talent knows how to do. He makes another copy of the sum, entire, every page, in his own bought paper and his own cramped hand.

And for the first time, the copy does not go into the deal box under his bed.

***

## VII. The Garrison Share

The quarrel that fills the season between the stores list and the silence is about money, because on this island everything is about money until the last week of its life.

The garrison account falls due at the turn of the year, and the English share is in arrears — genuinely in arrears; Daniel keeps the account and will not pretend otherwise even now. London is slow, Bantam is slower, and the castle's demand sits on Towerson's table gathering courteous reminders the way a wreck gathers weed. The governor's men press. The English house temporizes. It is the oldest, safest, most ordinary kind of dispute two trading companies can have, and both sides settle into it with something like relief, because an argument about arrears has rules, and religion, and precedent, and nobody has ever been marched up a causeway at night over interest.

Daniel works the figures honestly and finds the honest total, and in the working discovers the entry that will not leave him alone afterward.

The Japanese soldiers' wages.

They are in the garrison account — of course they are; the English share helps pay them, which means Daniel has entered them quarter by quarter for two years: so many men, at so much, victualled at so much, the neatest lines in the castle's whole demand. And they are in the English house's own letters too, the ones the assistants draft for Bantam when the ale is in them — but there they are not wages. There they are *the Japanese*, a word with a new weight on it, the hired blades the Dutch keep at our elbow, men of no nation and any master, the first thing an anxious Englishman's eye counts on the strand and the last thing he mentions in a report signed with his name.

The same men. Wages in one book. Suspicion in the other.

Daniel sets the two pages side by side one evening — he cannot help it; setting pages side by side is the whole of him — and sits looking at what the setting shows. Every party on this island is keeping two accounts of every other party. The castle enters the English as partners in the garrison book and counts their muskets in a private hand. The English enter the soldiers as a wage line and fear them in the post. The soldiers themselves, for all he knows, keep some third reckoning of the two pale nations that hire and suspect them in the same breath, and if they do, no one on the island can read it, and no one has tried. The interpreter the castle sent for has arrived, a narrow man off the Batavia ship who eats alone; his wage is in the new quarter's demand. Daniel entered it himself.

He tells Towerson only the honest total, and the chief pays what can be paid and disputes the rest, and the courteous reminders resume, and everyone is satisfied, because the arrears are a language everybody speaks.

But Daniel has stopped believing in the argument. An argument about money is what this island wears over whatever it is actually doing — he has watched too many of its pages walk in pairs to trust any single sheet again. Somewhere behind the arrears, in a book he will never be shown, there is another account of the English house, kept in another hand, waiting for its season.

He is right, and being right will do him no good at all, which is the way of margins.

***

## VIII. Two True Reports

The last packet from the Bantam desk arrives in January, through the market, the seals honest because the route was never the Company's.

It contains his own pages back, fair-copied — the neat hand has had his warehouse landings, his roof repairs, the rice allowance of the Japanese soldiers, all the ordinary exactness he has sold west at the rate — and beneath them, unsigned as always, an answer to the question he had sent in the autumn, the question that had begun as bookkeeping and become, in the writing, something nearer to divinity.

*If two true reports name different owners of the same store, which report do you keep?*

*Both. Ownership is the dispute. Do not edit it out.*

Daniel reads it three times. Then he writes the acknowledgement at the foot — *Both retained* — and puts the packet where the sum went, off the compound, out of the English house, into the keeping of hands the castle has never thought to count, because the castle, like the Company, believes paper lives in houses.

He pays the porters from his own wage. He enters the payment in his private book, under the honest heading, and sits looking at the page.

Two true reports. The castle's account of the season: administration, housekeeping, harbour rules. His own: a door being bolted. Both true, line by line. Ownership is the dispute — ownership of the island, of the treaty, of the twelve men sleeping around him in the dark of the house — and he has just obeyed the neat hand's instruction and declined to edit it out.

It is the last thing he sends west.

In February the packets stop being answered. The market route goes quiet, the way water goes quiet before it decides. The lame porter does not come. The fish basket arrives with only fish in it, which frightens Daniel more than the stores list did.

He begins sleeping badly, and counting the drill on the strand from his window, and finding the count higher.

***

## IX. New Year

The last ordinary supper is ordinary enough that nobody marks it.

Rice comes to the table in the pot it was boiled in, clumped at the bottom where the cook let the water run low. Two small fish have been fried whole and divided badly. The steward takes the head of one by seniority and then spends the meal complaining that the eyes accuse him. An assistant says they have cause. Another turns the fish on the dish so it can accuse Towerson instead.

The chief moves it back.

I am answerable for the house, he says. Not for the fish.

That distinction should go into the treaty.

Put it in the margin, Towerson says, and the table looks at Daniel.

He has earned this teasing. For two years he has corrected the supper account when a jar of oil is entered twice, asked whether six candles issued means six whole candles or six ends, and once made the steward restore half a measure of rice because the household book had charged a visiting boat crew for food they brought themselves. The men now appeal every small uncertainty to him. A cracked cup. A missing spoon. Whether the cook's thumb, found in the rice while he serves it, belongs under victuals or wages.

Daniel gives the same ruling each time.

Insufficient evidence.

The phrase has become useful. It excuses bad cards, empty bottles and every story told after the third cup. Tonight an assistant lays down a winning hand with such satisfaction that the others inspect his sleeves.

Insufficient evidence, the winner says.

Evidence is not the difficulty, Daniel says. Your arithmetic is.

When the rice is gone, the cards continue. The cook puts the pot near the door to soak. Somebody begins mending a split cuff at the table and is advised by three merchants who cannot thread the needle. The steward loosens his shoes beneath his chair. Outside, the roadstead moves through its harbour sounds: an oar against a thwart, a call from the water stairs, rigging answering a small change in wind. None requires the house to rise.

Daniel writes to Nell at the far end of the table while the others play.

*We had fresh fish tonight,* he begins. It seems too small after months without a letter ship and therefore exactly the thing she will want. He tells her about the accusing eyes and the marked card. He reports that his superior disclaims responsibility for marine judgement and that the cook's thumb remains unclassified. He can see her reading the page at the Rochester window, knowing which sentences are jokes without needing the jokes explained.

He starts to write that he expects to come after the next harvest. The sentence has appeared in three letters and become less true with every use. He scratches it through before the ink dries.

*I keep your question,* he writes instead. *I have not yet made the sum answer it.*

An assistant leans across to ask whether writing to one's wife during cards constitutes private trade. Daniel covers the page with his hand. The table objects that a man who copies everybody else's business has no doctrine of privacy. Towerson tells them to leave him enough marriage to come home to.

The word home passes without changing the room.

Daniel sands the letter, folds it and writes Nell's direction. He puts it beside the household packet for the next westward boat. Then he returns to the score and proves that the assistant who has been boasting of victory is down by four points.

They argue over the count until the lamp needs trimming. Somebody finds the missing spoon beneath the steward's plate. The cook's thumb is acquitted. Men take their candles and go to their beds still disputing the cards, and the English house settles around them with the small knocks, coughs and floorboard complaints of a place in which tomorrow is expected to resemble today.

The end begins with a question, which Daniel will later think is the only mercy the island ever showed him: it let the shape of the thing be honest from the first hour.

A Japanese soldier of the garrison — young, off his watch, alone — stands at the foot of the castle wall in the cool of the evening and asks a Dutch sentry about the watch. How many men stand it. When it turns. What the word is.

It is the kind of question soldiers have asked each other out of boredom on every wall ever built. It is also the kind of question a wall's owners may make into anything they need, and the castle, that season, is in need.

They take him inside. This is known in the town by morning, the way everything is known in the town by morning: not the fact of it, the absence — a man who drank at a certain stall does not come, and the stall-keeper's face is closed, and the strand drill forms up one short, and nobody looks at the gap.

For two nights, sounds come down from the castle that the town agrees not to have heard.

In the English house, the assistants play cards badly and lose count of the deals. The steward drinks. Daniel stands at his window counting nothing, because the thing he is listening for has no number, and on the second night Towerson comes and stands beside him, and the two men look up at the pale walls together.

They will ask him questions, Daniel says, until his answers fit a shape they have already drawn. And then the shape will need more men in it.

You have no evidence for that.

I have six reasonable explanations in a row.

Towerson is silent a long time. The sweetness of the cloves comes in on the land breeze, thick as ever. Nothing on the island smells any different from the day Daniel first raised its green head out of the sea, which seems to him suddenly the most terrible fact of the place: the trees will not even change their breath for what is coming.

We are merchants, Towerson says at last. Quietly. Not to Daniel — to the wall, to the treaty, to thirty years of standing up straight inside other men's arithmetic. The treaty holds.

On the third day the castle sends down an invitation, courteous as ever, for the English chief to come up: a matter touching the garrison account, best settled in person. Towerson puts on his good coat. At the door he pauses and looks back at the house — the low room, the cards abandoned, the clerks watching him with their unfinished faces — and Daniel, who witnessed the unloading, who names the porters, who cannot initial a false number, records his chief in the doorway with the terrible completeness that is his whole gift, and says nothing, and lets him go.

That night the soldiers come down for the rest of them.

They come with lanterns and with the courtesy that is worse than blows, and the arrests arrive dressed as procedure: a matter of questions, gentlemen; a formality; the governor regrets the hour. Daniel is taken between two Japanese soldiers whose wages he has entered with his own pen, through streets he has walked for two years, up the causeway he has audited stone by stone in the garrison account he was too exact to leave alone.

He counts the steps to the gate. There are one hundred and eleven.

It is the last count he will finish for a long time.

# The Coral Room (1623)

> **1623**
>
> *“The paper was finished before I was brought in. My answers were a formality the ink was waiting to receive.”*
>
> — Daniel Vale, factor, Amboyna, 1623

***

## I. The Table

The room is coral stone and has no window.

Daniel notices the absence the way he notices everything, at once and exactly, and understands what it is for before anyone has spoken. A window is a clock. Light moves, and a man in trouble can hold on to its moving. This room has been chosen by somebody who thinks about what men hold on to.

There is a table. There is a man behind the table, neither young nor old, with the settled face of a conscientious schoolmaster, and there is a clerk beside him with paper, ink and sand. The paper is not blank. Daniel sees that in the first minute: sheets already close-written, laid in a squared stack, wanting only entries in the spaces left for them. He has prepared enough documents to know a prepared document across a room.

The questions begin gently, in fair English.

The plot. Who ordered it. When it was to be. Who else.

There is no plot, Daniel says.

The man behind the table nods, unhurried, unoffended — a man checking a sum that has not yet come out right — and makes a small sign, and the soldiers behind Daniel take his arms.

What is done then has a procedure, and the procedure is the most terrible part of it, because procedure means it has been done before, refined, taught. The chair. The binding. The cloth over the face, drawn snug with two little tugs, like a barber settling a towel. And then the water, poured with care, without anger, without hurry, while the body — his body, which has kept his columns and carried his sea chest and stood his landings for two years — abandons him entirely and becomes a single animal shriek of drowning that no resolve, no reason, no innocence can reach.

They stop. That is the other half of the procedure. They stop, and the cloth comes off, and the room reassembles itself — table, schoolmaster, clerk, stack — and the voice asks, mild as morning:

The plot. Who ordered it. When it was to be. Who else.

There is no plot, Daniel says, or the wreck of his breathing says it for him.

The man behind the table nods, and the pen, which has been waiting, does not write it down.

That is when Daniel understands the room entire. Not from the water; from the pen. It writes when he says certain things. It rests when he says others.
