The paper on the table has already decided what happened on this island, and the room exists to move his voice from the answers the pen ignores to the answers the pen is waiting for. Truth is not being sought here. Truth is being transcribed backward.

He is a copying man. They are making him into a page.

The cloth again. The water again. Hours are the wrong measure and he stops using them. There are only two times in the room, and he moves between them at a speed of another man's choosing.

At the end of the first session — sessions, another word from procedure, the schoolmaster actually says it, we will resume the session tomorrow, as though there were a syllabus — Daniel is taken down a passage he cannot afterward reconstruct and put into a cell with three of the others.

He does not tell them what is in the room. They will each learn it, and know he knew, and there is nothing in the knowing that helps a man. He sits against the coral, which is sharp and holds the day's heat, and does the only work left in his profession.

He begins to count.

***

## II. The Wall

He counts because counting is what he has instead of prayer, and because a man who counts still owns the numbers.

Breaths. The guard change, which comes at intervals of about six hundred breaths in the day and eight hundred at night, when the watches grow careless. The drips of the wet season working down through the coral. The sessions — his own, and the other men's, which can be told by the order the cells empty and refill, by which assistant comes back able to walk and which must be carried, by who eats and who only sits.

On the second day they take Towerson.

Daniel knows it without being told, the way the town knew about the soldier: by absence, by the way the guards straighten, by a door heavier than the others. And then, because his cell shares a wall with the passage to the room, he knows it by the fact of him.

Not words. The wall is coral and a yard thick and words do not survive it. What survives is the fact — pressure, rhythm, the shape of a voice he has heard every day for two years, hanging its coat on a peg, losing a point and keeping the peace. The fact of Towerson goes on beyond the wall for two days.

Daniel counts.

He will tell one man, three years later, in a bar on Wapping Wall, what he was counting. Not the breaths. The lengths. Going by lengths, he is the best man I have ever served under — and the length of him is going on, hour upon hour, session upon session, two days of a man refusing to move his voice to where the pen waits. Every hour the wall holds is an hour the paper stays unfinished. Daniel counts it the way men at sea count a spent storm: not because counting helps, but because the number is the only witness they can offer.

On the third day the wall goes quiet.

The quiet is worse. Sound was the fact of him; the quiet is the fact of the paper, complete somewhere, sanded, squared. Daniel sits against the coral and discovers that he has lost the count — the breaths, the guard changes, all of it, gone between one hour and the next, and he cannot say when.

It is the one thing he has never lost. He does not get it back whole, ever, and he will spend the rest of his life keeping ledgers the way other men keep lamps lit, and only one woman will ever understand what the columns are for.

That evening the guards' voices in the passage are loose and ordinary again. One of them laughs. The syllabus has moved past its hardest lesson.

The next morning they resume the session.

***

## III. The Signature

The room has changed. It takes Daniel a moment to find the change, and then he finds it.

The stack of paper is thinner. Most of it has gone wherever finished things go. What remains lies before the clerk in a fair hand, and the schoolmaster turns it toward Daniel across the table, and Daniel reads — upside down first, out of habit, then righted by the clerk's careful fingers — his own answers.

They are not his answers. They are in his mouth's grammar, sprinkled with his true words — the landings, the stores, the hours he did keep — and threaded on a spine of confession he never spoke. Meetings he did not attend, arranged on evenings when he was standing landings he can date. An oath he never swore, sworn in a room he has never entered. His exactness has been harvested and rebound. Every date is possible. Every detail is his. The sum is a lie, and it is the best-made lie he has ever read, because he supplied the materials himself, one session at a time, answering the only questions his body left him able to answer.

Sign, the schoolmaster says. Not unkindly. He has never once been unkind, which is a thing Daniel will need years to find the word for.

And here is the fact Daniel will carry out of the room, heavier than the water: he signs.

Not at once. He asks for it to be read aloud, and it is, patiently, every word. He says, once, that it is false, and the schoolmaster nods, and the cloth is not even lifted from its basin — it does not need to be; it needs only to exist, folded, damp, in the corner of his eye. The body decides. The body has been taught what refusing costs, and the body signs, and the hand that cannot initial a false number — the one incorruptible thing he brought east, the talent that made him, the margin that named the porters — moves across the bottom of another man's truth and makes it legal.

They did not know what they were taking. That is what he can never explain afterward, to the Company, to the clerk with the gentleman's word, to anyone but a woman who once watched her own name being written by other hands. The water took hours. The signature took the thing the hours were for.

The pen is sanded. The page is squared away.

Thank you, Mr Vale, the schoolmaster says, and means it.

Daniel is taken back to the cells, and does not speak, and watches, through the high grate, the pens of the yard clerks crossing and recrossing the bright doorway of the governor's office, and cannot stop watching them, and understands that he has left the room and the room has not finished with him, and never will. He has learned to watch pens. It is the room's parting gift, and it is for life.

He does not say Nell's name. He has not said it once, in all the sessions, in any of the spaces the paper left. It is the one entry they did not get, because they did not know to ask for it, and a man can keep what nobody knows to ask for. In the cell, in the dark, he holds the name without saying it even to himself, the way you carry a coal in wet weather.

They have his hand.

They do not have where he keeps her.

***

## IV. Batavia

Some days later — he has lost the count; the days are a smear of grate-light and guard-noise — they come in and look at him.

It is a particular look and he knows it at once, because he has used it. It is the look a factor gives cargo when deciding between columns: sound or spoiled, keep or condemn, this manifest or that one. Two of them stand in the cell door and look at him that way, and confer, low, in Dutch he has learned enough of to follow and wishes he had not.

One of them says a word to the other, and the word is Batavia.

No reason attaches to it. No sentence is read, no mercy pronounced, no explanation offered then or ever. One word, passed between two men the way a bale is passed between two hooks, and the whole remaining shape of Daniel Vale's life is decided in a doorway by an entry he is not shown.

He is taken down to the water stairs at dusk with a guard on either side. The town is out of sight of the path they use, which is deliberate, and the boat is small, and the ship in the roadstead is Dutch and bound west, and nobody tells him any of this; he assembles it, entry by entry, because assembling entries is what is left of him.

From the deck, chained, he watches Amboyna go.

The castle first, pale on its height, its windows catching the last sun. Then the town, the roofs of it, one of which was his. Then the green steepness entire, headed in cloud, and over the water, impossibly, unforgivably, the sweetness — cloves, drying by the hundred thousand trees, the island's breath following the ship out over the roadstead as though nothing whatever had happened in it.

The others are still inside the walls. Towerson is still inside the walls. Daniel does not know what is coming to them, not yet, not for certain — the knowing will reach him later, in another cell, in another language, and take the last thing the room left standing. What he knows now is only the arithmetic of the doorway: they were looked at like cargo, all of them, and the columns were two, and he has been entered in the one that sails.

He is the only Englishman leaving Amboyna by water that week.

He does not yet know what that sentence costs. He will spend three years learning it — what he said, or didn't; what he was worth, or wasn't; why the boat for him — and at the end of the learning there will be no answer, because there was never a reason. There was an entry, made by a clerk with a patient pen, in a ledger he will never see.

The island drops below the sea's edge. The sweetness goes last.

Daniel Vale sits in his chains and begins, without meaning to, to count the ship's bells, and finds that the numbers still will not hold, and closes his eyes, and holds instead the one uncounted thing he saved out of the coral room, and sails west toward the word that was said over him.

# Amboyna (1623)

> **1623**
>
> *“The word arrived before the dead did. The dead never arrived.”*
>
> — Tom Fletcher, Surat, 1623

***

## I. The Word

Amboyna reaches Surat inside a sealed packet.

The ship carrying it has been expected for pepper prices, sailing orders and private letters. Men wait at the landing asking after brothers, debts and promotions. The packet clerk brings the official bundle ashore first, protected beneath waxed cloth while sailors' bags remain in the boat under rain.

The chief factor opens the seal.

He reads standing. Halfway through, he sits.

Nobody in the office asks what has happened. Rank teaches men to wait until bad news has been given a usable shape.

The chief reads the first page again, then the second. His lips move over one word.

Amboyna.

The word passes through the room without information attached. Tom knows the island: cloves, Dutch fort, small English factory, Japanese soldiers in Dutch pay, boats moving between places European maps draw larger than they are. He knows Towerson's name from cargo instructions. He has never met him.

How many? Tom asks.

The chief factor looks at him as though the question has crossed a boundary before permission.

Ten English.

Dead?

Executed.

For what?

Conspiracy against the Dutch castle.

Did they conspire?

The chief looks down at the packet.

They confessed.

That was not the question.

No, the chief says. It was not.

Outside the office, a clerk tells another clerk. A porter hears. By noon the market knows the English say Dutchmen killed English merchants on a clove island. By evening the story has acquired cannon, a siege and fifty dead. None of these travelled in the packet. Horror dislikes an empty column.

Tom does not repeat the number until he sees the list.

***

## II. Three Accounts

The packet contains three versions.

The Dutch account is cleanest.

English factors and Japanese mercenaries planned to seize the castle. Suspicion led to lawful examination. Confessions agreed. Sentence followed under local authority. The writing uses order as proof: discovery, questioning, confession, judgement. Each step stands behind the next so the reader cannot remove one without appearing to attack the whole structure.

The English account is a letter copied in haste before the factory closed. No plot. Arrests at night. Men held apart. Questions whose answers were decided in advance. Water.

The word appears once.

*They used water until the men would own any design put to them.*

Tom touches the scar above his eyebrow.

He reads the sentence again.

The third account is commercial.

It lists English property sealed by Dutch officers, debts likely unrecoverable, stores requiring reassignment and the effect upon clove supply. It was written after the first reports and before the bodies were cold enough for certainty. The final paragraph recommends strengthening English positions where defence is practical and reducing exposure where the Dutch monopoly makes small factories vulnerable.

Surat. Masulipatam. Cotton.

The chief factor finds Tom reading that page.

This is what London will want first, he says.

Before the names?

London will have the names.

First?

The chief is not a cruel man. Cruelty would make the exchange simpler.

Ships move before grief does, he says. If we do not alter the orders now, more men may be stranded.

Tom knows the logic. Weather does not pause because a sailor died. A damaged stay must be replaced before mourning because the next death is already loading it. He has used necessity to move men away from bodies.

The chief is right.

The page is still obscene.

Both facts stand.

Tom lays the commercial account beneath the other two rather than above them.

Paper order is not justice. It is the only correction available in the room.

***

## III. What the Company Does

The Company copies Amboyna.

One copy for London. One for the western factories. One for the ship commanders. Extracts for agents whose work may be affected. A private caution for English merchants dealing under Dutch guns. A shorter version for men not trusted with the names.

Every copy changes the event by choosing what the recipient needs.

Shipmasters receive the warning about Dutch searches and armed forts.

Factors receive the property losses.

Investors will receive the injury to English trade.

Sailors receive a rumour.

Families receive nothing yet because confirmation must precede alarm.

The dead are confirmed enough to redirect cargo and not confirmed enough to tell their wives.

Tom is ordered to inventory every English hand available for reassignment from the eastern stations. Boatswains, gunners, carpenters, clerks able to keep accounts in Portuguese, men who know the island passages. The chief wants readiness if London orders retaliation.

Or withdrawal.

Nobody uses that word.

Tom opens the personnel ledger.

Men become capacities. Able at guns. Experienced in surf. Malay enough for stores. Unfit after fever. Reliable. Difficult. The same categories he has used for years now stand beside Amboyna and reveal their full appetite.

He stops at one young factor newly arrived from England.

The man asks whether the executed Englishmen fought.

The account says there was resistance during arrest.

Then the Dutch had cause?

For arrest?

For concern.

Tom looks at him. Concern is a word power uses before it has chosen the act.

The factor wants to know whether he should accept a posting east.

Do you want it?

I want advancement.

That was not the question.

The echo belongs to Maggie. Tom gives it without explanation.

The man declines the posting.

The chief factor fills it the same afternoon.

One choice left in a man's hands does not stop the machine. It only changes which hand enters the slot.

At dusk the copying clerk brings Tom the extracts for inspection. He has made five versions from
the three accounts, each addressed to a different appetite.

The shipmasters' paper begins with the danger to English persons. The merchants' paper begins with
the seized goods. The paper for London begins with the affront to the nation and ends with a table
of losses so exact it makes rage look audited. A fourth, meant for the Company's servants in India,
asks for vigilance and restraint in the same sentence. The fifth is no more than six lines and will
be read aloud to the crews.

Tom reads it.

It says Englishmen were cruelly put to death after false accusation. It does not say how. It does
not mention the Japanese prisoners. It does not say that the men confessed, because a spoken
confession will travel farther than the means used to obtain it. It ends by assuring the sailors
that the Company will defend its faithful servants.

Will it? Tom asks.

The clerk looks toward the chief factor's closed door.

That is the form.

I asked whether it will.

I know what you asked.

The clerk is older than Tom had noticed. There is ink beneath all ten nails and a tremor in the
finger holding the page. His nephew serves at Masulipatam. The Company has not told him whether the
boy will be brought west or sent farther east to replace one of the dead.

Tom returns the extract.

Read the names before the assurance.

Those are not in the approved form.

Then approve them.

I cannot.

Tom could carry the paper through the door and make the chief answer. He can already feel the old
part of himself choosing the angle: shoulder first, voice low, no room left for retreat. Instead he
pulls a stool to the clerk's side.

Make two copies, he says. One as ordered. One with the names.

Which do we send?

Both.

That will be remarked upon.

Good.

They work by one lamp. The clerk writes while Tom checks the letters against the packet. When the
crew copy is finished, the ten English names stand between the accusation and the promise. The
Japanese remain a number because no other truth has reached the room.

Tom makes the clerk add: *and ten Japanese men whose names are not given in the account.*

It is not much. It is more than the form permits.

***

## IV. The Names

The list arrives two weeks after the first packet.

Towerson stands at the top.

Below him are nine English names with positions attached: factor, assistant, steward, servant. The positions shrink the men even while distinguishing them. Tom copies each name onto a separate page without the office.

The Japanese dead are counted, not named.

Ten Japanese mercenaries, the report says.

Tom asks the messenger whether another list exists.

Not in English hands.

The Dutch must have paid them.

Presumably.

Then there are names in a wage book.

Not in this packet.

Tom leaves ten blank lines below the English names.

The clerk objects that blank space invites later alteration.

That is what it is for.

Tom writes *names not yet recovered* beside the lines and signs beneath the note. Signs late. Signs after reading every word. The habit has become principle so gradually he cannot say where fear ended and judgement began.

A factor passing the desk sees the list.

What is that for?

The dead.

The official copy has them.

It has positions and a number.

What will yours do?

Nothing.

The answer surprises them both.

The page will not punish the Dutch, restore Towerson or give ten Japanese families a body. It may never leave Surat. Tom has learned from Maggie without seeing her: keeping a name is not justice, and calling it justice only steals from the larger debt.

He keeps the page anyway.

***

## V. Grief and Use

Within a month, Amboyna becomes an argument for cotton.

The chief factor does not say the massacre is useful. He says dependence on the spice islands is dangerous. He says the Dutch forts make English investment vulnerable. He says Indian cloth serves markets no single castle can close.

Every statement is true.

Orders expand. Brokers are summoned. Warehouse space is recalculated. Tom's double-tally procedure spreads to two new landings because more bales will pass through them. Men who curse the Dutch in the morning work harder by afternoon on the trade Amboyna has made urgent.

Tom hears Carter in a room eleven thousand miles away, though Carter has not yet heard Daniel speak: grief and use entering together, neither false, the second already consuming the first.

At the Surat table, the men drink to Towerson.

The chief pours Portuguese wine because nobody wants Dutch spirits. One merchant proposes never buying from a Hollander again. Another points out that half the ships carrying their letters east are Dutch or sail under Dutch passes. The vow contracts before the cup is empty.

Tom drinks water.

The young factor who declined the eastern posting asks what revenge would look like.

War, somebody says.

English forts.

Dutch blood.

A monopoly of our own.

The last answer is offered as a joke. Nobody laughs quickly enough.

Tom thinks of the rice thief at the warehouse door, Yusuf beside the false tally, Jack's half-wages disappearing between correct books. A system does not become just because the flag over it changes.

He touches the scar.

The factor asks what he would do.

Find out what happened.

We know.

We have three accounts.

They agree men died.

That is not the disputed part.

Later, in the warehouse, Tom watches the first consequence arrive in cloth.

Ratan's men bring blue cotton under tarred covers, twice the quantity ordered the month before.
The bales are set on the dry boards, opened, sampled and entered. One square carries a red climbing
flower close enough to the cloth beneath Maggie's bar that Tom's hand stops above it.

Ratan sees.

You know the pattern.

I know one like it.

English buyers ask for more now, Ratan says. They say the islands are closed.

Men are dead.

That also.

Ratan does not apologize. He has dyers to pay, printers waiting on carved blocks, carriers who must
eat whether English grief is pure or profitable. Tom has known him too long to mistake commerce for
indifference. Ratan touches the sampled edge.

If I refuse the order, will they live?

No.

If you refuse the cloth, will they live?

No.

Then we should make the account honest.

Together they reject three damp bales, correct two false weights and add the printers' rush fee as
its own line instead of burying it in the broker's charge. The dead remain dead. The living are
paid a little more plainly because of them.

Tom signs only after Ratan reads the total back.

On the quay, another factor tells him that Amboyna may be the making of Surat.

Tom turns so slowly the man hears his own sentence a second time.

I mean the trade here.

I know what you mean.

The factor retreats into talk of tonnage. Tom lets him. Striking him would cleanse one sentence and
leave the appetite that produced it untouched.

That night Tom writes no letter to Maggie. He places a clean sheet beside the Amboyna copies and
imagines telling her that ten men have died, ten others have died without names, and the warehouse
is busier. He cannot find an order for those truths that does not turn one into an explanation of
another.

He folds the blank sheet once and keeps it.

***

## VI. One Retained

The fourth account comes from Bantam in a packet without Company seal.

It is a copy of a harbour issue: provisions for prisoners transferred after the Amboyna examinations. Rice, water, guards, boat hire. The page has travelled through private merchant hands because official routes have begun swallowing anything that complicates the clean versions.

Tom reads the quantities first.

Enough rice for more men than the Dutch account says were held after sentence.

At the bottom is a note:

*One English factor retained for further examination and sent under guard to Batavia.*

No name.

No condition.

No explanation of why one man enters a boat while the others enter graves.

The chief factor says the note may be an error.

Then we correct it.

How?

Ask Batavia.

Through whom? The Dutch authorities accused our factory of conspiracy and executed the men responsible for answering letters.

Private ships still go.

Private ships carry private risk.

Tom looks at the page. Somewhere east, one man may be alive because a clerk chose *retained* instead of *condemned*. Or dead because the next page has not reached them. The difference is an entry moving between offices.

He thinks of the unsigned letter beneath his mother's death notice. Paper kept from travelling can imprison the living as completely as paper sent.

The chief tells him London will inquire.

How long?

Months.

Tom puts two fingers above his eyebrow.

The chief recognizes the gesture now.

What are you proposing?

Nothing yet.

This is true. It is also the answer Tom gives when action has begun before he can name it.

He makes a copy of the transfer note.

On his private list, beneath Towerson and the nine English dead, beneath the ten blank Japanese lines, he adds one more:

*English factor. Name unknown. Batavia.*

The word has travelled from Amboyna to Surat.

Now Tom begins travelling toward the word.

# The Widow's Years (1603–1623)

> **1603–1623**
>
> *“Some accounts are kept because payment is coming. Some are kept so nobody can say nothing was owed.”*
>
> — Anne Bell, Shadwell, 1623

***

## I. Longer Than a Wife

By 1623 Anne has been Matthew Bell's widow for twenty years, which is longer than she was his wife and nearly long enough for people to think widow is a trade.

Perhaps it is.

It has tools. A key worn flat at one tooth. A rent book with the first landlord's name struck through and his son's written above it. A good black dress that was black before Matthew died and has now been turned once at the cuffs. A way of standing inside an open door that makes a collector understand he has been admitted and may therefore be put out.

Both girls are women. The elder reads faster than Anne and keeps figures in a hand that begins like her father's before slanting stubbornly into its own. The younger remembers every face that has asked for money and none of the reasons offered. They earn. They contribute. Anne still counts the household as three because survival has taught her that independence is often several people refusing to let one another fall.

On Thursday afternoons she walks from Shadwell to the Pelican.

Maggie puts broth at the end of the bar without asking whether Anne has eaten. Anne brings whatever the house has sent home with her since the week before: an apron with a new tie, a shirt cuff turned, a rent demand read twice, a name remembered from a paid-off crew. They do not call these visits. A visit is what a woman makes when she has time to sit.

Today Anne carries three papers tied in thread and a child's shoe with the sole opening at the toe.

Maggie looks at the shoe.

Yours are grown.

Not every child is mine.

Maggie puts the bowl down first.

***

## II. The First Winter

Their friendship begins with food neither woman agrees to owe.

In the first winter after Matthew's death, Maggie brings barley broth to Shadwell every third day. Anne returns each pot clean with something repaired inside it. A split apron. A scorched cloth. Once, the tavern's best sheet, torn by a sailor who mistakes an upstairs room for a ship in weather and attempts to reef the bed.

Maggie sends a loaf with the next pot.

Anne sends back two pewter buttons and a note in the elder girl's large hand: *Sheet mended. Buttons supplied. One loaf too much.*

Maggie tears off the last sentence and uses it to light the taproom candles. Then she sends dripping and two onions.

Neither wins. That is why it lasts.

The girls eat before the women discuss Matthew. At first Anne permits no other order. A child who has watched adults lower their voices around her learns to hear death in an empty stomach. Maggie understands this without being taught. She arrives, fills bowls, waits for spoons to scrape, and only then reports what the parish has done with Rook's name or what somebody at the stairs claims to know.

Most reports are useless. Silas seen at Deptford. Silas on a collier north. Silas drowned off Gravesend. One week he is three places and dead in two. Anne asks who saw him. The answer is always a man who heard another man.

Do you want me to keep bringing the stories? Maggie asks.

No.

The next week Anne says, If a story has a name inside it, bring the name.

That becomes another procedure.

Maggie hears the river. Anne tests what it says. A name, a ship, a date, a person who will repeat it sober. If one part is missing, the story does not enter the house.

Years later, when nobody has brought anything true, Anne understands that waiting for news of a murderer has been eating time the girls can use. She stops asking.

Maggie stops bringing it.

Their friendship survives the loss of its first subject.

***

## III. What Women Charge

The account between them grows complicated because neither will put a price on the work that matters.

Anne will price six inches of hemming, a morning spent reading a lease and the ink needed to copy a demand. She will not price sitting beside Maggie when the anniversary of a Swedish son's impressment comes round and Maggie wipes the same clean place on the counter until the grain rises.

Maggie will price ale, pie, a room upstairs and broth carried out in a covered pot. She will not price the night the younger Bell girl burns with fever and Maggie sits at the pallet changing cloths while Anne sleeps for the first two hours in three days.

The fever comes in the ninth winter.

Coal costs more that week because the wind has pinned barges below the bridge. The physician wants payment before he enters. Anne pays him and knows at once there will be no meat until Sunday. Maggie arrives with beef bones, leeks and half a sack of coal contributed, she says, by a customer who lost a wager.

What wager?

That I would ask permission.

Anne is too tired to refuse properly.

The child lives. Afterwards Anne enters every farthing in the household book: physician, coal, linen spoiled, two days' work lost by Joan, candle ends, vinegar. Beside Maggie's name she leaves a blank.

Joan finds it.

What goes there?

Nothing she would accept.

Then why write her name?

So I remember the blank belongs to somebody.

The same blank appears in Maggie's reckoning. Anne never sees that book because it is not a book. It is a crowd of cuts beneath the bar, keepsakes in a fault in the wood, Bell's page folded beyond spilled ale. Yet Maggie knows which winter Anne misses Thursday because the rent rises, which girl needs shoes, which neighbour has taken ill and therefore which household Anne is quietly feeding in addition to her own.

Along the Wall, the same work passes hand to hand without ever entering a wage book. Hours at a sickbed. Children minded while a mother works. Washing laid out for a man who returns without wages. A loaf divided one place farther than the baker intended.

***

## IV. The Name Maggie Spends

In 1612 Maggie comes to Shadwell after closing, anger still making her hands exact.

Anne opens the door. Maggie has brought the last quarter of a pie under a cloth.

Who ate?

Tom ate first. The boy ate most of what remained.

Which boy?

Maggie tells her about Jack, the injured father, Carter's debt and Tom's berth. She tells her what she called Tom before putting him out.

Anne waits until the pie is warm again. The girls are nearly grown now and need no instruction to lay plates. Anne and both girls eat. Maggie does not. Anne cuts the remaining crust in half and leaves one piece before her until she does.

Only then does Anne say, He is not Matthew.

I called him Harcourt.

You used Matthew's death to sharpen the name.

He used the same words. The boy would sign with or without him.

That was Matthew's lie too.

Then I was right.

Anne folds her hands around the cup.

Right is not the same as exact. Harcourt made the paper hungry. Matthew fed it six years and called himself a hand. Tom found a boy already inside it and called the taking protection. I hear the likeness. I also hear where it ends.

Maggie's face closes.

You want me to fetch him back.

I do not want a grand act made for my benefit. You taught him the difference and put him out. Let the difference work if it can. But do not turn my dead husband into a cudgel because you are frightened of losing a living man.

Maggie looks down at her empty hands.

Too late.

For the words, yes.

And the rest?

Anne takes another bite of pie.

The rest usually takes longer.

Maggie does not ask forgiveness. Anne does not offer it. When she leaves, the empty pie dish is clean.

***

## V. Claims

By 1623 women come to Anne with papers because she is slow enough to distrust every line and quick enough to know which line will eat.

She is not a scrivener. A scrivener charges for words whether they help or not. Anne charges for the hour when the woman can pay and enters the hour when she cannot.

The child's shoe belongs to a sailor's wife from Ratcliff whose husband died homeward of fever. The Company has accepted the death and retained the wages pending proof that the claimant is his lawful widow. The two facts have occupied adjacent desks for five months without meeting.

The wife cannot leave three children alone to wait at the pay office. She cannot take them because the youngest cries indoors and clerks use noise as a reason to move poor people outside. She has asked Anne to read the demand. The refusal sends her toward another desk. Anne will not send her alone.

At the Pelican, Anne eats her broth. Then she unties the three papers.

One is a marriage line from the parish. One is a pay note carrying the dead man's mark. One is the Company's refusal, which does not refuse. It requests further satisfaction as to identity and leaves the kind of satisfaction blank.

Maggie reads over Anne's shoulder.

What do they want?

For her to tire.

Can she prove he drank here?

Drinking is not marriage.

It is to some men.

Maggie remembers two sailors paid off the dead man's ship. One lodges near Limehouse. The other works a lighter with Ned. Both saw the wife at the ship before sailing. One carried a letter from the husband to her on the outward leg and can identify the direction.

Anne turns over the Company's paper and writes five headings in the clean spaces: name, ship, date, knowledge, address.

Joan will make a fair copy tonight. Maggie will send word to the two men. Anne will take the wife to the pay office at opening, before a clerk has accumulated enough unfinished work to make another woman's claim feel like an offence.

And the shoe? Maggie asks.

The child must wear something while we wait.

Maggie measures the split sole against her palm and calls upstairs for Joan, who knows a cobbler willing to mend children's shoes in exchange for dinners he pretends not to need.

The chain forms without a committee. Tavern, widow, daughter, lighterman, witnesses, cobbler, dinner. None of it appears in the Company's account of wages eventually released.

That account will say the widow supplied sufficient proof.

***

## VI. Nobody Pays

They finish near dusk.

The two sailors have come, eaten and put their marks beneath statements Joan Bell reads aloud. The shoe has gone to the cobbler. The children and their mother are in Anne's rooms with stew enough for the evening. At first light Anne opens her own door, leaves the two older children with her daughters and takes the youngest and his mother to the pay office.

They arrive before the office door is unbarred and find twelve women ahead of them.

Nobody has been given an order. The order exists anyway, marked by shoulders against the wall, baskets at heels, children on a folded cloak and a baby asleep inside a shawl. A woman darning a stocking tells Anne to stand after her and before the blue basket. The basket guards a place no clerk recognises and every woman present will defend.

When the door opens, the line becomes a room.

It is narrower than the number of claims requiring it. Plaster has fallen from one corner in thin shells, exposing the lath beneath. The wall behind the benches is polished dark at shoulder height by coats that have waited there. A cold hearth holds yesterday's ash. One high window admits light without warmth, and beneath it a clerk's rail divides the people who have brought papers from the people permitted to delay them.

There are two benches. The women fit twenty bodies onto space intended for twelve, children counted as laps until they refuse. The Ratcliff widow sits with the youngest on her knees. Anne stands behind her and keeps one hand on the packet inside her coat.

The clerk does not appear with the door. A boy enters, puts an inkstand on the desk and goes away. Another man crosses the rear room carrying a book, sees the waiting women and closes the inner door. The room hears a bolt settle. Nobody complains. Complaint this early spends strength before it can purchase anything.

The woman with the sailor's cap resumes her stocking. Beside her, another turns the frayed edge of a cuff under with a thumbnail and holds it there until the first woman passes the needle. Thread travels down the bench. A woman near the cold hearth has brought a square of sailcloth and cuts patches from it for three different knees. She takes no coin. One of the mothers keeps the offcuts for soles.

Anne has brought work too: a child's shirt with one sleeve opening at the seam. She lays it across her forearm and begins. The Ratcliff widow looks at her.

You expected this long?

I expected them to hope for longer.

The woman with the stocking glances over.

Keep the marriage line in your own hand until he finds the death, she says. Once both are behind the rail, they can lose them together.

Anne nods. She had planned the same. She had not known the sentence belonged to others.

The women teach without announcing a lesson. The clerk nearest the window reads marks but dislikes seals broken at the edge. The clerk at the inner door will say a ship's book has not arrived when he means it is on the shelf behind him and he does not want to climb. If a claim is refused, ask him to write what remains wanting. If he will not write it, repeat the missing thing loudly enough for the next woman to hear. Never surrender the parish original when a fair copy will answer. Never say a husband had debts until the wage is acknowledged, because a debt waits more patiently than a widow and will be admitted first if invited.

Anne listens. She has spent twenty years learning how one man's paper eats. The room has learned how a hundred papers chew.

By midmorning the youngest child has cried himself hot. His mother apologises. Anne takes him, and the woman cutting sailcloth gives him her wooden bobbin. When he drops it for the third time, a girl offers a crust in exchange. He accepts with a merchant's gravity.

Food changes the mother before it changes the child. Anne sees her watching the crust.

She takes bread and a cold onion from her bag. Maggie packed both without asking who would eat them. Anne breaks the bread, gives the larger part to the widow and keeps the onion between them.

I am not hungry, the widow says.

That is useful. Eat before it passes.

The woman obeys. Around them other packets open without ceremony: an apple divided four ways, cheese pared thin, a heel softened in a cup for the baby. No meal is offered to the room. Every person merely discovers that what she has brought reaches one place farther than intended.

At last the Ratcliff widow is called.

Anne goes to the rail with her. The clerk looks first at Anne, who is older and holding the packet, and directs the question to her.

Your husband's ship?

Her husband, Anne says, and moves half a step aside.

The widow names the ship herself.

The clerk finds the death in a pay book. The entry is one line: name, rating, wages accrued, fever, dead homeward. A man's last months have been made short enough to fit beneath a ruler. The clerk touches it, then asks for proof of marriage.

The widow holds out the fair copy Joan made. Anne retains the parish line.

Original, the clerk says.

Upon acceptance of the copy.

I cannot accept what I cannot compare.

Anne shows him the original without releasing it. He compares the hands, annoyed by the distance of one arm. Then he asks who knows the claimant to be the same woman.

Anne places the two sailors' statements on the rail. Their marks stand beneath words read aloud before they touched ink; their addresses and shipboard knowledge are entered beside them. One saw the widow at the sailing. One carried her husband's letter outward.

They are not present, the clerk says.

Your request did not require their bodies.

It required satisfaction.

What part remains unsatisfied?

The clerk turns the refusal over as though its blank back may contain the answer his office omitted five months ago. Behind Anne, the room continues its work. Cloth draws through fingers. A child whispers a counting game. The woman with the blue basket has returned and the place kept for her has closed neatly around her skirts.

The muster must be compared with the pay ledger, the clerk says.

Compare it.

The ledger is not in this room.

Where is it?

In examination.

Anne looks past him. A broad book rests on the shelf beside the inner door, its leather back lettered with the dead ship's name.

Is examination the shelf behind you?

He turns before he can stop himself. The room sees. A small sound moves along the bench, not quite laughter and more expensive to the clerk.

He takes down the book.

The two entries agree. That should finish it. Instead he says the officer authorised to release the wage has gone to dinner.

When will he return?

When his dinner is finished.

The clerk closes the pay book with the widow's proof still beneath his hand.

Anne does not move from the rail.

Enter that the proofs have been received.

The claim is not concluded.

Then enter what has happened, not what has not.

He tells her to sit. She tells him she will sit when the papers are back in the widow's hand. The room does not look up. That is its assistance: twenty witnesses refusing the appearance of a disturbance while making it impossible for him to pocket the pages unnoticed.

He returns the originals and writes a receipt for the copies. Anne reads it to the last word before yielding the space at the rail.

The authorised officer's dinner lasts through the next bell and part of the one after. The benches exchange occupants but not knowledge. A mother carries another woman's baby to the yard. Anne closes the shirt sleeve. The Ratcliff widow learns which pay day begins before sunrise and which entrance opens first, then repeats both facts to a younger woman.

When the inner door finally opens, no heralding occurs. A fed man sits. The clerk places three accepted claims before him and seven to one side. Anne watches the Ratcliff papers enter the smaller pile.

The officer reads the witness marks, checks the matching lines and signs. Payment will not be made today. The wages must be drawn against the next appointed issue, less the dead man's recorded advances and any lawful claim already lodged. The widow's face empties at the first sentence and begins returning at the second. Delay has changed shape. It now has a date.

Anne asks for the date on the receipt.

The officer looks at the clerk. The clerk looks at the room. He adds it.

They leave with no coin. The youngest sleeps against his mother's shoulder. When the widow begins to thank Anne and cannot finish without crying, Anne gives her the packet to carry. Work steadies the hands better than consolation.

I know the entrance now, the widow says. And the hour. And not to give them the parish line.

She has learned the room's account. She will bring it back when another woman asks.

By the time Anne returns to the Pelican, the light is going. Maggie wipes the end of the bar.

What do I owe you? Anne asks.

For what?

The witnesses ate.

They were hungry.

Joan's time.

Joan's time is Joan's to price.

The broth.

You mended the cellar apron.

That was last Thursday.

Then we are behind.

They have been behind for twenty years.

Anne takes out her household book. Matthew's name appears on its earliest pages in the small entries of a living man: coal carried, candles bought, twelve shillings received, sixpence missing and found in the other coat. After his death the entries continue without him. Rent. Bread. Shoes. Fever. Work. Two daughters becoming women one paid hour at a time.

Maggie looks at the narrow columns.

Do you still reckon what his death cost?

No.

Why keep it, then?

Anne closes the book.

Some accounts are kept because payment is coming. Some are kept so nobody can say nothing was owed.

Beneath Maggie's hand, behind the oak, Matthew's other account remains folded in the dark. It does not absolve him. Anne's household book does not condemn him. The two pages perform different work and neither is asked to finish the dead.

Outside, a woman knocks and waits because she has not yet learned that the Pelican permits women with urgent papers to use the latch.

Maggie reaches for it.

Anne is closer.

I can manage a door, she says, and opens it herself.

# Batavia (1623–1626)

> **1623–1626**
>
> *“The man is the message.”*
>
> — Mara de Sousa, Batavia, 1625

***

## I. The Unclosed Entry

The desk that was too heavy for one man to take crosses the water in a Company hull, freight paid at Mara's own rate.

Six men carry it up from the Batavia quay. The joiner who built it is twenty years and a sea away; the top still does not rock. She sets it in a ground-floor room within sight of the castle, near enough that the castle's clerks can reach her without deciding the walk means anything, and stretches the old string across the new walls. One cord she pins exactly as it travelled: its far end beneath a copy of a harbour list, its near end beneath a clerk's transfer line.

*One English factor retained for examination and onward account. D. Vale.*

The entry is two years old now. Mara has watched it renew itself quarter by quarter in the castle's provision books — rice, water, guard wages, a surgeon's visit charged and initialled — and in all that time no examination has been attached to it. Nobody examines Daniel Vale. Nobody releases him. The entry has outlived its reason the way debts outlived their men in Harcourt's books, and for the same cause: closing it would require somebody to write down why it was opened.

She goes to the cells once, early, before she spends anything.

Names can be forged. She has forged enough to know. She stands in the visiting room with a castle clerk beside her and asks the thin Englishman to write his name, and watches the letters come cramped and hard into the page, the nib catching on the downstroke of the V exactly as it caught on a rice account four years ago. The hand is the one she circled. The man attached to it watches the pen the whole time it is in her fingers, and does not once look at the door.

You asked me a question once, she says in English. Which report to keep, when two true reports disagree.

The Englishman's eyes come up from the pen very slowly.

Both, he says. The voice is a ruin. You wrote: ownership is the dispute.

Do not edit it out, Mara says.

It is all the introduction either of them makes. It is enough. He knows her the way she knows him — by the writing, which is the only part of a person paper ever honestly carries.

After that she goes to work on the entry.

She does not ask the fiscal for mercy. Mercy is a favour, favours are debts, and a debt held by a powerful man is a leash. Instead she brings him arithmetic. The commissioners in Europe are pricing Amboyna between the two Companies, claim against counter-claim, and every quarter the castle feeds an Englishman it has neither tried nor released, its own provision book adds a line England's lawyers will one day read aloud. A prisoner without a charge is not a prisoner. He is an unclosed entry, renewing itself against the Company's account.

Close it while closing is cheap, she says.

The fiscal reads the assembled pages twice. Released to whom? he asks. A body must be received. I will not have him drown in the roads and become a second claim.

An English signature and an English deck, Mara says.

Then find them, the fiscal says, and believes he has ended the matter.

She reads the roads. English hulls are rare at Batavia now; the English council keeps its house and its protests and sends its ships to Bantam. But one has come in at last, carrying letters too angry to trust to a Dutch bottom, and when Mara asks the harbour clerk who keeps her deck, the clerk shrugs and gives her the only name a deck requires.

The boatswain.

***

## II. The Claim

It has taken Tom fourteen months to travel toward one line of harbour ink.

He volunteers for the runs other men avoid, Surat to Bantam through a sea gone official with other people's guns, and at Bantam he volunteers again, and by the time the council's letters want carrying to Batavia there is no cheaper way to move them than the boatswain who keeps asking. Nobody wonders at it. The Company has learned that Tom goes where the work is worst, and has long since stopped looking for the reason, which suits the reason.

The English house at Batavia is a claim with rooms attached.

Clerks compute it daily: ten men, their wages, their goods, their seized stores, the insult to the nation entered under separate cover. The dead have become exact. Tom stands at a rail of ink and asks the question he has carried from Surat.

The retained factor. The one sent here under guard.

The council man does not look up. That matter is not helpful.

He may be alive.

That is what is not helpful about him. The man signed a Dutch confession. Produce him, and the Dutch produce the signature, and half the claim begins to argue with itself. Ten dead men make a massacre. Nine dead men and a witness under water make a proceeding.

He says it without cruelty, which is the worst of it. Harcourt's voice needed no cruelty either. Tom looks at the clean columns and understands that he has crossed half the world to stand in the same counting room he left, and that his own flag is flying over it.

He has corrected the machine for twenty years, one line at a time. He has never before heard his own side price a living man below a dead one and call the price patriotism.

Where is he held? Tom asks.

The council man finally looks up. Leave it, boatswain. The matter is being managed.

By whom?

That evening a boy stops him in the lane behind the godowns and puts a folded paper into his hand. No seal. No name. An hour, a street, a door — which in Batavia is a name, and the council's clerks have already taught him whose. There is a broker the castle listens to, they say, when they think he is not listening. A woman. She sets her own rate and the fiscal pays it.

Tom reads the paper twice, the habit of a man who signs late.

Then he goes.

***

## III. The Counting Room

The room is long and low, strung with cords like rigging scaled for a chart table, and smells of ink, sandalwood and the harbour. Tom checks it from the threshold the way he checks every room, takes the doorway out of twenty-two years' habit — and stops, because behind a heavy teak desk, in dark silk that holds blue where the lamp finds it, sits a woman he has seen once in a dream he never told anyone, standing behind a bar she never stood behind, bolting a door from within.

She looks at him. He looks at her.

The scar through his eyebrow is new to her. The way he has put himself in the door is not. She has seen a man stand exactly so, half a head shorter than what was coming, bleeding and staying — and he has seen this stillness before, this way of watching a room decide, from behind a counter, beside a mallet, a lifetime ago, in the breath before one word.

One long look. Not love. Recognition.

Neither names it. He knows her name — it has lain under a bar in Wapping for twenty-two years in a dead man's hand — and he does not spend it. She never had his. A deck's name is enough, and she has been priced too often herself to price a man past what the work requires.

You are the boatswain, she says.

You are the broker.

Sit down, she says, and he does, and the night they are not speaking of sits down with them.

She lays two papers on the teak, squared to the edge. The first is Dutch: an order of release, sealed, wanting only the receiving signature. The second is her own — a passage account, terms exact — and where the passenger's name stands, Tom stops reading.

He takes his private list from inside his coat. Below Towerson and the nine, below ten blank lines, stands his last entry: *English factor. Name unknown. Batavia.* He borrows her pen without asking, crosses out three words and writes two.

Daniel Vale.

Mara watches the correction the way Maggie once watched a hinge repaired: as the nearest some people come to saying what they mean.

Your council would not give you the name, she says.

My council is adding him up.

I know. I read their sums. She turns the release a half-inch straighter. That is why the paper wants a deck and not a house. He is released to a signature, not to the claim. Once the claim has him, he becomes evidence, and evidence is kept, and I have seen what keeping does to that man.

What is your rate? Tom asks.

There is no rate. Some work I pay for myself.

He looks at her then, properly, and the question he cannot ask — *why* — stands in the room with all the other things standing there. Mara answers the askable part.

There is a room in London that reads accounts truly, she says. I have carried that fact twenty-two years. It is the most valuable thing I own, and I am spending it once. A tavern on Wapping Wall. The Pelican. When England lets him ashore, take him through that door and give him to the keeper.

I know the house, Tom says.

Yes, Mara says. I thought the deck might.

Nothing else is said about it. Nothing else needs surviving.

She rises, and from a shelf behind the desk takes a squat stone bottle, and uncorks it, and pours one measured pull into a cup, and sets it in front of him. Tom drinks. Juniper, fire, pine resin — his eyes water, and the woman in dark silk does not laugh, and for a moment the room is full of everyone who has ever handed this particular fire across this particular distance.

For the passage, she says, corking the bottle with her thumb. Courage enough to reach the river. After that he will need his own.

At the door Tom pauses, hand on the latch, and does not look back, and goes. Mara stands a while at the desk. Then she writes the evening's last entry in the book no clerk will ever audit, where the room in Wapping has stood as an asset for twenty-two years, and marks it, for the first time, partly drawn against.

***

## IV. The Man Who Reads

The release is an afternoon of pens.

A clerk reads the entry aloud, all eleven words, twice — once for the castle's book and once for the receiving signature — and Daniel stands between two guards in clothes somebody has found for him, cut for a bigger man, and watches the pen the way he watches all pens now, and waits to learn which column he is being moved to.

The receiving signature belongs to an English boatswain: a scarred, weathered man of middle years who fills the doorway of the office without seeming to notice he is doing it. The clerk pushes the paper at him, sand ready, in the manner of clerks everywhere — sign here, receive the person — and the boatswain does a thing Daniel has not seen done in three years, in two Companies, in any language.

He reads it.

All of it, to the end, at his own pace, while the sand waits and the guards shift and the afternoon creaks. Then the page above it. Then, only then, and slowly, he signs.

Daniel has watched many men sign. He has never once, since the coral room, watched a man read first, and something in him that has been braced since dawn — braced, if he is honest, since a schoolmaster's voice first said *session* — lets down by a fraction of an inch. Whoever this man belongs to, whatever ledger is moving him, he is not the pen's servant. The pen waits for him.

At the gate the boatswain steps into the doorway — Daniel's chest closes; a man in a doorway is a fact his body has opinions about — and then, catching himself on some instruction Daniel cannot see, steps aside, and leaves the opening empty, and waits.

Daniel does not move. An open door held open by a stranger is still a door and still a stranger.

The boatswain does not explain, or reassure, or use the word free, which would have finished it. He says: The boat is at the third stair. Can you walk that far?

Yes, Daniel says.

Then that is all we are doing today.

It is the exact size of a sentence a man can obey. Daniel walks it. On the deck a sailor swings the water dipper toward him and his hands begin before he can govern them — both hands, flat, shaking, the old animal shriek rising silent through his arms — and the scarred man takes the dipper away without hurry, as though it were a fouled line, and puts his own small beer in Daniel's grip instead, and sends the sailor forward. After that, no one swings the water dipper toward Daniel again, and no one says why not, and no one looks at him when he drinks.

The place given him aboard is not quite a cabin. A partition makes three walls beneath the quarterdeck; a light hinged screen supplies the fourth and is lifted aside whenever men carry stores through. There is a narrow berth, a chest whose owner has gone forward for the passage, and a chair that faces the closed screen.

Daniel stops at the opening.

The boatswain goes in first. He does not test the bed or make a show of finding danger absent. He takes the chair by its back, turns it until a seated man can see the opening and the strip of deck beyond, and wedges one rear leg beneath the chest batten so the roll will not turn it back. Then he leaves.

Daniel sits.

The first food comes before dusk. Rice, fish broth and a piece of shore bread lie on a board set within reach of the chair. The cup has a wooden cover fitted over it. No hand carries the meal into the enclosure. A sailor puts the board down outside, pushes it across with one foot and continues aft with a second bowl, as though Daniel's supper is one delivery among several and therefore contains no question.

Daniel waits until the deck has changed voices before he eats.

The broth is warm. He takes three spoonfuls, then bread, then the rest of the broth. The rice defeats him. It remains on the board when the sailor returns. The man looks at the bowl, looks toward the galley and takes it away without asking whether Daniel disliked it, whether he is ill, whether the Dutch fed him rice, whether a man released from three years of rooms ought to show more gratitude for food.

At morning the portion is smaller.

That is how the deck learns him: by subtraction without remark. Half a bowl. One piece of bread. Broth cloudy enough not to shine back at him. When the shore bread is gone, ship's biscuit is laid in the broth until the edge yields to the spoon. The covered cup appears on the left of the board each time, always in the same place, so his hand can find it before his eyes must.

At first the cup holds small beer. Later, when the beer sours and is not improved by being called beer, there is thin barley broth, water clouded with rice, or fresh water carried under the cover and left until he is alone. At Bantam the casks are filled again. The drink changes. The cup does not.

No order establishes this. Daniel listens for one. He listens for his name in the boatswain's voice, for the explanation that will turn the arrangement into a favour and the favour into a debt. He hears orders for braces, casks, watches, a boat warped closer under the quarter. He hears a sailor corrected for leaving a coil where a running foot can find it. He never hears himself discussed.

The men announce themselves through work. A hand comes for the borrowed chest and knocks once on the partition before entering. The sailmaker needs the deck space and tells Daniel which way the rolled canvas will pass. A boy sweeping under the berth pushes dust into a pile, sees Daniel's feet and says only lift, when you can. Nothing in the words asks him to be well.

On the third night the lantern outside the partition begins striking its bracket whenever the ship rolls. Iron against iron. A small, exact blow, pause, return. Daniel counts twenty-seven before he understands that he is counting. At thirty-four the boatswain arrives, takes down the lantern and carries it away.

Dark is not better.

Daniel sits with both hands on the chair and hears the deck continue without the light. Nobody comes to explain the removal. Some time later the boatswain returns with the catch newly pinned and a strip of leather between bracket and frame. He hangs the lantern, tests the swing once and goes. The light remains. The striking does not.

During foul weather the opening must be closed against spray. The first time a sailor reaches for the partition, Daniel is out of the chair before either of them knows he has moved. The sailor stops with the hook in his hand.

Rain coming aft, he says.

Daniel can see it beyond him, grey and hard.

Leave a hand's width, the boatswain says from somewhere on deck.

The sailor secures the screen with a gap at the hinge. Rain works through and darkens the floor. Daniel watches the narrow line of the deck through it until the squall passes. When the screen is opened again, no one congratulates him for having remained.

The passage makes no steady improvement. One day he eats at every issue and stands for an hour in the open. The next he cannot cross the partition and the food stays until flies find it. A week after that he walks to the rail, sees a sailor rinsing a bucket and returns so quickly he strikes his shoulder on the chair. The boatswain moves the bucket. He does not move the sea.

Daniel begins to understand that this is the limit and the mercy of the arrangement. The deck cannot remove water, doors, hands, orders or men standing over other men. It can put a cover on a cup. Turn a chair. Leave food within reach. Repair one striking lamp. Make the next necessary thing small enough to take without promising what comes after it.

A deck, it turns out, has better manners than a court.

He never asks who paid for him. He has assembled it, entry by entry, the only way he knows: the woman who verified his hand; the boatswain who reads before signing; two exact people, strung together across water by paper he cannot see. Somewhere in the world there is an account with his name in it, kept by hands that name their porters.

It is not freedom. It is the next thing to it: being carried by people who keep true books.

***

That night, in a ground-floor room within sight of the castle, Mara unpins one cord from her wall and rolls the string small. The wall keeps the pinhole. The account it belonged to is not closed — she has stopped believing accounts close — but it has moved, at last, out of the columns that were killing it, onto water, going west.

The man is the message. She has sent the room back one of its debts.

***

## V. The Desk

The Company receives him at Surat the way it receives everything. It finds him a column.

Before the column, the ship must give him back.

At the outer roadstead the room beneath the quarterdeck is dismantled around him. The borrowed chest returns to its owner. The chair is pulled free of the batten and carried aft. A sailor lifts the screen from its hooks so cargo can pass, and in one movement Daniel's fourth wall becomes two boards in a man's arms. The covered cup goes to the galley. The strip of leather stays beneath the lantern catch, indistinguishable from every repair aboard.

Nothing that helped him has been entered as his property. Every part must remain with the ship.

Daniel watches the familiar arrangement become equipment again. He understood it was temporary. Understanding does not prevent his body from reading the cleared space as eviction.

Tom brings no reassurance. He gives Daniel the next action.

The ladder first. Then the boat. Nothing beyond it until we are aboard.

Daniel can see the ladder. That makes the order possible.

Men go down ahead with mail, the surgeon's box and two water casks needed ashore before the tide turns. Nobody waits beneath Daniel with arms raised. Tom descends first and sits where Daniel can see him, one hand on the gunwale, looking toward the river instead of upward. Daniel takes each rung at the speed his feet permit. The men loading the casks work around that speed as though the delay belongs to tide and tackle, not to him.

In the lighter, a jar of water stands beneath a cloth. The boatman reaches for its cup, sees Tom's hand move once across his knee and pours for himself instead. Daniel's covered cup has come down separately in a small provision basket. No order is spoken. The small accommodation crosses from one crew to another because a man notices, a passage more fragile than any paper Mara has paid to send.

The river narrows. Heat gathers beneath the coat Daniel was given in Batavia, but he cannot yet make himself remove it. Villages, palms and low muddy landings move past at the speed of oars. The open banks should feel like release after stone. Instead there is nowhere for the eye to stop. He watches the provision basket between his shoes until the factory stairs rise ahead.

A receiving clerk waits beneath an awning with a list. He asks the ship, the date, Daniel's rating and what effects accompany him. The questions are ordinary. Daniel reaches the last and cannot answer. He had a sea chest at Amboyna. Shirts. Letters from Nell. A deal box of copied pages. The Dutch made each object evidence, property or ash. None has crossed with him.

No effects, Tom says.

The clerk looks at the bigger man's coat and asks whether it is issued clothing.

Loaned for the passage.

To be returned?

When he has another.

The clerk leaves the recovery line blank. Daniel watches the space remain open and waits for somebody to put a price into it. Nobody does.

Tom does not carry him up the stairs. He walks one step below, near enough to stop a fall and far enough that Daniel never feels a hand deciding his direction. At the top, a Company servant points toward the factory and begins to explain the room, allowance and duties prepared for the returned factor.

Tom lets the explanation pass. The chair, cup and patched lantern could not come ashore as Daniel's possessions. Their lesson can. A useful arrangement is not the object. It is the person who notices what the object must do.

Released is not a station, so a desk is. A ledger, an allowance set against his back wages, a room that holds the heat the way the cells held the damp. Around him, a factory of men who have been advised — in the wordless way such advice travels — that the word Amboyna is not helpful, and who therefore say it never, which means Daniel lives inside the word entire, since it is the shape of every silence aimed at him.

On the first morning the desk faces the wall and the chair leaves the door behind Daniel's right shoulder. He stands beside it without removing his coat. Clerks pass, glance and find reasons to pass again. The scarred boatswain arrives carrying a coil for the warehouse, sets it down, turns the desk a quarter round and tests whether the chair can see the entrance without blocking the clerks' path. Then he carries the coil onward. By the time the chief factor comes to inspect his new hand, the room appears to have arranged itself.

At noon the shipboard cup appears beside the ledger, covered and set on his left, with bread and broth but no charge. Daniel understands that Tom has carried one practice ashore and left the rest for the room to learn. Food is never used to summon an answer.

He keeps the ledger exactly. His figures become the cleanest in the house, and the factors praise them without looking at him, and none of them understands what they are watching. The columns are not diligence. The columns are the wall of the last room he owns. A man can hold on to a column when he cannot hold on to anything else, and Daniel holds on for nearly a year: entry by entry, quarter by quarter, hand steady from eight in the morning until the light goes, and shaking the rest of the time, in the privacy the Company's incuriosity mercifully provides.

In the evenings, sometimes, a scarred boatswain sits within sight of him and says nothing.

Daniel never asks him why. The watching appears in no book — he has checked, being what he is; there is no order, no allowance, no entry anywhere attaching that man to this desk — and paper's silence, which once meant menace, has begun in this one case to mean the opposite. Whatever the boatswain is doing, nobody is paying him to do it. In Daniel's ruined arithmetic, that makes it the only account in Surat he can trust.

Twice he puts paper by the lamp to write to Nell.

The first time he writes her name and sits until the candle is half gone and writes nothing under it, because everything true is an injury — *I signed a lie; I watched a pen decide the world; I am not the man who promised you a story* — and everything gentle is a lie, and he of all men cannot make his hand do it. The second time he does not manage the name. How do you tell someone you are not the man they remember? The question folds itself around the pen and takes it, the way the riding turn took the boy's fingers at the capstan, and Daniel puts the paper away and adds, in the privacy of his own head, the cruellest entry in his private book: that the coral room reaches even Rochester, and Nell is under the water too, and does not know it.

The end, when it comes, is one word long, which is fitting, since his whole life has been decided in single words — Amboyna, Batavia, retained, both.

A form arrives from the chief factor's office in the spring of 1626, a routine certification for the season's stores, wanting only his signature under the standing phrase.

*Examined and found sufficient.*

Daniel reads the word examined.

He sets down the pen — places it, exactly, parallel to the page, the way he places everything — and says, to nobody in the room, in a voice the clerks will repeat to each other for a week:

I cannot.

And the scarred boatswain, who is there, who is always somehow there, stands up without a question in him, and goes out, and comes back before evening having done something at the shipping lists, and that is how Daniel learns — not being asked, being told, the only kindness left that his body can accept — that he is going home.

***

## VI. The Eighth Letter

Tom has written to Maggie seven times and sent nothing. The seventh letter lies unsigned in his sea chest, under a parish notice, and on the passage west he does not take it out. He understands at last what the eighth letter is. It is not paper. It walks the deck at his side in a bigger man's coat, flinching at the water the ship is made to cross, and when they reach the river he will not send it — he will hold it upright through a doorway he was told not to darken, and let it say what no page of his ever could.

The passage west takes five months, and Daniel stands at the rail for most of them.

The sea he crosses is the sea that was crossed to kill his friends: the same water, folded and refolded eleven thousand miles, indifferent as ink. He learns, slowly, in fair weather, to stand the sight of it. The boatswain's deck carries him the way it carried him from Batavia — a place kept clear, meals left without comment, no questions, work happening around his stillness with the tact of men who have all, in their different tonnages, been broken somewhere and shipped home once themselves.

England comes up out of the north Atlantic grey as the day he left it, as though the five years were weather that happened to other people. The Channel. The estuary. The river, narrowing, crowding, bristling with masts — more than he remembers, riding deeper than he remembers, the trade he once wanted to be a story in, going on at full flood without him.

On the last morning, coming up with the tide, the boatswain stands beside him at the rail and looks west up the water toward whatever it is that scarred men look toward, and says the only sentence of the whole passage that is not about work.

There is a house on the Wall. We will go there first.

# The Man Who Came Back Wrong (1626)

> **1626**
>
> *“The charges were a formality. The questioning was procedure. The killing was the actual point.”*
>
> — Daniel, factor, Amboyna, 1626

***

## I. The Word from Amboyna

A homeward ship from Surat comes up the river on a grey afternoon, riding low, sails slack, in no hurry at all. Word comes off it before the crew do: an Amboyna man is aboard. The name passes hand to hand along the waterfront, faster than good news ever travels.

*Amboyna.*

London has had official accounts and counter-accounts for two years. The river has had rumours longer. What it has not had is a man who was inside the rooms and came home.

By the time it reaches the tavern by the stairs, the place has changed without anyone quite watching it happen. Longer bar. Flagstones where the rushes used to be. A new sign nobody remembers the old one well enough to miss. The boy who signed his contract here years back, with half his wages promised against his father's debt, sailed south and came home nine years on as somebody else — a Company boatswain the keeper would not serve, shown the door with his mother's thimble at his feet. Fourteen years, now, since he was through it. The bar kept count of him after all. It keeps count of everything.

The ships on the river have multiplied. The Company has factories now — Surat, Masulipatam, Agra — small English footholds on coastlines and river towns that never asked for them, each one a bet that the local ruler stays friendly and the weather stays kind.

Amboyna was one of those bets. It failed three years ago. This afternoon, one of its surviving entries has come upriver.

***

## II. The Man Who Came Back Wrong

The light has gone flat and grey, and the river is low enough to show the mud: dark, streaked, the low-tide smell of brine and rot coming in through the window.

The heavy oak door opens, bringing in the sharp chill of the Thames, and two men come through it.

The first moves with the heavy, rolling balance of a deep-water sailor: forty-five, weathered to old leather, a jagged white scar through one eyebrow, and no boatswain's whistle anywhere about him. Maggie stops wiping the counter. The silence of 1612 stands in the room between them, fourteen years wide, and neither of them puts a word into it. Whatever Tom has come back for, it is not absolution, and he does not ask for any. He is holding the second man up.

The second man walks like a man not sure the ground will hold. Slow. Careful. Thin, and not hunger-thin, or not only that: he has stopped eating, because food has become a problem instead of a comfort. His clothes are English but old, and they hang wrong, cut for a bigger man than the one left inside them.

Tom sets him on the stool nearest the fire — the one that was Barnaby's, then briefly his own, and nobody's for fourteen years — and takes the wall beside it, standing, like a man on watch. Daniel sits. Doesn't order. His hands lie flat on the counter, trembling. His eyes are on the wood but not looking at it. They are looking at a room with no windows, somewhere behind them.

This bar has seen the look before. Men who come back changed in ways you can't quite point to. The tremor. The stillness. An attention that is really its opposite: total withdrawal, into a place nobody else can follow. The sea does this to some men. The tropics do it worse. Amboyna did it worst of all.

Maggie comes over, slower on her feet than she was a generation back, when the scarred man now standing watch by the hearth was a boy at this same bar with a paper in his pocket. No man in the room has found it useful to notice, or would say so if he had. She sets a tankard down without asking. You don't ask a shaking man what he wants. You put ale in front of him.

Drink, she says. Not a suggestion.

Daniel obeys. One pull, then another. The tremor slows. Doesn't stop. Slows is the best this bar can do for him this afternoon.

She pours nothing for Tom. He does not ask. Some accounts stay open even while other business is done across them.

But on the second evening a meat pie arrives in front of him without a word — mutton and mace, the way a boy at this bar liked it once, and the way nobody has asked for it in fourteen years. Tom looks at it for a long moment before he picks up the fork. No ale comes with it, and he does not ask for any. The pie is not forgiveness. It is older than forgiveness, and stubborner: the thing that went on being true the whole time the other thing was true too.

On the third evening, while Daniel sleeps sitting upright by the fire, Maggie asks the question she has kept back from Tom. Jack has crossed her threshold for years. That is not whose account she wants now.

Jack?

Tom knows which Jack. There are names a room has only one of.

Alive. Rigger at Deptford. Wife, little girl, another coming. He was one voyage with me and would not take a second.

Did you keep him alive?

Some days. Other days he kept me alive.

And the account?

She knows it closed. She is asking how.

Cleared.

From his half wages?

Tom looks toward Daniel, though Daniel is not hearing anything in this room.

Not all of them.

Maggie waits.

The morning after you put me out, Jack brought a list of questions to the hiring hall. Written in another man's hand. Asked every one while the clerk tried to talk over him. Surgeon, kit, spoiled stores, replacement fees. Took half the morning and made the men behind him start asking too.

Maggie picks up the rag.

Where did he get such troublesome questions?

Never said.

And the wages?

I put two shillings of mine against Carter's account for every month he served. Called it instruction money in the book so Harcourt would pass it.

Why did you not tell me?

You told me not to come back until I remembered. You did not say to send reports.

Maggie wipes the same place twice. You remembered before today, then.

Some of it.

And stayed away fourteen years.

That too.

She goes back behind the bar. No ale comes back with her. The account between them has not closed. It has, however, received an entry neither of them knew how to make in 1612.

***

## III. The Water

It takes Daniel three days to talk. Three days of sitting, drinking ale he can't taste, flinching at the door. Maggie puts food in front of him. He pushes it round the plate. The other patrons give him room. The river does its business outside the window; the ale stays good; Daniel sits.

On the fourth day Carter comes in. Ships' chandler now in everything: rope, tar, canvas, whatever a vessel needs before it sails — the same Carter who stood in the wall of river-men the night the room sat as a court, twenty-three years heavier in purse since. Big man, big voice, and he has known Daniel long enough to know he isn't just another broken sailor. He pulls up a stool, orders two ales, slides one across.

You're Company. Amboyna.

Not a question. Carter knows because Carter knows everything that moves through the docks.

Daniel doesn't look at him. Drinks some of the ale. They killed them, he says. His voice is flat, worn smooth from saying it too many times already. They killed all of them.

I know, Carter says, quieter than usual. We heard.

They arrested us on false charges. Said we were plotting with the Japanese mercenaries to take the fort. There was no plot. They dragged us out at night. Ten Englishmen. Ten Japanese. Put us in a room and asked questions, and when we didn't answer the way they wanted—

The bar has gone quiet around them. Men who weren't listening are listening now.

Daniel's sentence stays broken where it stopped.

***

## IV. The Room

The room is coral stone and has no window, and it is three years ago, and it is also always.
